History of CCB Status 
This is the CCB Project Validation 
Gold Level Criteria 
We aim to generate exceptional benefits related to climate and biodiversity 
through  the  SMPP,  under  both  the  Verified  Carbon  Standard  (VCS)  and  
Climate, Community, and Biodiversity Alliance (CCBA) standards. 
Climate:  
The SMPP will prevent the emission of an average of 1,338,569 tonnes (t) 
CO
2
-equivalents  (CO
2
-e)  annually through  the  restoration  of  a  degraded  

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION: 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
  
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
3 
peatland area. This will be achieved through the restoration of the project 
area’s hydrology through the construction of dams to block canals that are 
currently draining the project area. Detailed information about the SMPP’s 
climate benefits generated through project activity can be found throughout 
Section 3 (with project activities described in Section 2.1.11).  
Biodiversity: The SMPP will protect critical habitat for 10 endangered and 
critically endangered species, as well as 19 additional vulnerable species. 
The species protected within our project area include the endemic Sumatran 
tiger  (Panthera  tigris  ssp. Sumatrae),  the  Sumatran  Surili  (Presbytis 
melalophos),  and  the  Sambar  dear  (Rusa  unicolor),  as  well  as  other  
important  species  such  as  the  sun  bear  (Helarctos  malayanus),  the  agile  
gibbon (Hylobates agilis), and the rhinoceros hornbill (Buceros rhinoceros). 
The SMPP will increase the protection of the project area, thereby protecting 
the endangered habitat that these species depend on. Detailed information 
about  the  SMPP’s  biodiversity  benefits  generated  through  project  activity  
can  be  found  throughout  Section  5 (with  project  activities  related  to  
biodiversity described in Section 2.1.11.6). 
A summary of these biodiversity and climate benefits is available in Section 
1. 
Expected Verification 
Schedule  
This is a joint validation/verification. The first verification for the SMPP was 
completed concurrently with this validation.

1 SUMMARY OF PROJECT BENEFITS 
This  section  highlights  some  of  this  project’s  important  benefits.  Section  1.1 (Unique  Project  Benefits)  
should be considered in conjunction with project’s conceptual model and is specific to this project. Section 
1.2 (Standardized Benefit Metrics) provides estimates on quantifiable benefit that will be generated over 
the lifetime of the project.  
1.1 Unique Project Benefits 
The Sumatra Merang Peatland Project (SMPP) aims to generate a variety of project benefits throughout 
the lifetime of our project. This section outlines unique project benefits that are not captured in Section 1.2 
below.  
Outcome or Impact Estimated by the End of Project Lifetime 
Section 
Reference
 
1) Fire prevention and fire threat reduction, achieved through: 
• Reduction in human activity within the SMPP area (this is the greatest risk that leads 
to fire) to prevent fires; 
• Satellite-based early warning system (EWS) to detect and mitigate fires as early as 
possible;  
• Forest patrols focused on detecting and identifying hotspots of fire threats, and 
continuously monitoring the fire threats in the SMPP; 
• Fire training for SMPP staff working inside the project area; and  
• Fire training for nearby concessions and SMPP stakeholders. 
Through our project efforts, we aim to achieve a target of zero fires within the project area and 
maximize fire resilience in the SMPP and surrounding landscape. These efforts which will allow 
natural forest ecosystems to recover, provide extended habitats that support key species, and 
limit the negative impacts to health and livelihoods from smoke haze and air pollution. 
2.1.11

2.1.11.5 
 
1
 Land with woody vegetation that meets an internationally accepted definition (e.g., UNFCCC, FAO or IPCC) of what 
constitutes a forest, which includes threshold parameters, such as minimum forest area, tree height and level of crown 
cover, and may include mature, secondary, degraded and wetland forests (VCS Program Definitions) 
2
  Reduced  emissions  from  deforestation  and  forest  degradation  (REDD)  -   Activities  that  reduce  GHG  emissions  by  
slowing or stopping conversion of forests to non-forest land and/or reduce the degradation of forest land where forest 
biomass is lost (VCS Program Definitions) 
3
 Afforestation, reforestation and revegetation (ARR) - Activities that increase carbon stocks in woody biomass (and in 
some  cases  soils)  by  establishing,  increasing  and/or  restoring  vegetative  cover  through  the  planting,  sowing  and/or  
human-assisted natural regeneration of woody vegetation (VCS Program Definitions) 
4
 Improved forest management (IFM) -  Activities that change forest management practices and increase carbon stock 
on forest lands managed for wood products such as saw timber, pulpwood and fuelwood (VCS Program Definitions)

2 GENERAL 
2.1 Project Goals, Design and Long-Term Viability  
2.1.1 Summary Description of the Project (G1.2) 
About 60% of tropical peatland areas are located in the Southeast Asia region, with an estimated area of 
25 million hectares (Mha).
1
 Southeast Asia’s tropical peatlands are particularly carbon-rich, comprising 77% 
of the global tropical peatland carbon poo
l, which is an estimated 68.5 gigatonnes (Gt).
2
 Of the 11 countries 
in Southeast Asia, Indonesia’s 57.4 Gt carbon pool is the largest at 65%, with an area of 15 million hectares 
(Mha) of peatland.
2,3
 Although peatlands have the unique capacity to store carbon in massive quantities,
4
 
these ecosystems are rapidly degrading due to industrial activities and land-use changes such as logging, 
conversion  to  crop  and  palm  oil  plantations,  and  drainage.
3,5
  These  practices  lead  to  organic  matter  
oxidation and render peatlands a globally significant source of damaging greenhouse gas (GHG) emissions. 
Additionally, the impacts of climate change such as El-Niño events have affected an increase in forest fires 
that degrade peatland areas while simultaneously creating costs associated with health and other social 
impacts on communities.
3
 Sumatra in particular has a vast extent of peatlands that comprise 15% of the 
island’s  area.
6
  Throughout  Sumatra  and  Kalimantan  these  ecosystems  represent  an  estimated  30  Gt  of  
carbon, across approximately 13 Mha.
5
 
The  Sumatra  Merang  Peatland  Project  (SMPP)  aims  to  protect  and  restore  22,922  ha  of  a  peatland  
ecosystem  located  in the  Musi  Banyuasin  district  in  South  Sumatra  province.  The  SMPP  sits  within  the  
Merang-Kepayang peat dome, one of the largest carbon rich peat swamp areas in Sumatra, covering more 
than 150,000 ha. This area and the surrounding peat zones are known for high levels of biodiversity and a 
multitude  of  critically  endangered  and  vulnerable  species  including  the  sun  bear  (Helarctos  malayanus), 
the Malay tapir (Tapirus indicus), and the Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris ssp. Sumatrae).  
The Merang-Kepayang peat dome was first subjected to commercial exploitation in the 1980s, primarily for 
the  extraction  of  timber.  The  SMPP  area  was  commercially  logged  throughout  the  1990s  and  saw  the  
beginning  of  the  site’s  degradation  as  canals  were  dug and  trees  felled.  Commercial  timber  operations  
ceased in 2000 after the site’s commercial timber stocks were exhausted, however degradation of the site 
continued as small-scale illegal loggers continued to extract timber from the area for local sawmills through 
an  ever-expanding  network  of  canals.  Around  this  time,  the  expansion  of  commercial  agriculture  in  the  
areas  surrounding  the  SMPP  area  began  to  disrupt  the  overall  hydrology  of  the  landscape  and  gradual  
desiccation of the peat. Fires became a more prevalent feature in the landscape. Despite these drivers of 
land-use  change,  the  SMPP  area  remained  a  site  of  biodiversity  significance,  harboring  numerous  
endangered  or  rare  species.  Large  fires  in  2004,  2006,  and  2015  continued  the  deforestation  trend  and 
further threatened these habitats.  
In the absence of this project, this peatland ecosystem would be at particularly high risk for conversion to 
industrial  agriculture,  namely  fast-growing  Acacia  plantations  grown  for  pulp  and  paper.  This  conversion  
would lead to lowered water table levels and hence elevated GHG emissions due to increased microbial 
decomposition  of  the  peat,  as  well  as  increased  fire  risks.  The  project  prevents  that  scenario  from  
happening  by  securing  an  Izin  Usaha  Pemanfaatan  Penyerapan  Karbon  dan/atau  Penyimpanan  Karbon 
(IUP PAN/RAP or PAN/RAP) carbon storage and sequestration license – the first ever such license to be 
issued in Indonesia – which gives the company legal tenure of the project area and is a business license 
for  the  utilization  of  environmental  services  in  protected  and  production  forest  areas.  In  addition  to  
preventing  the  project  area  from  being  converted  for  industrial  agriculture,  the  project  implements  fire  
prevention,   hydrological   restoration,   forest   rehabilitation,   biodiversity   conservation   and   community   
development interventions.  
The SMPP is implemented by PT Global Alam Lestari (PT GAL), an Indonesian company that secured the 
PAN/RAP license for the project site, and Forest Carbon, a Southeast Asian technical Reducing Emissions 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
20 
from Deforestation and forest Degradation plus carbon stock enhancement (REDD+) consulting and project 
development firm.  
Project Objectives 
The SMPP aims to restore the ecological functions of the Merang peatland through the use of sustainable 
land-use models that benefit critically endangered biodiversity and provide benefits for local communities. 
These overarching objectives will be achieved by implementing ecosystem and habitat restoration activities 
while  establishing  sustainable  avenues  for  economic  success  amongst  the  local  communities.  Specific  
objectives focused on climate, biodiversity, and community goals are outlined below: 
Emphasizing climate action: 
• Credibly avoid peatland emissions through the protection and rehabilitation of the project site. 
• Restore  the  variety,  integrity,  and  extent  of  forest  types  to  their  original  pre-2007  fire  state  to  
rejuvenate the presence of valuable ecosystem services and peatland habitats. 
• Restore the hydrological integrity of the peatlands. 
• Prevent  fires  and  ensure  the  safety  of  local  people  through  forest  fire  training,  monitoring,  and  
prevention procedures. 
Supporting communities: 
• Introduce economic opportunities and sustainable livelihood opportunities, focused on community-
directed  investments  with both  immediate,  short-term,  and  long-term  strategies  in  the  SMPP’s  
neighboring communities.  
• Build stronger communities. 
• Ensure  continued  access  and  sustainable  use  of  natural  resources  by  communities  and  local  
stakeholders. 
Protecting biodiversity: 
• Increase  populations  of  critically  endangered  flora  and  fauna  by  eliminating  the  causes  of  
deforestation and degradation in the project site. 
• Establish ecological integrity with long-term ecosystem restoration. 
Annual average and total GHG emission reductions and removals from the SMPP are 1,432,105 tonnes (t) 
CO
2
-equivalents (CO
2
-e) and 125,869 t CO
2
, respectively, totaling 62,912,755 t of Verified Carbon Units 
(VCUs).

Sumatra, Republic of Indonesia (see Map 1). The Musi Banyuasin regency is located in the northern part 
of South Sumatra province and is between 103-104° 45' east (E) and 1.3° - 4° south (S), 14,265.96 square

Environmental conditions of the SMPP area including topography, soil, climate, hydrology, vegetation types 
are described below. 
2.1.5.1 Topography 
The SMPP falls within a topographically uniform and flat landscape. The majority of the project area sits 
within an altitude of 20-30 meters above sea level (m.a.s.l.) (see Map 3). The highest altitude is towards 
the center of the SMPP area (30-40 m above sea level) while the lowest areas are found in the western 
part  of  the  concession  near  the  Buring  river  as  well  as  the  eastern  part  near  the  Kepayang  river  (0-10 
m.a.s.l.). All areas in the SMPP concession area have a slope class of 0-8%, which indicates flat terrain 
(see Map 4).

2.1.5.2 Soil 
Peat soils are created from the accumulation of organic materials, such as the residue of plants, that have 
composted at a slow rate over a long period of time.
7
 Based on the Food and Agriculture Organization of 
the United Nations (FAO) soil classification system, peat soils are called Histosol soils.
7,8
 There are three 
levels of peat maturity, fibric (immature), hemic (medium maturity), and sapric (mature) peat. Peat soils are 
generally inundated all year long, unless manually altered and drained due to canal construction to develop 
industrial forest plantations or as an irrigation system (see Map 5). Naturally, peat soils are located on the 
top-most layer with mineral or alluvial soils underneath.
7
 A peat dome stores water and releases it back into 
the  watershed  at  a  slow  rate.  Hence,  during  the  dry  season,  a  high  volume  of  underground  water  is  
discharged at a slow rate, ensuring peat soils stay inundated until the next wet season.
8
  
  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
26 
Map 5. Soil types in and around the SMPP.  
 
2.1.5.3 Climate 
The  SMPP  area  experiences  a  tropical  monsoonal  climate,  with  two  distinct  rainy  and  dry  seasons.  For  
most of the year, there is significant rainfall in the study area (see 
Table 1).  There  is  only  a  short  dry  season  starting  from  June,  which  lasts  through  September.  Average  
annual rainfall is 2,179 millimeters (mm). The lowest average rainfall is in August (peak of the dry season), 
while the highest average precipitation falls in January (peak of the wet season). The annual temperature 
averages around 26.8°C. The average highest temperature is in October (27.8°C), while the lowest average 
temperature occurs in January (26.0°C).

2.1.5.4 Hydrology 
As  mentioned  in  Section  2.1.1,  the  project  area  is  part  of  the  Merang-Kepayang  Peat  Hydrological  Unit  
(Kesatuan Hidrologi Gambut or KHG). Geographically, the SMPP is situated between two main rivers, the 
Merang in the west and the Kepayang in the east, both of which are tributaries of the more prominent Lalan 
river. Other tributaries and canals are present, as well as the Tembesu Daro canal and the Buring river that 
cuts  across  the  northwestern  part  of  the  project  boundary.  This  area  experiences  a  tropical  monsoonal  
climate  with  wet  and  dry  seasons.  The  hydrology  profile  of  the  Merang  peat  dome  makes  this  area  
particularly vulnerable to desiccation from canal construction, especially during the dry season, where lower 
water levels are exacerbated. The average height difference from the top of the peat dome is more than 5 
meters above most rivers. During the rainy season, the majority of the project area experiences a period of 
inundation, followed by drainage and drying out. Based on field visits, it is clear that drainage is the main 
cause of peat drying in the concession. 
In  the  Merang-Kepayang  Hydrological  Unit,  canals  are  manmade  and  were  created  for  transporting  
harvested timber out of the Merang-Kepayang forest area. In early 1990, the Tembesu Daro river was only 
600 m long but illegal loggers extended the scale of this small river.
8
 By 2009, the river was already more 
than 14 kilometers (km) cutting through the deep peat dome of the SMPP area.
8
 
The SMPP site experienced a series of major fire events in 2015 when a significant portion of the site was 
burned, causing notable uneven patterns in the vegetation moisture content and plant-water stress level. 
After three years of post-fire recovery, this degraded landscape is still facing challenges to foster vegetation 
regrowth, considering previous peatland drainage from the canal network.

2.1.5.5 Vegetation Types 
Through an extensive analysis, our land cover classification map shows that 95.4%, 1.9%, 1.3% and 1.4% 
of the SMPP site are 1) Open canopy tree/shrub/herbaceous mix, 2) Close canopy tree/shrub/herbaceous 
mix, 3) Dense forest, and 4) Water/swamp mix. The land cover classification for the SMPP project area is 
available in Map 7.

The  final  classification  for  forest/non-forest  classes  showed  that  1.3%  and  98.7%  of  the  SMPP  site  
contained forest and non-forest areas respectively. This merged classification achieved 99.1% of overall 
accuracy.  
Each of the four land cover classes are described in detail below: 
2.1.5.5.1 Open canopy tree/shrub/herb mix 
The  open  canopy  tree/shrub/herb  mix  is  dominated  by  understory  dense  ferns  and  short  shrubs.  While  
Melaleuca cajuputi (gelam) occurs scattered in low densities throughout the SMPP area and only dominates 
the vegetation in the central area along the Tembesu Daro canal, that has burnt at least four times since 
2000,  covers  approximately  1,200  ha.  In  this  area,  Melaleuca  cajuputi  trees  4-5  m  tall  (dbh  is  4-5 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
30 
centimeters (cm)) form a relatively dense cover (up to 50-70% locally) and totally dominate the tree stratum, 
although  other  species  (notably  Cratoxylum  arborescens/geronggang, Melicope  lunu-ankenda/sepongol 
and Alstonia  pneumatophora/pulai)  also  occur.  Compared  to  other  secondary  peat  swamp  vegetation at 
the site, it is the least diverse (see Figure 2). Sedges (Scleria sumatrana) and ferns (Blechnum indicum and 
Stenochlaena  palustris)  form  a  dense  ground  cover,  interspersed  with  an  occasional  climber  (Artabotrys 
suaveolens, Uncaria lanosa) or small shrub (Melastoma malabathricum).  
2.1.5.5.2 Closed canopy tree/shrub/herb mix 
An  average  of  13  plant  species  were  found  at  each  location  during  a  field  survey  (April  24
th
-26
th
,  2018),  
ranging from only 8 species at the Melaleuca cajuputi (gelam) dominated vegetation (site 4; see D below) 
to 27 species along the Buring river in the far northwest of the location. A total of 42 plant species were 
recorded  on  site,  including  at  least  three  exotics  (Acacia  crassicarpa,  Ludwigia  hyssopifolia,  & Mikania 
cordata).  Trees  are  found  at  all  locations  and  secondary  vegetation  with  ferns/sedges  only  was  not  
encountered  (see  Figure 2).  Most  trees  found  are  pioneer  species  (Alstonia  pneumatophora  (pulai), 
Archidendron clypearia, Cratoxylum arborescens (geronggang), Elaeocarpus petiolatus, Fagraea fragrans 
(tembesu),  Leea  indica  (mamali),  Macaranga  pruinose (mahang), Melaleuca  cajuputi  (gelam), Melicope 
glabra (bangun-bangun), Melicope lunu-ankenda (sepongol) and Trema orientalis) although some are also 
characteristic of mixed, primary peat swamp forest trees (e.g. Alstonia pneumatophora (pulai))). Along the 
Buring river (Figure 2) the number of trees remaining from prior to the 2015 fires is much higher than other 
areas, except in a zone around the ‘hutan sisa’ in the southeastern part of the SMPP area. From the ground, 
tree  species  that  could  be  seen  within  several  hundred  meters  include  Alstonia  pneumatophora  (pulai), 
Campnosperma coriaceum (terentang), Dialium platysepalum (keranji), Diospyros sp. (arang-arang), Dyera 
polyphylla (jelutung), Koompassia malaccensis (menggeris), Quassia borneensis (pahit-pahit), Parastemon 
urophyllus (mariawan) and Shorea uliginosa (meranti kelungkang).  
2.1.5.5.3 Dense forest 
Dense forest at the SMPP (of which a 232 ha remnant remains: the ‘hutan sisa’) is characterized by the 
presence of a wide range of tree species (>200), a closed canopy (with a height of ±20-25 m) and emergent 
species that tower above the closed canopy to heights of 35-45m (Figure 2
). Closed canopy tree species 
include Campnosperma coriaceum (terentang), Carallia brachiata (semeragi), Dacryodes costata (siuai), 
Durio  carinatus  (durian  burung),  Ganua  motleyana  (ketiau/nyatoh), Horsfieldia  glabra  (darah  kero),  
Mezzetium  parviflora  (makai  hitam),  Myrtistica  iners  (biawak), Palaquium  burckii  (balam), Polyalthia 
sumatrana (makai  putih),  Sindora  bruggemanii  (kayu  kapas),  Syzygium  acuminatissimum (balam  cabe),  
Syzygium clavimyrtus (balam sisil), Xylopia altissima (jangkang) and Vatica ressak (rasak).  Emergent tree 
species   include   Alstonia   pneumatophora   (pulai), Dyera   polyphylla   (jelutung   rawa),   Koompassia 
malaccensis (kempas), Shorea  teysmanniana (meranti  merawan),  Shorea  uliginosa  (meranti  kelungkan)  
and Tetramerista  glabra  (punak).  The  understory  includes  various  palms  such  as  Cyrtostachys  renda  
(sealing  wax  palm),  Eleiodoxa  conferta  (asam  payau),  Licuala  paludosa  (fan  palm  or  palas)  and  various  
rattan species (e.g. Korthalsia sp., the fish-tail rattan) and the tall pandan Pandanus atrocarpus, several 
aroids  including  Aglaeonema  nitidum  and Alocasia  longiloba,  a  sparse  scattering  of  ferns  and  several  
scattered sedges (including Mapania bancanum). The ‘hutan sisa’ at SMPP has been selectively logged at 
the edges, but to a large extent it is both floristically and structurally indistinguishable from primary mixed 
peat swamp forest.

2.1.5.5.4 Water/swamp mix 
Apart from a few small peat swamp forest streams, open water formerly did not occur in the SMPP area, 
so this habitat is largely new, which is also reflected by the paucity of aquatic species in the open waters. 
One would normally expect blackwater streams in open areas (i.e. without dense tree cover) to be lined 
with Pandanus helicopus (rasau) and Hanguana malayana (bakung), which is the case along rivers in the 
vicinity, such as along the Sungai Merang. However, Hanguana malayana seems absent in much of the 
SMPP area (except a small amount along the Buring river), and although present, Pandanus helicopus is 
scarce but  can  be  expected  to  expand  along  many  of  the  streams  in  the  coming  years.  Bladderwort  
Utricularia aurea is the only species characteristic of open blackwater that is fairly common throughout the 
area (Figure 2).   
2.1.6 Social Parameters (G1.3) 
2.1.6.1 Communities and other stakeholders 
The SMPP is in an isolated area with comparatively little interaction with local people. However, there are 
still  some  important  groups  related  to  the  project  location  and  activities,  and  these  groups  have  been  
defined in this section. In the Climate, Community & Biodiversity Standards, Third Edition, a “community” 
refers to any group of people that derive any income, livelihood, cultural values, or other contributions to 
well-being from within the project area. Using this definition from the CCB standards, there are a total of 21 
traditional fishermen and two small-scale palm oil farmers that rely on the SMPP site in some way, thus 
resulting in a fishing community and a farming community.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
33 
Fishing  activities  are  spread  across  three  main  areas,  including  those  used  by  four  fishermen  along  the  
Kepayang river, four fishermen along the Buring river, and the remaining along the Tembesu Daru canal. 
Proximity  of  large  industrial  activities  among  other  factors,  has  resulted  in  the  Lalan  river  becoming  too  
polluted to fish safely. Fishing communities therefore rely on the cleaner waters in and around the SMPP 
for safe and productive fishing efforts, despite the considerable distance away from their homes. 
“Other stakeholders” are any groups other than communities that have the potential to be affected by project 
activities, whether or not they live in or outside of the project area. For the SMPP we define the residents 
of  nearby  settlements  and  villages,  including  those  primarily  from  the  formally  recognized  Muara  Muang 
and Kepayang villages, as well as other unofficial settlements along the rivers, as other stakeholders (see 
Table 3 in Section 2.1.9 for further description).  
There  are  no  individuals  living  within  the  SMPP  area.  These  other  stakeholders  are  somewhat  removed 
from  the  project  site,  at  a  significant  distance  from  the  SMPP  by  boat,  car,  or  motorbike  (Map 8).  For  
example, Kepayang village is located about 15 km from the SMPP’s main camp, which is about a 45-minute 
to 1.5-hour motorbike or car ride. On a small motorized canoe (often used by local fishermen), it can take 
roughly  two  hours.  However  due  to  their  relative  proximity,  they  are  potential  beneficiaries  of  project  
community  development  activities  (see  Section  4.4.1  for  further  details  of  our  involvement  with  these  
stakeholders). Table 3 provides a detailed description of these areas. 
2.1.6.2 Local ethnicities 
All  of  the  people  living  in  this  region,  the  fishermen  and  farmer  communities,  as  well  as  the  other  
stakeholders,  have  diverse  ethnic  backgrounds.  There  are  currently  no  longer  any  self-described 
indigenous people living in the area. The current inhabitants of this region have mostly migrated from other 
parts  of  Indonesia  and  have  lived  in  this  region  for  a  long  time,  dating  back  to  the  founding  of  these  
settlements and villages. Many people have come from other areas in Sumatra, primarily from further north 
of the SMPP area, including from Jambi province and further away from Medan in North Sumatra, as well 
as ethnic groups of Javanese and Melayu origin. Additionally, in Kepayang village there are some residents 
originally from Bugis and Kalimantan. 
2.1.6.3 Land-uses and economic well-being 
Community fishermen groups interacting with the SMPP are comprised primarily of retired older men, many 
of  whom  may  not  be  able  to  complete  other  jobs  or  do  not  have  other  stable  income  sources.  Of  these  
fishermen, nine individuals rely solely on this fishing activity as their livelihood and harvest fish full time. 
The other twelve individuals conduct their fishing activities on a seasonal basis and rely on other jobs, using 
fishing to supplement their income and diversify their food sources. Both categories of fishermen consider 
these fishing activities within the SMPP and neighboring areas a crucial aspect of their livelihoods to support 
their families. The farmers that cultivate land along the southeastern border of the SMPP are usually of a 
lower economic bracket compared to others within the nearby villages, and as there are many opportunities 
for  other  more  formal  jobs  in  the  wider  area,  farming  is  much  less  common.  Specifically,  these  farming  
activities focus primarily on small scale rubber and palm oil production. Further details of the fishermen and 
farmer communities are described in more detail in Section 2.1.9.  
Through discussion with local fishermen, we have estimated the economic value of fishing livelihoods in 
the SMPP to range from 47 USD profit per month per adult up to 175 USD. We have also learned that more 
elderly fisher groups catch less than their younger counterparts. 
None of the identified community members permanently reside within the SMPP, but instead live among 
the other stakeholders in the closest villages and settlements (however it is important to note, as described 
in    Table 3 in Section 2.1.9, that these villages and settlements are still a significant distance away from the 
SMPP).  
Other  stakeholders  find  livelihoods  through  more  formal  employment  in  areas  throughout  the  Musi  
Banyuasin regency, and very few were identified as deriving livelihoods from within the project area. There 
are a variety of economic opportunities available in areas outside of the SMPP, which include opportunities 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
34 
with nearby companies in the mining, oil and gas, industrial agriculture, paper pulp, and palm oil production 
sectors, and to a lesser degree, farming including cultivation of rubber, cassava, and pineapple crops.  
The relatively stable and varied access to livelihood opportunities results in communities that have access 
to resources, a steadily declining poverty line and strong community infrastructure. Most people live in well-
built sturdy homes with sanitation facilities, and many drive cars and other transportation vehicles. In 2018, 
9.8% of the population of Indonesia lived below the national poverty line.
10
 In Musi Banyuasin regency, the 
percentage  of  the  population  that  is    impoverished  has  been  declining  over  the  years  with  a  drop  from  
18.99% in 2011 to 16.75% in 2017.
11
 In the province of South Sumatra, the poverty level is defined from a 
monthly  household  expenditures  metric  that  is  set  at  less  than  or  equal  to  IDR  400,000.  Additionally,  in  
2017 the Human Development Index (HDI) of the Musi Banyuasin regency was 66.96. Based on a social 
appraisal  of  the  nearby  villages,  the  average  monthly  income  per  household  in  the  Muara  Merang  and  
Kepayang villages is 2-3 million IDR, or 24-36 million IDR per year (estimated through discussions in these 
villages). This value equates to around $4.61- $6.91 USD per day, which is also well above the international 
poverty line of $1.90 USD per day according to the World Bank. 
2.1.6.4 Public health 
The areas near the SMPP where identified community members and other stakeholders live have existing 
public health infrastructure which meets limited basic health needs and support to pregnant mothers. The 
Muara Merang and Kepayang villages have a variety of healthcare facilities available, consisting of a health 
branch  clinic  (Puskesmas  Pembantu or Pustu),  a  village  health  center  (Pusat Kesehatan  Desa or 
Puskesdes), and a village maternity clinic (Pondok Bersalin Desa or Polindes). There are health officers in 
these areas but no full-time doctors. There are also health workers from the neighboring companies that 
regularly  visit  the  villages  and  settlements.  In  Muara  Merang  and  Kepayang  there  is  access  to  both  
professional  and  traditional  midwives  that  support  the  women  living  in  these  areas.  Residents  of  Tebing  
Harapan,  a  hamlet  of  Kepayang,  also  have  access  to  health  facilities  that  are  owned and operated by a 
neighboring  palm  oil  plantation.  There  is  also  access  to  medicines  and  vaccines  facilitated  in  part  by  
Indonesia’s national healthcare system. While there are no large hospitals in proximity, this existing network 
of  basic  healthcare options  supports  the  families  living  in  these  areas,  and  if  more  serious  treatment  is  
required, individuals can travel to the larger hospitals in Palembang and Bayung Lencir.  
The quality of river water is poor and often unsafe for use in these settled areas. However, most families 
have proper toilet facilities. Wells or the rivers are used for washing, bathing, and toilet use if proper toilets 
are  not  available.  For  drinking  and  cooking,  residents  use  collected  rainwater,  but  also  buy  wholesale  
bottled water, sourced from treated rainwater or containers brought from Palembang.  
2.1.6.5 Education 
The  areas  near  the  SMPP  have  a  variety  of  opportunities  for  formal  education.  This  widespread  
infrastructure includes four elementary schools (Sekolah Dasar or SD) in Muara Merang and Kepayang, a 
junior high school (Sekolah Menengah Pertama or SMP) in Muara Merang, a learning park in Kepayang, 
and two additional learning centers (Pusat Kegiatan Belajar Masyarakat or PKBM). As many teachers come 
from  within  Muara  Merang,  teacher  attendance  is  adequate.  More  than  50%  of  children  who  finish  SD  
typically continue their education with SMP. The closest high schools (Sekolah Menengah Atas or SMA) 
are in Bayung Lencir. Some residents also go on to attend universities, often in Palembang or Jogjakarta. 
In addition, residents of Tebing Harapan are provided elementary school facilities by the adjacent palm oil 
plantation company. There are also after school programs that keep children in a learning environment for 
extended  hours  and  provide  time  for  parents  to  continue  working  to  further  their  income  from  livelihood  
activities.  As  part  of  SMPP  community  development,  we  will  work  with  SMPP  stakeholders  to  further  
improve learning opportunities.

2.1.7 Project Zone Map (G1.4-7, G1.13, CM1.2, B1.2) 
Map 8. The SMPP project zone. 
 
 
The SMPP project zone consists of the formal project area, the government delineated peatland 
hydrology unit (PHU), and the village areas where community development plans are taking place. The 
project zone includes active fishing grounds inside and near the boundaries of the SMPP and the two 
small farms located on the southeast border of the SMPP. The project zone will cover all settlement areas 
associated with the villages of Kepayang and Muara Merang as these villages will be beneficiaries of 
community development activities. From an ecological perspective, the PHU represents peatland areas 
that will benefit from improved functionality of the PHU. Within the PHU, there are commercial forest 
plantations, palm oil, plantations, and to a limited degree small holder farms. The project zone is a total of 
85,182 hectares. 
 
The SMPP project area is defined by the PAN/RAP license and covers a total area of 22,934 ha minus 12 
ha of land that have been excised which cover the farms of the two families that continue to conduct small-
scale palm oil production. 
The SMPP sits within the Merang-Kepayang peat dome, the largest peat swamp dome in South Sumatra, 
which covers more than 150,000 ha. A number of critical climate and biodiversity assets are linked to the 
wider peat  swamp  forest  zone  that  surrounds  the  SMPP:  the  Sembilang-Berbak  National  Park  to  the  
northeast and the Muaro Jambi peat swamp to the north.

2.1.8 Stakeholder Identification (G1.5) 
Stakeholder  identification  for  the  SMPP  applies  to  our  project’s  communities  and  other  stakeholders.  As  
indicated in Section 2.1.6 and further elaborated in Section 2.1.9 below, our communities are defined as 
the  fishermen  and  farmer  groups  that  derive  their  livelihoods  within  the  SMPP  area,  and  the  other  
stakeholders  are  the  people  that  inhabit  settlements  that  are  located  outside  of  the  SMPP  area  at  fairly  
considerable distance. 
To identify and define the community members and other stakeholders within the SMPP, we employed a 
range of methods and strategies. First, we began identifying the groups relevant to the SMPP through an 
assessment  of  previous  socio-economic  assessments  of  the  project  region,  combined  with  knowledge  
provided by our local partners, and through an extensive literature review of previous studies in the SMPP 
area.  Our  approach  then  transitioned  into  direct  engagement  in  the  field,  to  communicate  directly  with  a  
wide variety of the people in the area we had identified. We have conducted this direct engagement since 
the project start date in January 2016 (see Section 2.1.14).   
For this field-based  effort,  we  are  implementing  Participatory  Rural  Appraisal  (PRA) methods to conduct 
social  surveys  in  rural  settings  in  an  efficient  and  participatory  manner  over  time.  This  is  an  extensive  
ongoing  effort,  that  was  started  in  January  2016,  and  is  ongoing.  This  PRA  consists  of  key  individual  
interviews in a one-on-one setting, group interviews, formal meetings, participatory mapping, observations 
throughout  the  site  over  time,  and  focus  group  discussions  (FGDs).  To  ensure  participation  with  our  
community  members  and  other  stakeholders,  we  have  established  long-term  positive  relationships  with  
these local groups, to maintain direct lines of communication between stakeholders and the project. 
We  have  documented  at  least  22  engagements  with  community  fishermen,  one  engagement  with  a  
community  farmer,  50  engagements  with  school  groups  or  other  education  facilitators,  11  engagements  
with village officials, 9 engagements with managers from nearby concessions, 3 engagements with local 
women’s’ groups, and 45 engagements with village residents in the Muara Merang and Kepayang villages 
and other nearby settlements. Further details of these stakeholder consultations are available in Section 
2.3.7 below.  
This extended PRA process, combined with our consistent and direct communication with local people, has 
ensured that we have a strong understanding of the relationship that people in the project zone have with 
the SMPP area or are impacted by the project. 
2.1.9 Stakeholder Descriptions (G1.6, G1.13) 
As per the CCB standards, our communities are defined as the groups of fishermen and farmers working 
in the SMPP area, a total of 21 traditional fishermen and a group of two small-scale palm oil and rubber 
farmers. There are no residents within the SMPP area, and therefore our other stakeholders are the 
largest group of involved local people as they live closer to our site than anyone else, even though they 
do not derive their livelihoods from within the SMPP. 
 
As  described  above  in  Section 2.1.6,  the  project  defines  other  stakeholders  as  the  approximately  6,000  
residents of nearby settlements as they have the most identified and addressable needs as beneficiaries 
of  project  community  development  work.  These  other  stakeholders  are  primarily  from  the  formally  
recognized Muara Muang and Kepayang villages, as well as other unofficial settlements along the Lalan 
and Kepayang rivers. In these areas there is low population density with most people inhabiting the Muara 
Muang  and  Kepayang  villages,  as  well  as  several  sub-villages  (hamlets)  and  many  informal  settlements  
throughout  the  region.  Approximately  3,316  people  comprising  851  households  reside  in  Muara  Muang,  
and 2,436 people in 789 households reside in Kepayang as of a 2017 site visit (information was provided 
to our team by local village officials in the field visits).
12
 It is important to emphasize that these stakeholders 
are still fairly removed from the project site, at a significant distance from the SMPP, as described in Section 
2.1.6.

Community fishermen groups that operate within the SMPP are comprised primarily of retired older men, 
many of whom may not be able to complete other jobs or do not have any other stable income. They do 
not reside within the SMPP but live in neighboring settlements and enter and exit the SMPP concession to 
conduct their fishing activities. Of these fishermen, nine individuals rely solely on fishing for their livelihood, 
and harvest fish full time. The other twelve individuals conduct their fishing activities on a seasonal basis, 
also relying on other jobs but use fishing to supplement their income and diversify their food sources. Both 
categories of fishermen consider these fishing activities within the SMPP a crucial aspect of their livelihoods 
to  support  their  families.  The  total  of  21  community  fishermen  range  in  age  from  39  to  69,  and  live  in

the  community  fishermen  and  the  two  farmers,  and  this  confidential  information  can  be  supplied  to  the  
auditor upon request. 
The farmers that grow crops along the SMPP border are of a lower economic bracket compared to others 
within the nearby villages, and as there are stronger employment in the wider area where farming is much 
less common. Specifically, these farming activities are focused on small farms of rubber and palm oil. In 
the SMPP, two farmers occupy 8 and 7 ha of farmland, respectively, with 5.9 ha and 5.6 ha located within 
the  SMPP  license  area.  These  two  farms  in  the  SMPP  are  identified  in  Section  4.1.3 in  Map 33.  These 
fishermen primarily use tradition fishing methods that use wire or wood and bamboo traps, as well as fishing 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
38 
poles and lines (tajur). Fish are either consumed for subsistence living needs or sold as fresh catch or as 
dried and salted. The fishermen usually sell their catches in the weekly markets of the nearby settlements 
and villages. Fishing activities in the SMPP already rely on a diverse variety of species, and it is likely as 
the project develops, a higher abundance and diversity of fish species will be present in the project area. 
 
The  other  stakeholders  in  nearby  settlements  find  livelihoods  primarily  through  formal  jobs  working for 
nearby  companies  with  mining,  oil  and  gas,  industrial  agriculture,  paper  pulp,  and  palm  oil  production  
activities, and to a lesser degree, farming including cultivation of rubber, cassava, and pineapple crops. 
2.1.10   Sectoral Scope and Project Type 
The SMPP is an Agriculture, Forestry, and Other Land-Use (AFOLU) project. More specifically, the project 
activities  are  categorized  as  a  combination  of  Afforestation,  Reforestation,  and  Revegetation  (ARR)  and  
Wetlands Restoration and Conservation (WRC). Under the WRC component, the project activities relate to 
Restoring Wetland Ecosystems (RWE). The SMPP is not a grouped project.  
2.1.11   Project Activities and Theory of Change (G1.8) 
The SMPP aims to restore a large peatland ecosystem that is currently in a predominantly degraded state 
due  to  the  historical  impact  of  logging,  canal  construction,  and  fires.  The  project  activities  have  been  
designed to maximize the ability of the project to restore the ecological functions of the project area, and 
the  wider  peat  dome  in  which  it  exists,  while  building  strong  and  supportive  relationships  with  the  broad  
range of stakeholders within the landscape. The SMPP is structured with the conceptual model illustrated 
in    Figure 3 describing in more detail the direct project interventions and how they help to meet our project 
objectives.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
39 
 
Figure 3. Conceptual model of the SMPP.  
Our project activities cover seven core areas and are describe in detail below:  
2.1.11.1   Forest patrolling 
Patrolling activities aim to reduce or eliminate risks to the project site that cause ecosystem degradation, 
including illegal logging, poaching, exploitative fishing, and further conversion for small-scale agriculture. 
In addition to the potential removal of flora and fauna from the site, these activities create heightened fire 
risks and keep canals open and active.  
Patrolling activities include: 
• Physical demarcation of the project area;  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
40 
• Stakeholder engagement to educate on the project area boundary and permissible activities within 
and in the near vicinity of the project area; 
• Establishment  of  a  series  of  guard  posts  around  the  perimeter  of  the  project  area,  strategically  
located at known key access points (see Map 9);   
• Establishment of a radio communication system for effective monitoring and reporting on potential 
risks (including fire – see below); 
• Regular foot, bicycle, boat and vehicle patrolling along the perimeter and within the project area. 
Patrol teams consist primarily of community members from the two nearby villages. 
• Data collection and analysis on identified threats (i.e. evidence of illegal logging, encounters with 
poachers or animal traps, illegal entries to fish, etc.) to prioritize patrolling activities; 
• Regular drone monitoring across the project area provides additional information on potential risks 
in harder to access areas of the project site; and 
• Engagement with forest police and local law enforcement to prevent forest crimes.  
Limiting  illegal  incursions  not  only  prevents  the  extraction  of  valuable  flora  and  fauna,  but  also  greatly  
reduces  the  risk  of  potential  fire  vectors  that  are  known  to  have  major  negative  impacts  on  peatland  
ecosystem restoration from entering the project area.  
2.1.11.2   Fire prevention and monitoring 
Historically, fire has had the largest impact on the integrity and health of the SMPP area. Fires can either 
initiate within the project area or spread from neighboring concessions, which occurred in 2015. Fires pose 
an especially dangerous risk to peatlands, typically during dry spells (i.e. in an El Niño year) and if water 
levels have been artificially lowered from canal construction. Peatland fires can spread quickly and burn for 
long periods of time underground. Therefore, maintaining fire preparedness and dispatching rapid response 
teams are critical means of avoiding fires and extinguishing them as quickly as possible.  
Fire prevention and monitoring activities include: 
• Patrol activities to prevent the access of fire vectors into the project area (see activity 1 above). 
• Establishment of an early warning system (EWS) to rapidly inform the project management team 
of   potential   fires.   The   EWS   combines   satellite   data   alerts   (Moderate-Resolution   Imaging   
Spectroradiometer or MODIS hotspots), patrol team communications, and drone monitoring to alert 
senior technical project staff of potential risks. Established protocols are subsequently followed by 
field teams to investigate and respond as necessary. 
• Establishment of Fire Response and Suppression (FRS) teams. These are the field teams based 
at the project site who can respond in case of a fire alert. All members of the field teams, including 
forest   patrol   team   members,   biodiversity   staff   and   the   project   site   manager,   have   been   
professionally  trained  in  peatland  firefighting  by  the  fire  brigade  of  the  Indonesian  Ministry  of  
Forestry. The training documents a Standard Operation Procedure (SOP) for handling fire, outlining 
communication  logistics  to  inform  the  all  key  stakeholders  in  surrounding  concessions  and  
communities. SMPP teams are also fully equipped with the necessary firefighting equipment, such 
as personal protective clothing, pumps, hoses, and other safety gear. 
• Establishment  of  cooperation  agreements  with  neighboring  concessions.  In  most  cases  fires  
originate outside of the project area, due to the higher intensity of agricultural activities and human 
presence in those areas. The SMPP’s EWS extends far beyond the boundaries of the project area 
to  monitor  and  report  fire  presence  in  nearby  concessions.  Additionally,  the  FRS  teams  are  
available  to  assist  nearby  concessions,  and  have  extinguished  several  fires  successfully,  in  
concessions that have less-developed response protocols.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
41 
• Stakeholder engagement to train local individuals in the surrounding residents on best practices for 
avoiding and preventing fires, and information on how to signal help when fires arise.   
Fire  prevention  and  monitoring  activities  contribute  to  the  project  objectives  by  greatly  limiting  GHG  
emissions due to peat fires, allowing natural forest ecosystems to recover and provide extended habitats 
for key biodiversity species, and limit the negative health and livelihood impacts of excessive haze.   
2.1.11.3   Canal blocking and hydrology management 
The presence of canals in the project site has significantly contributed to desiccation of the peatland. The 
extensive peatland drainage in surrounding areas due to logging and commodities production has further 
exacerbated  drying  throughout  the  landscape.  Restoring  the  hydrological  integrity  of  the  project  area  is  
therefore  of  critical  importance  to  its  long-term  recovery,  as  well  as  preventing  ongoing  GHG  emissions  
from peat microbial decomposition and peatland fires.  
This includes the rewetting of partially drained peatlands in and around areas with existing canals, and the 
conservation  of  undrained  and  partially  drained  peatlands  (CUPP)  in  those  areas  further  away  from  the  
existing  canal  network.  Existing  canals  are  principally  small  logging  canals  (shallower  than  1  meter  and  
narrower than 2 meters), with only a few medium sized canals (wider than 2 meters) present.  
Canal blocking activities include: 
• Conducting  a  technical  assessment  on  the  most  feasible  canal  block  strategy  taking  into  
consideration access needs, hydrological flows, priority restoration areas, etc. Careful attention will 
be placed on the need to avoid ecological leakage. 
• Development of a proposed canal block plan (see Map 9). 
• Installation of different canal blocks types depending on canal size, water flows, and topographical 
gradients.  Canal  blocks  will  be  constructed  as  a  cascade  to  gradually  raise  water  levels  across  
drained areas and limit the pressure exerted on any one canal block from water flows. Canal blocks 
will  be  principally  built  using  natural  materials  available  on-site  (lying  deadwood  and  peat).  
Compaction dams will likely be required in areas towards the edge of the project area boundary to 
prevent water outflow.  
• Ongoing  monitoring  of  water  table  levels  and  canal  block  conditions.  In  combination  with  an  
iteratively refined hydrological model, ongoing monitoring data will allow for continued optimization 
of  water  table  levels  throughout  the  project  area.  Regular  checks  on  canal  block  conditions  will  
ensure maintenance can be administered quickly should repair be required.  
• Collaboration  with  neighboring  concessions  on  hydrological  management.  The  SMPP  has  and  
continues  to  actively  engage  with  neighboring  concessions  to  promote  improved  hydrological  
management, both for the SMPP area and the neighboring concessions. The SMPP has already 
successfully  lobbied  the  nearby  PT  WHLM  concession  to  install  compaction  canal  blocks  in  the 
large canal that forms the southern boundary of the SMPP area. 
The canal blocking activities are essential in supporting the SMPP area to return to its natural ecological 
condition.  Raised  water  table  levels  reduce  the  risk  of  fire,  prevent  microbial  decomposition  and  peat  
subsidence, and provide the necessary hydrological conditions to allow for the successive regeneration of 
forests.  
  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
42 
Map 9. Potential locations and types of canal blocks within the SMPP area. 
 
2.1.11.4   Reforestation and assisted natural regeneration (ANR) 
After the 2015 fire, the majority of the project area is now covered in young regenerating fallows and shrubs, 
with a small remaining forest block on the eastern side of the project area (see Section 2.1.7 for a full land 
cover listing). Over the lifetime of the project, the intent is to support the recovery of the project area back 
to a fully forested state. This will be achieved primarily through natural regeneration due to the absence of 
fire  disturbance  and  restoration  of  the  project  area’s  natural  hydrological  functions.  Further  forest  
regeneration activities will be conducted through a combination of active planting and ANR activities to both 
support natural regeneration and canal blocking. The three key areas for reforestation and ANR activities 
are currently considered:  
• The area to the north of the largest remaining forest block in the eastern part of the project area to 
act as a natural link to the Berbak-Sembilang national park, which is to the east of the project area;  
• The  areas  in  and  around  the  existing  gelam  tree  vegetation  to  promote  biodiversity  values  and  
reduce the risk of fire, and;  
• The areas bordering conservation forest areas of nearby concessions.  
Key reforestation and ANR activities include: 
• Establishment  of  tree  nurseries  at  guard  posts  and  within  neighboring  areas  (see  below  on  tree  
nurseries  as  a  community  development  activity)  by  collecting  seeds  and  seedlings  from  the  
remaining forest block in the eastern part of the SMPP area. Only native species shall be used for 
any planting activities. 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
43 
• Trialing of ANR and reforestation approaches to inform the development of a final reforestation and 
ANR strategy. 
• Annual ANR and planting activities in the project area. 
• Potential technical support to reforestation activities in the nearby Kepayang village forest.  
The reforestation and ANR activities will lead to reduced fire risk, the restoration of natural habitats, carbon 
storage, and generation of local incomes through the involvement of communities.  
2.1.11.5   Community development 
Almost none of the members of the communities in the broader project zone derive direct livelihood benefits 
from the project area. Nevertheless, SMPP is committed to supporting community development activities 
in the villages closest to the project, to demonstrate how large land-use projects can act as positive agents 
of  change  in  the  broader  landscape  in  which  they  operate.  Furthermore,  building  strong  and  supportive  
relationships with local communities prevents risks associated with future encroachment in the case where 
the  project’s  restored  ecological  functions  become  attractive  for  illegal  activities  such  as  logging  or  
poaching.  
Key community development activities include: 
• Education  support  through  continued  education  opportunities  for  local  teachers  through  Open  
University  (Universitas  Terbuka),  after  school  programs  for  elementary  school  children,  adult  
education for employees who do not hold a high school diploma, improvement of elementary and 
middle school sanitation facilities, and enrichment and maintenance of school libraries. 
• Improved livelihoods and quality of life through local food sourcing for project employees, training 
for the craftsmen/women and support to community health programs. 
• Investments in women’s groups in both Muara Merang and Kepayang villages.  
• Youth and local employment to increase the number of SMPP field staff recruited locally and the 
creation of educational development and capacity building opportunities for employees.  
• Community  garbage  and  plastic  waste  cleanup  initiatives  including  plastic  recycling  and  trash  
collection, which creates value from plastic waste, supported by community clean up events on a 
monthly basis. 
• Co-financing for the construction of household rainwater catchment systems to address the need 
of clean and pollution-free water 
Community  investments  are  designed  to  be  an  integral  part  of  SMPP’s  operating  expenses.  The  project  
aims  to  deliver  tangible  benefits  to  surrounding  communities  by  through  community-based  business  
development programs and education initiatives. The project will continue to allocate a significant portion 
of its operating budget for community development throughout its long-term operations.  
2.1.11.6   Biodiversity management and monitoring 
The project expects that through the implementation of the above listed patrolling, fire suppression, canal 
blocking,  and  regeneration  activities,  biodiversity  quantities  and  variety  will  increase.  This  will  be  due  to  
both the reduction in threats and restoration of natural habitats. As such, biodiversity specific activities are 
limited to monitoring programs at this stage. The monitoring program will track potential fluctuations across 
specific species and inform management activities across the SMPP area. 
The  project  intends  to  devote  significant  resources  to  detecting,  identifying  and  understanding  the  
characteristics,  distribution,  sensitivity  and  vulnerability  of  HCVs.  The  intensity  of  SMPP  activities  are  
considered  low  because  all  or  most  of  the  natural  ecosystems  in  the  project  area  are  designated  as  
conservation areas and activities include restoring degraded natural ecosystems. Furthermore, there is no 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
44 
activity that relates to converting forest to monoculture plantation, grazing or browsing by domestic animals, 
or substantial abstraction of water.  
2.1.11.7   Cross-cutting 
This final group of direct interventions does not directly address specific threats. However, this cross-cutting 
allows for the overall enabling conditions that support the effectiveness and sustainability of the other direct 
project interventions.  
Key cross-cutting interventions include: 
• Implementation  of  adaptive  management:  The  effectiveness  of  project  interventions  will  be  
periodically  reviewed  to  determine  if  changes  are  necessary  in  the  location,  intensity,  approach,  
capacity needs, and resourcing of activities. Evaluations will be primarily conducted by the Project 
Management Board and key technical staff.  
• Implementation of effective administration and human resources protocols: The backbone of any 
organization  or  project  is  an  effective  administrative  system  and  qualified,  well-trained  staff  to  
implement  project  activities.  These  systems  and  protocols  will  be  continuously  reviewed  and  
revised to ensure effective project management and logistical planning. 
• Robust   financial   management:   This   includes   the   implementation   of   accounting   systems,   
development of clear financial management SOPs and elaboration of annual budgets. Additionally, 
it requires the marketing and sale of VCUs to ensure the ongoing financial flows required to sustain 
the long-term sustainability of the project.  
• Ensuring  legal  compliance:  To  retain  the  legal  rights  associated  with  the  PAN/RAP  license,  the  
project will need to abide by a number of reporting requirements at various levels of government, 
conduct  various  forest  carbon  management  activities  including  monitoring,  evaluating,  and  
reporting, pay taxes, prepare work plans and performance reports for the Ministry of Forestry, and 
ensure ongoing compliance with all other requirements to maintain its active license status.  
2.1.12   Sustainable Development  
The  SMPP  is  aligned  with  a  number  of  sustainable  development  and  environmental  protection  targets  
designated  by  the  Indonesian  government.  The  following  key  policy  components  align  directly  with  our  
project   objectives,   including   emphasizing   climate   action,   supporting   communities,   and   protecting   
biodiversity.  
Indonesia’s  Medium-Term  Development  Plan  covers  nine  priorities,  called  Nawacita,  which  include  
references  to  sustainable  development  in  the  forestry  sector.  Specifically,  the  Environment  and  Forestry  
Development Strategic Priorities Plan for the period between 2015-2019
13
 aims to protect the quality of the 
environment to maintain ecosystem services function and to improve water security and community health;  
utilize forest and environmental resources in sustainable manner to improve the economy and prosperity 
of local people; and preserve the balance of ecosystems and biodiversity as a way to support sustainable 
development.  
These targets for reporting and monitoring are listed below.  
2.1.12.1   Economic growth and development 
The government seeks to improve the economic development of Indonesia with a target to increase the per 
capita income from USD 3,918 in 2015 to USD 6,0181 by the end of 2019.
14
 
The   SMPP   contributes   to   economic   growth   by   providing   project   stakeholders   with   employment   
opportunities and a variety of livelihood enhancement activities. The project will employ local stakeholders 
including  work  involving  forest  protection  and  facility  management,  and  provide  access  to  education,  as  
well as livelihood enhancements from investments into income generating activities.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
45 
2.1.12.2   Poverty and equity 
Based  on  Indonesia’s  National  Medium-Term  Development  Plan  for  2015-2019  it  is  expected  that  the  
poverty rate can decrease to 7.0-8.0% by the end of 2019 and the unemployment rate can become 4.0-
5.0%  by  the  end  of  2019.  In  order  to  achieve  these  goals,  the  Indonesian  government  plans  to  improve  
access to job opportunities by increasing the productivity of industries that can create jobs and increasing 
workers’ living standards and changing low-skilled industries to skills-based industries. The government is 
highly  focused  on  lowering  poverty  levels  in  rural  areas,  to  develop  Indonesia  from  the  periphery  by  
strengthening regions and villages.
14
 
The  SMPP  will  support  rural  poverty  reduction  by  generating  employment  for  groups  with  low  education  
levels and support an increase in household incomes. The SMPP has a target to hire 80% of its field staff 
from  communities  in  proximity  to  the  project  area.  In  addition,  the  SMPP  is  working  to  ensure  gender  
balance and aims to employ 25% women as project staff, along with a community development component 
targeting a number of women’s groups in nearby villages and settlements. 
2.1.12.3   Education 
The Ministry of Education and Culture has a target to increase the capacity and quality of 14,000 educators 
throughout the country. The government also aims to improve infrastructure for education by building 210 
new schools and 2,500 classroom; rehabilitating 41,000 classrooms and renovating 294 schools as well as 
building  2,140  laboratories  and  1,332  libraries.
15
 The  SMPP’s  educational  program  is  clearly  in  line  with  
these development targets as it seeks to support school infrastructure initiatives and increase the capacity 
of local teachers in the area. 
2.1.12.4   Sustainable forest and land-use 
The Indonesian Peat Restoration Agency (Badan Restorasi Gambut or BRG) has a target to restore two 
million ha of peatlands in the country. The project area is located in one of the agency’s priority area.
16
  
2.1.12.5   Climate change 
Indonesia ratified the Paris Agreement as part of its commitment to address climate change and submitted 
its National Determined Contribution (NDC) which is also in line with Nawacita priorities that are integrated 
across  the  Medium-Term  Development  Plan.  The  SMPP  aims  to  contribute  to  Indonesia’s  overall  CO
2
 
emission reduction target by 2020 in conjunction with the appropriate monitoring and reporting.

The project start date is January 1
st
, 2016, as according to the VCS definition, this was the date activities 
on  the  ground  were  begun  to  contribute  toward  GHG  emission  reductions  and  removals.  In  January  of

of the project’s main camp and by starting to conduct formal patrol activities leading to effective prevention 
of fire and forest degradation.

2.1.19   Benefit Permanence (G1.11) 
While the SMPP has a 25-year IUP PAN/RAP carbon storage and sequestration license that is extendable 
up  to  50  years,  the  project’s  activities  are  designed  to  enhance  the  CCB  benefits  beyond  the  project’s  
lifetime.  Community  development  activities  are  designed  to  increase  family  incomes  by  creating  local  
employment opportunities. The project will make investments into small businesses and provide support to 
increase the quality of education to increase the education levels among nearby community members. By 
investing  in  the  human  capital  of  the  region  SMPP  support  can  create  more  possibilities  for  formal  
employment  with  more  reliable  and  higher  wages.  The  project  will  also  promote  the  importance  of  
conservation  and  hydrology  management  to  nearby  villages  as  a  way  build  local  stewardship  for  the  
landscape. Youth will be targeted to promote a shift away from exploitative land-use practices to activities 
in  the  formal  economy.  These  community  development  activities  are  outlined  in  more  detail  in  Section  
2.1.11.  
From a biodiversity permanence perspective, the SMPP will rewet the peatlands through the construction 
of permanent canal blocks which is a recognized approach to restore the hydrology of this degraded area, 
which will reduce the risk of fire in the landscape allowing the natural vegetation to be restored over the life 
of the project. This activity is explained in more detail in Section 2.1.11.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
50 
2.1.20   Financial Sustainability (G1.12) 
The SMPP and its project proponents have received an investment from Althelia Climate Fund (ACF) and 
the Belantara Foundation totaling 5.6 million Euros which is sufficient to cover all project costs until it has 
secured the needed revenues to develop a sustainable revenue source. ACF has a proven track record in 
investing  in  conservation  projects  globally  and  supporting  them  to  achieve  VCS/CCBA  certification.  In  
collaboration with its partner organization, Ecosphere+, ACF is experienced in marketing and sales of VCS 
credits on the global market and has already attracted potential buyers for the SMPP credits. Forest Carbon, 
one  of  the  project  proponents,  has  a  track  record  supporting  projects  as  they  develop  and  obtain  
VCS/CCBA certification. Along with PT Global Alam Lestari, both project proponents have been in operation 
for a number of years and are established companies with stable cash flows. Documents supporting these 
investments  and  outlining  the  project’s  financial  feasibility  and  breakeven  and  can  be  produced  for  the  
project auditor for inspection.  
2.1.21   Grouped Projects  
This is not a grouped project.  
2.2  Without-project Land-Use Scenario and Additionality  
2.2.1 Land-Use Scenarios without the Project (G2.1) 
The conditions of the project area prior to the start of the project can be described as a highly degraded 
peatland area, resulting from consistent and severe fire incidents and illegal logging activities spanning the 
last 20 years. The condition of the project site was not a result of actions by the project or related parties to 
purposely create GHG emission potential for subsequent reduction. 
We demonstrate that the project has not been implemented to generate GHG emissions for the purpose of 
their subsequent reduction, removal or destruction in Section 3.1.4 and the project’s baseline scenario and 
additionality  is  justified  by  using  the  Clean  Development  Mechanism  (CDM)  Afforestation/Reforestation 
(A/R) Methodological tool “Combined tool to identify the baseline scenario and demonstrate additionality in 
A/R CDM project activities”.  
As described in Section 3.1.4 the most likely land-use in the absence of the project is the establishment of 
an industrial Acacia plantation. The 2015 fires would likely have been beneficial for an Acacia plantation 
company  as  the  fires  would  have  cleared away  significant  aboveground  biomass  that  the  plantation  
company  otherwise  would  have  had  to  clear  mechanically.  As  such,  the  SMPP  area,  in  its  post-2015 
condition, would have been considered as an attractive area for an Acacia plantation company as it would 
have been able to establish its operations more extensively from bordering concessions and at lower cost 
than in the pre-2015 condition.  
2.2.2 Most-Likely Scenario Justification (G2.1) 
Justification of the most likely land-use scenario in the baseline is provided in Section 3.1.4.    
2.2.3 Climate, Community and Biodiversity Additionality (G2.2) 
Benefits to the climate, communities and biodiversity would be negatively impacted in the absence of the 
project. The most credible baseline for the project is the development of an industrial paper pulp plantation 
as there are no financial, technological, institutional, or capacity barriers. 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
51 
In terms of biodiversity, an industrial plantation would clear most of the concession area and only keep a 
small portion of the area, usually 10%, as a conservation area. While an Acacia plantation can have certain 
biodiversity benefits such as connectivity and habitat for certain niche species, it does not create anywhere 
near the benefits of a natural forest area due to on-going commercial activities. 
Industrial plantation companies often have Corporate Social Responsibility programs and make donations 
to nearby communities. However, the communities that generate their livelihoods from the project area such 
as fishermen and small-scale farmers would lose access to these livelihood resources if a plantation was 
developed. Further, the SMPP will have a more comprehensive community development plan and will be 
investing in communities at a higher level than that of a business as usual Acacia plantation company. 
In terms of climate, the baseline scenario would emit millions of tons of CO
2
 further causing climate change. 
As  a  result  of  the  project  activities  such  as  canal  blocking,  the  SMPP  will  prevent  CO
2
  emissions  from  
occurring as compared to the baseline scenario as outlined in section 2.1.17. 
The identified without project scenario for both communities and biodiversity is described in more detail in 
Section 4.1.4 and 5.1.3 respectively. 
2.2.4 Benefits to be used as Offsets (G2.2) 
The project does not intend to generate any community or biodiversity benefits to be used as offsets.  
2.3  Stakeholder Engagement  
2.3.1 Stakeholder Access to Project Documents (G3.1) 
To provide stakeholders with access to project documents, the SMPP Field Office will maintain a full, printed 
version of the project description (PD) document in English for public viewing. The executive summary will 
be  translated  into  Bahasa  Indonesia  and  will  be  given  to  community  leaders  to  make  available  in  public  
places in the surrounding villages, and the full PD with its executive summary will be posted in English and 
in Bahasa Indonesia on relevant SMPP social media or website accounts.  
2.3.2 Dissemination of Summary Project Documents (G3.1) 
To  disseminate  project  documents  to  communities,  other  stakeholders,  and  the  public,  the  SMPP  Field  
Office will maintain a full, printed version of the PD in English for public viewing. The executive summary 
will be translated into Bahasa Indonesia and will be given to community leaders to make available in public 
places in the surrounding villages, and the full PD with its executive summary will be posted in English and 
in  Bahasa  Indonesia  on  relevant  SMPP  social  media  or  website  accounts.  In  addition  to  the  executive  
summary, we will provide a brochure in Bahasa Indonesia advertising the project, and an email address 
will be posted in local villages and given to SMPP communities and other stakeholders as a point of contact. 
Finally,  notices  in  Bahasa  Indonesia  advertising  the  public  comment  period  and  field  validation  will  be  
posted in communities throughout the project area. It will include details on how to make a comment on the 
CCB version.  
2.3.3 Informational Meetings with Stakeholders (G3.1) 
The  project  began  conducting  informational  meetings  in  2016  and  continued  to  conduct  more  informal  
follow-up meetings through 2019. The purpose of these meetings was to continue to build trust and forge 
relationships  with  project  stakeholders  and  engage  in  further  efforts  to  understand  the  local  history,  
livelihoods and potential community concerns. Identification of other social, religious, and economic factors 
that could potentially influence how the community respond to the project were also considered to ensure 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
52 
a more seamless transition for surrounding communities and other stakeholders as implementation begins 
to scale-up. 
2.3.4 Community Costs, Risks, and Benefits (G3.2) 
The  project  has  implemented  a  variety  of  social  consultations,  described  further  in  Section  2.3.7,  where  
information was provided on the potential costs, risks and benefits to the identified communities. The SMPP 
aims  to  work  collaboratively  with  the  identified  communities  of  fishermen  and  farmers  to  ensure  their  
engagement  throughout  the  duration  of  the  project  (see  sections  2.1.6 and 2.1.9 for  more  details  on  
community and stakeholder groups). These identified project communities are familiar with the project and 
its intentions as a result of consultative meetings. The primary communication method with the communities 
is through direct meetings as many do not have mobile phones and are often located at their remote fishing 
areas or farms. 
Other stakeholders, who are residents of Muara Merang, Kepayang, and other nearby settlements were 
also engaged as part of the consultative process. The project organized formal group meetings that were 
announced  through  letters  to  village  officials.  These  consultation  meetings  with  other  stakeholders  took  
place during May and July 2018 in Muara Merang and Kepayang. Follow up meetings have taken place 
with  other  stakeholders  in  these  villages  through  one-on-one  discussions,  small  group  meetings,  and  
through participation in formal village meetings where village officials invited project representatives. These 
meetings with other stakeholders discussed project costs, risks and benefits to the relevant stakeholders.
  
Further, an SMPP field office was established in early 2016 and now serves as an information hub that is 
open to community members and other stakeholders who wish to gain information about the project and/or 
submit comments or grievances.  
The communication with the fishermen and farmers can be improved through more consistent meetings. 
The project will therefore conduct quarterly meetings with the fishermen and farmers and discuss project 
activities and potential impacts on them. The project will also conduct quarterly meetings with stakeholders 
in  Merang  and  Kepayang  to  provide  updates  on  the  implementation  of  the  community  development  
program.  If  any  concerns  are  highlighted  by  the  communities  and  stakeholders,  these  will  be  addressed  
with follow up meetings and plans to mitigate any negative impact. 
The consultative process consisted of multiple one-on-one meetings with community members, and larger 
formal group meetings with other stakeholders from the villages of Muara Merang and Kepayang. Details 
of the consultations including the dates, locations and number of attendants, are provided in Section 2.3.7.  
2.3.5 Information to Stakeholders on Validation and Verification Process (G3.3) 
To provide information to communities and other stakeholders on the validation and verification process, 
the  SMPP  Field  Office  will  maintain  a  full,  printed  version  of  the  PD  in  English  for  public  viewing.  The  
executive  summary  will  be  translated  into  Bahasa  Indonesia  and  will  be  given  to  community  leaders  to  
make available in public places in surrounding villages, and the full PD and its executive summary will be 
posted in English and Bahasa Indonesia on relevant SMPP social media or website accounts. In addition 
to the executive summary, we will provide a brochure in Bahasa Indonesia advertising the project, and an 
email address will be posted in local villages and given to SMPP communities and other stakeholders as a 
point  of  contact.  Finally,  notices  in  Bahasa  Indonesia  advertising  the  public  comment  period  and  field  
validation will be posted in communities throughout the project area. It will include details on how to make 
a comment on the CCB version.  
2.3.6 Site Visit Information and Opportunities to Communicate with Auditor (G3.3) 
The  SMPP  staff  meet  with  the  identified  project  communities  and  residents  of  nearby  villages  and  
settlements  on  a  frequent  basis.  In  order  to  prepare  for  the  audit,  project  staff  will  inform  relevant
