2.3.7 Stakeholder Consultations (G3.4) 
The project has conducted a combination of formal stakeholder consultations and informational meetings 
with the communities dating back to 2016 (see Section 2.3.3). We will continue to facilitate these meetings 
throughout the duration of the project. We recognize the feedback and advice provided by these parties is 
critical  to  the  long-term  success  of  this  project,  and  therefore  consider  all  information  gathered  in  these  
meetings. Additionally, this feedback is a valuable opportunity for us to enhance and adapt all aspects of 
project design and implementation according to guidance from relevant identified stakeholders.  
Participatory Rural Appraisal (PRA), a method used to conduct social surveys in rural settings in a quick 
and efficient manner, is described above in Section 2.1.8. Our community development team have carried 
out  extensive  direct  engagement  with  the  communities  and  other  stakeholders  in  the  SMPP  and  
surrounding villages and settlements. Overall, since our project start date in 2016, this effort has consisted 
of a variety of meetings and discussions. This engagement has been broad and in addition to formalized 
discussions, many meetings were informal conversations without specific written agendas. However,  we  
have documented at least 22 engagements with community fishermen, one engagement with a community 
farmer,  50  engagements  with  school  groups  or  other  education  facilitators,  11  engagements  with  village  
officials,  9  engagements  with  managers  from  nearby  concessions,  3  engagements  with  local  women’s  
groups, and 45 engagements with village residents in the Muara Merang and Kepayang villages and other 
nearby  settlements.  A  summary  of  these  meetings  is  outlined  in  Table 7  below.  These  stakeholder  
consultations  and  engagements  consisted  of  one-on-one  interviews,  group  interviews,  formal  meetings,  
and  focus  group  discussions  (FGDs).  Throughout  the  duration  of  the  project,  until  most  recently  in  April  
2019,  we  have  consulted  with  the  local  communities  and  stakeholders  to  ensure  mutual  understanding  
regarding all project activities and potential impacts. 
Stakeholders were consulted on the development of the community development program. During a series 
of meetings, communities requested support on improving the education of the nearby villages, supporting 
the health facilities that exist, and supporting them on improved livelihoods. As a result, these programs 
have been included in the design of the program.

7 within our communities, including with one farmer from Karang Agung, three fishermen from Kepayang, 
and one fisherman from Bakung, a sub-village of Muara Merang. In addition, other stakeholders from the 
Muara  Merang  area  including  Bakung  and  Bina  Desa,  from  the  Kepayang  area,  and  from  unofficial  
settlements including Tebing Harapan, Buring, and Sagita were interviewed in this setting. There were 7 
females and 20 males that participated in the interviews. We also conducted meetings directly with local 
women’s  groups  in  Muara  Merang  and  Kepayang  villages.  These  interviews  have  provided  invaluable  
information  about  the  activities  of  communities  and  other  stakeholders  in  relation  to  the  SMPP.  As  we  
continue to engage with these groups throughout the duration of the project, we will pay attention to the 
attitudes of individuals and listen to the feedback provided, so that we can employ adaptive management 
to guarantee positive impacts to all entities. Our PRA approach to community engagement and stakeholder 
consultations ensures that we maintain long-term positive relationships with these local groups, to maintain 
direct lines of communication between stakeholders and the project proponent.

2.3.8 Continued Consultation and Adaptive Management (G3.4) 
Throughout  the  lifetime  of  the  project,  we  will  maintain  a  direct  line  of  communication  with  community  
members, and relevant stakeholders. This will establish a commitment to communication and consultation 
to keep stakeholders informed of project activities including restoration, maintenance, monitoring and the 
CCB  validation  and  verification  process.  SMPP  staff  in  the  field  will  maintain  communications  with  the  
fishermen  and  farming  community  groups  and  other  stakeholders  through  in-person  meetings.  We  will  
actively  listen  to  recommendations  made  by  any  identified  community  members,  or  other  stakeholder  
groups, and adapt and improve our methods as necessary.  
The project will host a meeting and site visit with impacted fishermen to assess the impact of the dams on 
their ability to continue subsistence fishing and receive input on how to improve conditions for them. The 
project will look for solutions that meet the farmer’s needs and work to implement them. The results of these 
impact mitigation activities will be reviewed in the quarterly meetings. 
Our  project  has  an  adaptive  management  plan  to  effectively  grow  as  our  project  progresses,  and  
systematically  develop  existing  practices  through  project  monitoring  and  evaluation.  We  will  periodically  
review our plans, methods, goals and objectives, to incorporate new lessons learned, available technology, 
and  scientific  knowledge.  These  strategies  will  be  in  accordance  with  our  project’s  Standard  Operating  
Procedures (SOPs) and our monitoring plans (see sections 0, 4.4.1, 5.4.1).   
2.3.9 Stakeholder Consultation Channels (G3.5) 
We have conducted a number of stakeholder engagement and consultation meetings with identified project 
communities  and  other  stakeholders  from  the  nearby  villages  and  settlements.  Our  project  staff  have  
conducted  participatory  surveys  with  the  Participatory  Rural  Appraisal  (PRA)  method  as  part  of  the  
consultative process. This included focus group discussions (FGDs), interviews with men and women living 
in  nearby  settlements  and  villages,  and  observation  and  ground  checks  with  local  residents  including  
participatory  visits  to  a  variety  of  important  areas  and  community  epicenters.  See  Section  2.3.3 to  learn  
more  about  informational  meetings  with  stakeholders  and  Section  2.3.2  for  more  information  on  project  
information dissemination.  
2.3.10   Stakeholder Participation in Decision-Making and Implementation (G3.6) 
The SMPP engages in consistent consultations with project communities and other stakeholders on project 
implementation and design as a way to incorporate local knowledge into the project. Consultations ensure 
to engage with both men and women, and more marginal stakeholder groups in culturally appropriate ways 
to ensure that the project can hear a wide range of perspectives. Our project consultations and methods 
are described in more detail in Section 2.3.7.  
2.3.11   Anti-Discrimination Assurance (G3.7) 
Our project team holds every individual involved in project design and implementation to a high standard 
of integrity, to ensure no one is involved in, or complicit in, any form of discrimination or sexual harassment 
with  respect  to  the  project.  This  includes  discrimination  based  on  gender,  race,  religion,  ethnicity,  social  
class, or other aspects of human individuality. Throughout the project duration, the SMPP will abide by all 
relevant  Indonesian  labor  laws  and  regulations.  Further,  the  project  has  an  equal  opportunity  policy  that  
ensures that the project will not engage in or be complicit in any form of discrimination or sexual harassment. 
To ensure compliance, the project has a grievance system in place that will provide all project employees, 
identified  community  members,  and  other  stakeholders  with  a  formal  system  in  the  event  that  any 
discriminatory actions or sexual harassment does occur, to ensure grievances can be addressed quickly 
and appropriately.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
56 
2.3.12   Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (G3.8) 
We recognize the importance of a feedback grievance redress mechanism within the SMPP to keep the 
project  running  smoothly  and  free  of  conflict.  If  conflicts  or  grievances  arise,  the  project  relies  on  the  
feedback  and  grievance  redress  policy.  This  process  is  designed  to  be  an  efficient,  fair  and  accessible  
mechanism  for  resolving  complaints  and  conflicts  in  a  transparent  and  comprehensive  manner.  The  
mechanism  can  also  address  issues  or  conflict  between  the  community  and  the  project,  or  issues  use  
related to employee rights, work practices, and employee safety raised by project staff or contractors.  
If any grievances occur and are reported from surrounding communities or other relevant stakeholders, we 
will quickly respond to them by following the formal mechanism process. Grievances will be assessed to 
identify  and  verify  the  cause,  actors  and  scale  of  grievances,  and  our  project  team  will  recommend  
resolution options based on feedback from the stakeholders. If a grievance is not amicably resolved after 
this process, it will be submitted to an unbiased third party for a formal mediation and arbitration process, 
and subject to a hearing at which both disputing parties can testify. All cases will be referred and examined 
to the extent allowed by Indonesian laws and regulations of the relevant jurisdiction before decisions are 
made. 
This  grievance  policy  is  outlined  in  the  document  “SMPP Grievance Redress Mechanism” and will be 
described  in  general  at  community  meetings,  and  the  document  will  be  provided  to  key  locations  in  the  
project  area.  Further,  the  grievance  policy  was  translated  into  Bahasa Indonesia and  will  be  kept  at  the  
SMPP field office and given to project community members other stakeholders. To ensure transparency, 
the project will maintain a record book with all received and resolved grievances, which can be viewed upon 
request by project stakeholders or auditors. 
2.3.13   Accessibility of the Feedback and Grievance Redress Procedure (G3.8) 
Our project encourages identified community members, stakeholders, and employees to visit the project 
office  and  forest  patrol  posts  which  are  located  around  the  perimeter  of  the  project  area  to  discuss  any  
issues or feedback directly with project staff. Verbally reported grievances will be documented by project 
staff and submitted into the formal system for resolution. At these project locations, grievance submission 
forms will be available and can be submitted directly to project staff. In nearby settlements, the project’s 
community  liaison  officer’s  phone  number  and  email  will  be  distributed  to  village  leaders  and  will  be  
available to meet with stakeholders to receive reported grievances. To ensure transparency, the project will 
maintain a record book with all received and resolved grievances, which can be viewed upon request by 
project stakeholders or auditors. 
2.3.14   Worker Training (G3.9) 
The SMPP has a target to employ 80% of its field staff with residents of nearby villages and settlements. 
As  part  of  the  on-boarding  process,  the  project  provides  all  staff  with  an  orientation  program  where  it  
provides  overviews  of  the  project's  Standard  Operating  Procedures  (SOPs)  and  policies  with  specific  
attention to health and safety policies. The employees also receive training in the use of technology such 
as GPS units, computers, solar energy, and firefighting equipment which will expand employee knowledge 
increasing  their  prospects  of  future  employment.  Local  capacity  is  constantly  being  supported  through  
training  programs  and  recruitment  drives  for  full-time  and  part  time  employment  opportunities  to  ensure  
strong local capacity despite staff turnover. 
2.3.15   Community Employment Opportunities (G3.10) 
The  SMPP  is  an  equal  opportunity  employer  and  is  committed  to  engage  with  the  identified  community  
groups  and  local  stakeholders  on  employment  opportunities.  To  achieve  this,  the  project  has  a  policy  to  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
57 
hire 80% of its staff from local areas as a way to increase community and stakeholder benefits. Further, the 
project  has  a  target  to  have  25%  of  its  work  force  be  women  to  ensure  greater  gender  balance  in  fields  
traditionally dominated by men. To create opportunities for marginal groups, the project also has positions 
available  for  staff  who  have  not  obtained  high  school  diplomas  to  ensure  these  vulnerable  groups  have  
access  to  meaningful  employment  opportunities.  The  project  also  works  with  staff  to  increase  their  
education levels through continuing adult education programs. 
Job opportunities are publicly advertised in similar channels where other project news is publicized, such 
as  community  sign boards  and  announcements  via  community  leaders.  Meetings  are  convened  within  
project stakeholder villages and settlements as part of the recruitment process in order to spread the word 
and  attract  candidates.  The  project  has  also  communicated  that  it  is  always  actively  recruiting  for  forest  
patrol officers and that interested parties can apply directly at the project office.  
Job applicants are selected for interviews based on their skills and experience required for the advertised 
positions. Successful candidates are then selected in accordance with the project’s equal opportunity policy. 
Applicants  who  live  in  the  local  villages  and  settlements  are  given  preference  in  the  case  where  two  
applicants show the same capacity for a given position whereas one is from the local area and the other is 
not. 
2.3.16   Relevant Laws and Regulations Related to Worker’s Rights (G3.11) 
The  SMPP  complies  with  all  national  and  provincial  laws  related  to  worker’s  rights,  including:  Law  No.  
13/2003 regarding Manpower. This is an umbrella law on labor relationship in Indonesia, which stipulates 
the  working  conditions  and  rights  and  obligations  of  the  employees  and  the  Company.  The  SMPP  
operations fully attend to the rights of workers and seek to ensure that the rights of all employees are upheld 
in a manner where all individuals are aware of their rights. The project has its own human resource policies 
containing the details of employment practices that we follow. These policies are explained to all staff during 
their  orientation  program  and  are  available  for  review  in  the  project’s  field  office  and  posted  at  all  forest  
patrol offices. SMPP has principles to treat all persons in the project with dignity and respect. 
2.3.17   Occupational Safety Assessment (G3.12) 
The  project  is  committed  to  ensuring  occupational  safety  for  all  project  staff  and  follows  all  relevant  
Indonesian  worker’s  rights  laws  and  regulations,  particularly  those  pertaining  to  health  and  safety.  The  
project has created a comprehensive Health and Safety Plan and informs all staff on the workplace risks 
and  safe  practices  to  mitigate  those  risks  during  the  employee  orientation.  A  hard  copy  of  the  relevant  
project regulations will be kept at the project office for reference by project staff. Project staff are exposed 
to  a  number  of  risks  that  include  strenuous  activity  in  wet,  humid,  and  hot  conditions,  forest  fires,  
transportation accidents, interactions with illegal loggers, encounters with dangerous wildlife, and exposure 
to mosquito borne sicknesses. The health and safety training for the project addresses these risks and also 
trains  staff  on  the  proper  handling  of  equipment  and  other  materials.  In  addition,  project  proponents  
maintain a public liability insurance of 9,000,000 EUR in case major accidents do occur. 
2.4  Management Capacity  
2.4.1 Project Governance Structures (G4.1) 
The SMPP is implemented by two project proponents: PT GAL and Forest Carbon. The joint development 
of the project is managed via a project management board that is composed of representatives of PT GAL 
and  Forest  Carbon  with  an  observer  from  the  investment  partner,  Althelia  Climate  Fund.  This  body  is  
responsible for:  
• Developing and approving strategic plans; 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
58 
• Approving annual budget and large expenditures; 
• Reviewing key personnel performance; 
• Evaluating project performance;  
• Determining project finance needs; and 
• Providing oversight to subcontractors and consultants. 
At the implementation level, each proponent focuses on different areas of implementation. PT Global Alam 
Lestari, a subsidiary of an established Indonesian forestry company, specializes on the ground operations 
for  forest  restoration  in  the  project  area.  Its  staff  have  significant  experience  in  overseeing  the  
implementation  of  large-scale  land  management  projects  in  Indonesia.  As  the  holder  of  the  PAN/RAP  
license, its role in the project is to implement all field activities in line with the project design, manage and 
oversee community and stakeholder relations, and ensure compliance with all government regulations.  
Forest Carbon is a leading forest conservation and restoration company that has been working in Indonesia 
since  2007.  Forest  Carbon  has  staff  experienced  in  project  management,  VCS/CCBA  project  design,  
Geographic Information Systems (GIS) and remote sensing, forest and biomass inventories, biodiversity 
mapping and monitoring, and community engagement. Forest Carbon’s also leads project management, 
project design, field data collection and analysis, VCS/CCBA certification monitoring and compliance, and 
technical advisory services.

2.4.3 Management Team Experience (G4.2) 
Provided below is a description of the experience and qualifications of the key members of the management 
team. 
2.4.3.1 Project Management Leads 
Dr. Riza Suarga – President Director – Global Alam Lestari – SMPP Project Lead 
Dr. Suarga is PT GAL’s President Director and is a seasoned corporate executive with over 30 years of 
experience  running  large-scale  businesses  in  Indonesia’s  forestry  sector.  His  academic  credentials  are  
extensive, with a BA from Ohio University in engineering, a master’s in human resource management from 
Triskakti  University,  and  a  Ph.D.  in  natural  resource  management  from  the  University  of  Indonesia.  His  
experience  and  wide-ranging  academic  background  make  him  well-suited  to  manage  teams  within  the  
sustainable forestry and conservation sectors. As a member of the Indonesian Chamber of commerce for 
over eight years, he is currently the chamber’s liaison at the Ministry of Environment and Forestry since 
2014 where he serves as the chairman of the Ecolabel desk focusing on certification standards for products 
from the forestry sector. He has also been a member of the Indonesian Forest Concessionaires Association 
(Asosiasi  Pengusaha  Hutan  Indonesia or APHI)  since  1998  and  served  as  Vice  Chairman  of  its  Carbon  
Team as well as the Chairman of its Law & Regulations Board. He has written numerous academic articles 
covering topics such as sustainable forest management and ways to combat illegal logging in Indonesia. 
He is fluent in English, Bahasa Indonesia, and conversant in Japanese. 
Mr. Jeffrey Chatellier – Managing Director - Forest Carbon – SMPP Project Lead  
Mr. Chatellier is Forest Carbon’s Managing Director. He is an environmental scientist who has worked on 
environmental conservation, forestry, and sustainability for the past 10 years. With a combined background 
in economics and environmental science, Mr. Chatellier has advised major corporations and international 
environmental  organizations  on  forest  conservation,  environmental  risk  mitigation,  and  sustainability.  He  
has worked closely on the development of multiple REDD+ projects in Southeast Asia, which has given him 
in depth knowledge of various carbon standards and the technical and political challenges to conservation 
projects  in  the  developing  world,  including.  He  has  reviewed  existing  scientific  carbon  accounting  
methodologies  and  worked  with  companies  on  developing  the  necessary  project  documents  to  obtain  
carbon credits, Rimba Raya peatland project in Central Kalimantan, Indonesia, and the REDD+ project in 
Keo Seima Wldlife Sanctuary, Cambodia.  
In addition to these two managers, the team of project team is highly skilled in forest management, peatland 
science, Indonesian and legal and policy compliance, GIS and remote sensing, community engagement, 
that will support project management and implementation.

Sebastian Persch - Forest and Peatland Scientist – Forest Carbon – SMPP MRV 
Mr. Persch  is  a  tropical  peat  hydrology  expert  who  has  worked  on  various  topics  related  to  forestry,  
conservation, and climate change since 2011. He specializes in tropical peat ecology, including advanced 
data  analysis  and  statistical  programming  using  open  source  software  and  has  conducted  research  on  
peatlands in Sumatra on carbon stock and carbon dynamics estimation. Sebastian holds B.Sc. and M.Sc. 
in Tropical Forestry Ecology, all from the Georg-August Universität Göttingen in Germany and is currently 
pursuing  a  PhD.  He  conducted  post-doctoral  research  at  the  Center  for  International  Forestry  Research  
(CIFOR),  and  his  dissertation  focused  on  root  dynamics  within  land-use  change  from  forest  to  oil  palm  
plantation  in  the  tropical  peat  swamps  of  Berbak  National  Park  and  the  Taman  Hutan  Raya  in  Jambi,  
Indonesia. He has held previous positions and consultancies with the Centre de cooperation Internationale 
en recherche agronomique pour le developpement (CIRAD), the James Hutton Institute, and the University 
of  Gottingen.  With  Forest  Carbon  he  has  completed  assignments  modeling  baseline  and  expected  
emissions from peat drainage in Berbak National Park and in the degraded peat lands of the Ex-Mega Rice 
project area in Central Kalimantan. For those assignments Sebastian modeled emissions from drainage, 
established proxies with calibrated GHG emission profiles in Strata I and identified possible canal blocking 
locations in conjunction with Forest Carbon GIS and Remote Sensing teams.

Mary Osmond – Legal Advisor – Forest Carbon – Legal Compliance for SMPP 
Ms. Osmond is a trained lawyer with a background in business and environmental law in Indonesia. She 
holds  a  law  degree  from  Atmajaya  University  and  an  LLM  from  the  University  of  Groningen  in  the  
Netherlands and is also member of the Indonesian Bar Association (Perhimpunan Advokat Indonesia or 
PERADI). She has significant experience on government regulation and policy and previously acted as the

2.4.5 Financial Health of Implementing Organization(s) (G4.3) 
Both  project  proponents,  PT  GAL  and  Forest  Carbon,  are  financially  stable  companies,  capable  of  
implementing and maintaining a project of the scale of the SMPP. Additionally, the initial project activities 
are  fully  supported  by  the  Althelia  Climate  Fund.  See  Section  2.1.20  for  information  on  the  financial  
sustainability  of  the  project’s  funding.  Project  validators  may  request  to  see  more  specific  financial  
statements and to verify the financial health.  
2.4.6 Avoidance of Corruption and Other Unethical Behavior (G4.3) 
As  a  collaborative  effort,  the  SMPP  team  is  committed  to  upholding  a  high  level  of  integrity  and  
professionalism  throughout  all  aspects  of  project  design  and  implementation.  We  have  a  zero-tolerance 
attitude  towards  corruption  and  unethical  behavior,  and  are  not  involved  in,  or  complicit  in,  any  form  of  
corruption such as bribery, embezzlement, fraud, extortion, and collusion. The project is also compliant with 
the US Foreign Corruption Practice Act (FCPA). 
2.4.7 Commercially Sensitive Information (Rules 3.5.13 – 3.5.14)  
Our project’s commercially sensitive information that is not publicly available includes: 
• Project  implementation  plans,  financial  plans,  budgets,  and  projections  –  Including  detailed  
organizational  project  implementation  and  work  plans  as  well  as  initial  and  long-term  project  
budgets for the duration of the project (30-year projections). 
• Original  data,  electronic  shape  files,  computer  model  code,  and  classified  satellite  imagery  – 
Including GIS project boundary, land-use, and remote sensing data, biomass inventories, models 
assessing hydrology and carbon calculations, and social assessments in the SMPP area. 
• Agreements  and  contracts  between  project  proponents,  other  project  entities,  and  stakeholder  
groups – regarding implementation of project activities. 
Specific information is available privately to project validators upon request.  
2.5 Legal Status and Property Rights  
2.5.1 Statutory and Customary Property Rights (G5.1) 
In the project area there are no outside statutory claims as the project has officially received all statutory 
rights to manage the area under its PAN/RAP license as described in Section 2.5.7. However as described 
in  the  Section  2.1.6  on  identified  project  communities,  a  number  of  fishermen  from  nearby  villages  and  
settlements access the areas inside or bordering the project area. Two small farms border the concession 
area and have a total overlap of approximate 11.62 hectares. Both groups have customary usage rights in 
the  project  area  but  not  statutory  rights.  Details  of  the  areas  of  their  customary  usage  are  described  in  
Section 4.1.3 and shown in Map 32 and Map 33.
 
2.5.2 Recognition of Property Rights (G5.1) 
Our project is committed to recognizing, respecting, and supporting all property rights of communities and 
other stakeholders. An assessment of the project area showed that there are no groups living in the project 
area full time and that there were two small farms that overlap with the project license area and a small 
number  of  fishermen  that  fish  inside  and  near  the  project  area.  However,  the  project  was  specifically  
designed, following the identification of these customary rights holders, to ensure that no impingement upon 
these rights holders or their livelihoods will occur.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
63 
2.5.3 Free, Prior and Informed Consent (G5.2) 
As  outlined  previously,  the  project  does  not  impinge  on  any  rights  holder’s  access  or  use  of  the  project  
area. As per CCB requirements, FPIC is required only when these rights are negatively impacted. If it is 
ever  determined  that  the  project  impacts  these  rights  holders,  either  inadvertently  or  through  the  
implementation  of  project  activities,  then  an  FPIC  process  will  be  initiated.  Nevertheless,  the  project  
continues  to  adopt  a  highly  consultative  engagement  process  which  ensures  that  all  stakeholders  and  
communities are consulted in an informative and meaningful way.   
2.5.4 Property Rights Protection (G5.3) 
Our project activities do not lead to involuntary removal or relocation of property rights holders from their 
lands  or  territories  and  does  not  force  rights  holders  to  relocate  activities  important  to  their  culture  or  
livelihood. If in the future any relocation of activities needs to be undertaken, it will take place with a written 
agreement that demonstrates that the agreement was made with the free, prior, and informed consent of 
those concerned and includes provisions for just and fair compensation. 
2.5.5 Illegal Activity Identification (G5.4) 
Small-scale  illegal  activities,  such  as  illegal  logging  or  hunting,  were  historically  practiced  in  parts  of  the  
project area and could negatively impact the rehabilitation of the forest and reduce the returning levels of 
biodiversity. The project has constructed and staffed several forest patrol posts along the boundary of the 
project area to ensure an equal distribution of forest patrol coverage to all parts of the project area. The 
SMPP is also reducing the risk of these illegal activities and negative impacts with the implementation of 
its forest protection plan and a community development plan that provides sustainable livelihoods options. 
With stronger economic alternatives, the project expects that the need to participate in illegal activities will 
be further reduced. These project activities will reduce the risk that benefits from the project are derived 
from illegal activities. 
2.5.6 Ongoing Disputes (G5.5) 
There are no ongoing disputes or any previously unresolved conflicts inside the project area.

2.5.9 Project Ownership (G5.8) 
PT Global Alam Lestari, an SMPP project proponent, holds the land tenure rights for the SMPP, with the 
entire project area officially recognized and nationally zoned as a Production Forest area (Hutan Produksi 
or  HP).  In  2012,  PT  GAL  submitted  a  request  for  an  IUP  PAN/RAP  license  for  the  utilization  of  carbon  
sequestration  and  carbon  sinks.  This  license  was  granted  by  the  government  on  July  12
th
,  2013  by  the  
Department of Forestry (Decree of the Minister of Forestry No. SK. 494 Menhut-II/2013). This license falls 
under  regulation  P.36/Menhut-II/2009  on  the  utilization  license  of  carbon  sequestration  and/or  sink  in  
production forest and explicitly allows the project to conduct restoration and conservation activities and sell 
carbon credits on the international voluntary markets. See Section 2.5.2 for further details.  
2.5.10   Management of Double Counting Risk (G5.9) 
The  SMPP  has  not  received  any  form  of  environmental  or  social  credit,  including  any  tradable  climate,  
community  or  biodiversity  units.    The  SMPP  does  not  participate  under  any  other  program  to  create  
environmental or social credits.  
2.5.11   Emissions Trading Programs and Other Binding Limits  
The SMPP’s activities and GHG emission reductions do not fall under any other emissions trading program 
or other binding limit in relation to GHG emissions.  
2.5.12   Other Forms of Environmental Credit  
The  SMPP  only  seeks  to  generate  VCUs  and  has  never  received  another  form  of  GHG-related 
environmental credit.  
2.5.13   Participation under Other GHG Programs  
The SMPP does not participate under any other GHG program.  
2.5.14   Projects Rejected by Other GHG Programs  
The SMPP has not applied for or been rejected by any other GHG program.  
2.5.15   Double Counting (G5.9) 
The SMPP does not generate credits in a country participating in a compliance mechanism, as such double 
counting risks do not need to be considered.

3.1.3 Project Boundary 
3.1.3.1 Spatial boundary of the project area  
Following  the  VCS  Module  X-STR,  the  project  area  was  stratified  into  the  following  discrete,  relatively  
homogenous units: 
1.    Aboveground biomass (AGB) stratification and vegetation types; 
2.    Differentiation of peatland from non-peatland; 
3.    Stratification of the peatland area based on peat thickness and peat depletion time (PDT); 
4.    Area of peatland eligible for crediting; and 
5.    Stratification according to PDT.  
Sections 3.1.3.1.1  to 3.1.3.1.7 below  describe  the  procedures  followed  to  stratify  according  to  the  units  
listed  above.  All  the  analyses  took  place  following  the  project  area’s  delineation.  Spatial  models  were  
performed  in  the  resolution  (i.e.  raster  pixel  size)  that  suit  the  nature  of  input  datasets  or  parameters  
settings, and then standardized into a minimum mapping 50-m units. 
3.1.3.1.1 Aboveground biomass (AGB) stratification 
We stratified the project area into land cover classes based on vegetation types that are indicative of varying 
aboveground carbon stocks as a result of land cover classification. This classification will be the basis to 
inform the 2020 field inventory design to quantify AGB and carbon (C) in each stratum. The classification 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
73 
also informs the appropriate emission characterization and emission factors. Annex 3 provides details on 
the employed methodology to conduct the classification.  
Classification data acquisition 
We performed a land cover classification on medium-resolution Landsat 8 satellite imagery products (WRS 
path/row  125/61,  acquisition  date:  2016/08/22),  with  Landsat  8  Operational  Land  Imager  (OLI)/Thermal  
Infrared  Sensor  (TIRS)  instruments  on  board.  The  U.S.  Geological  Survey  (USGS)  offers  on-demand 
products  of  Landsat  8  OLI/TIRS  Surface  Reflectance  data.
17
  The  Surface  Reflectance  products  correct  
satellite images for atmospheric effects to create Level-2 data at a 30-m spatial resolution.     
The Level-2 products used for the project area land cover classification included surface reflectance rasters 
of seven visible multi-spectral bands (bands 17), and spectral indices of Normalized Difference Vegetation 
Index  (NDVI),  Enhanced  Vegetation  Index  (EVI),  Soil  Adjusted  Vegetation  Index  (SAVI),  Modified  Soil  
Adjusted Vegetation Index (MSAVI), Normalized Difference Moisture Index (NDMI), Normalized Burn Ratio 
(NBR),  and  Normalized  Burn  Ratio  2  (NBR2).  We  also  computed  two  other  indices  using  the  surface  
reflectance bands: Normalized Difference Water Index (NDWI) and Modified Normalized Difference Water 
Index (MNDWI). 
In addition, we transformed the original surface reflectance bands 1-7 by conducting principal component 
analysis  (PCA)  to  reduce  the  dataset  dimensionality.  With  PCA,  the  original  seven  bands  that  were  
correlated  were  transformed  into  a  simpler  dataset  of  uncorrelated  variables  representing  the  most  
important variance from the original dataset.
18
 We included the first two PCA axes into the classification 
models, which accounted for 95.5% variance of the original bands 1-7 surface reflectance distributions. 
Reference data acquisition 
For  satellite  reference  imagery,  we  obtained  two  Sentinel-2A  Level  1C  images  (acquisition  date:  
2018/06/02) from the Sentinel data hub of the European Space Agency (ESA).
19
 The multispectral visible 
bands of Sentinel images contained different spatial resolutions (20 m and 10 m). We first performed a pan-
sharpening technique to resample the visible bands to 10-m spatial resolution to enhance visual contrast, 
texture, and pattern among different vegetation types. 
We obtained 2017 high-resolution Light Detection and Ranging (LiDAR) orthorectified color photographs 
(produced and shared by Deltares, see Annex 4 for the LiDAR data acquisition specification). A total of 244 
and  48  orthophotos  at  10-cm  spatial  resolution  covered  8,622  ha  of  the  project  area.  In  addition,  we  
obtained  2017  canopy  height  data  at  a  spatial  resolution  of  25  m  (produced  and  shared  by  Deltares),  
produced from median values of canopy profile readings in 25-m grid cells.   
In 2018, we collected additional high-resolution aerial photographs using a fixed wing drone, with a 2014 
Skywalker frame, a Sony QX1 camera, and a 20-mm lens. The 20 photo strips had a spatial resolution of 
3.5-3.7 cm. The drone strips covered 4,714 ha of the project area, mostly in non-overlapping areas with the 
LiDAR orthophotos. 
From  August  to  November  2018,  our  peat  inventory  team  documented  vegetation  type  at  each  peat  
sampling location, with clear and consistent land cover class definitions given during inventory training. We 
completed peat inventory by mid-November, with approximately 80 of these ground plot data (see Annex 5 
for the peat inventory sampling design). 
Classification training sample development 
We generated randomly distributed 200 x 200 m and 100 x 100 m virtual plots within the coverage of LiDAR 
and  drone  photographs  across  the  study  area,  as  well  as  field  observation  from  approximately  80  peat  
inventory plots. These virtual plots were mostly at least 200 m apart to minimize spatial clustering. 
We were aware that all the reference data came from 2017-2018 collections, while the classification image 
was  dated  2016.  To  account  for  young  vegetation  regrowth  into  a  later  succession  type,  we  performed  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
74 
visual  examination  to  compare  color,  shape,  pattern  and  texture  using  the  same  visible  band  composite  
between the 2016 Landsat 8 and 2018 Sentinel-2A images. 
The classification model required iterative refinement on training samples in order to improve classification 
accuracy. We conducted the classification runs with anticipation to merge a few classes in the final output. 
Classification modeling 
We  employed  Random  Forest,  a  supervised,  decision  tree-based  classifier.  Earlier  studies  have  used  
Random  Forest  for classification  of  multi-sensor  remote  sensing  and  geographic  data.
20
  Random  Forest  
proved  a  robust  tool  for  yielding  accurate  land  cover  classifications,  training  data  reduction  and  noise  in  
addition to identifying the most important variables.
21,22
 
We compared preliminary classification results to determine the best input variable combination. The best 
performing input variables were NDWI and PCA axes (three bands in total). Therefore, we continued and 
finalized the Random Forest classifier with these three variables. 
Accuracy assessment 
The  reference  data  for  accuracy  assessment  included  the  LiDAR  and  drone  photos.  Given  the  need  to  
balance thematic accuracy with positional accuracy, we employed a clustered pixel approach, creating a 
cluster of pixels (i.e. virtual plots in 3 x 3 pixel size = 90 m) using a stratified random sampling design to 
place testing samples in each of the land cover classes.
23
 
To ensure that the virtual testing samples contained homogeneous information of a land cover class within 
each sample, we excluded classification features that were smaller than the virtual plots to avoid multiple 
classes seen within one reference sample. Within the remaining map, we created stratified random virtual 
testing samples relatively proportionate to the land cover class of each area, following best practices for 
random sample size.
24,25
  
In  the  Sampling  Design  Tool,
26
  we  allocated  sample  sizes  for  each  class  ranging  from  30  to  100  virtual  
plots. With the criteria of minimum distance (200 m) between samples within each stratum, random samples 
that were spatially clustered were eliminated. 
We  conducted  the  accuracy  assessment  by  visual  interpretation  to  evaluate  whether  each  virtual  plot  
belonged  to  its  corresponding  land  cover  class,  according  to  the  reference  data.  Agreement  and  
disagreement  were  summarized  in  an  error  matrix,  with  calculation  of  overall  accuracy,  user  accuracy 
(complement  of  commission  error),  producer  accuracy  (complement  of  omission  error)  and  kappa  
coefficient per guidance of Global Observation for Forest Cover and Land Dynamics (GOFC-GOLD)
27
 and 
Global Forest Observations Initiative (GFOI).
28
 
Accuracy assessment results 
The final classification of four land cover classes showed that 95.8%, 1.9%, 1.3% and 1% of the project 
area  were  1)  Open  canopy  tree/shrub/herbaceous  mix,  2)  Close  canopy  tree/shrub/herbaceous  mix,  3)  
Dense forest, and 4) Water/swamp mix, respectively. This classification achieved 92.7% overall accuracy 
and  a  kappa  coefficient  of  0.87,  indicating  87%  reduction  in  error  generated  by  the  classification  in  
comparison  with  a  completely  random  process  (Table 11).  This  final  classification  for  forest/non-forest 
classes  showed  that  1.3%  and  98.7%  of  the  project  area  contained  forest  and  non-forest  areas,  
respectively. This merged classification achieved 99.1% of overall accuracy.

The known canals and rivers, which often did not appear in the Landsat image due to the image resolution, 
were added to the final classification by overlaying our GIS canal/river network layer. This resulted in a final 
map   showing   that   95.4%,   1.9%,   1.3%   and   1.4%   of   the   project   area   were   1)   Open   canopy   
tree/shrub/herbaceous  mix,  2)  Close  canopy  tree/shrub/herbaceous  mix,  3)  Dense forest,  and  4)  
Water/swamp mix, respectively (see Map 10 below).

3.1.3.1.2 Stratification of peatland and non-peatland 
Stratification of areas in peat versus non-peat areas, as well as the peat thickness was determined through 
a field peat inventory, and subsequent peat depth modeling (see Annex 5 for a detailed description of the 
peat inventory and thickness modeling). This sampling design fulfilled the requirements described in the 
VCS methodology VM0007 modules M-PEAT  and  X-STR. The result showed that 99.99% of the project 
area constituted peat thickness ≥ 50 cm. 
3.1.3.1.3 Stratification of peatland based on peat thickness and peat depletion time (PDT) 
Drained peatland is subject to microbial decomposition and uncontrolled burning. Therefore, peat at some 
locations in the project area may be depleted before the end of project period in the baseline scenario. We 
calculated the amount of time it would have taken for the peat in the project area to become depleted (peat 
depletion time; PDT) without the project
 based on the following parameters: 
1.    Peat thickness; 
2.    Drainability elevation limit; 
3.    Surface elevation; and 
4.    Subsidence related to microbial decomposition and burning.  
Peat thickness 
Following VCS Module X-STR requirements, our analysis indicated that the entire peatland in the project 
area must be stratified, although stratification by peat thickness at a 50-cm resolution was not necessary 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
77 
(see Table  12  below).  Therefore,  a  wider  range  of  peat  thickness  was  used,  and  the  project  area  was  
stratified into 15 classes as presented in Table 13 and Map 11.

Digital elevation model and drainability elevation limit 
We assumed conservatively that in the baseline scenario, the conversion into Acacia plantations would not 
require mechanical pumping. Therefore, the Drainability Elevation Limit (DEL) restricts the thickness of peat 
that may be lost. DEL is defined as the elevation at which the peat cannot be drained any further without 
mechanical pumping. When subsidence results in the surface of the land reaches DEL, the remaining peat 
layer would stay waterlogged and no further drainage would be possible. The detailed methods used for 
creating the DEL map (Map 12) are in Annex 7 for hydrological modeling processes. 
 
 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
80 
Map 12. Drainability elevation limit of the project area.

start  date,  we  obtained  a  surface  elevation  map  (Digital  Terrain  Model  or  DTM)  derived  from  LiDAR  
(produced  and  shared  by  Deltares).  Annex 4  describes  the  specifications  of  the  LiDAR  tasks,  DTM  
processing methodology, and accuracy.

3.1.3.1.5 Stratification based on emission characteristics 
Our emission characteristics are based on the initial and designated land-use and the drainage status of 
the  project  area  in  both  the  baseline  and  project  scenarios.  Different  land-use  strata  and  drainage  
characteristics impose significant differences in emissions and carbon stock. We describe the baseline and 
project scenarios as well as associated emissions in Sections 3.2.1 and 3.2.2, which serve as a basis for 
calculating the area eligible for WRC and ARR crediting. 
3.1.3.1.6 Eligible area for WRC crediting 
We determined the area eligible for crediting by following the VCS rules as set out in VM0007 Module X-
STR Section 5.4, with the Total Stock Approach. 
We used the PDT assessment and the assessment of project scenario peat depletion to determine the area 
eligible  for  crediting  for  the  baseline  for  WRC  peatland  activities.  The  crediting  period  under  which  our  
project’s  GHG  emission  reductions  or  removals  are  generated,  closely  aligns  with  the  area  for  crediting  
within the project area.  
To delineate the eligible area for crediting within our project, we took a three-step approach. More detail is 
available in VCS methodology VM0007 Module X-STR, Section 5.4. The steps were as follows:  
Step 1. We calculated the successive changes of peat carbon stock within each stratum over 100 years 
with the baseline scenario. We then mapped the remaining carbon stocks at t = 100 (Map 17). Our methods 
for calculating baseline scenario dynamics of carbon stocks over time are given in Section 3.2.1. 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION

project  scenario.  We  then  mapped  the  remaining  carbon  stocks  at  t  =  100.  Our  methods  for  calculating  
project scenario dynamics of carbon stocks over time are given in Section 3.2.2.

3.1.3.1.7 Eligible area for ARR crediting 
VCS Agriculture, Forestry, and Other Land-Use (AFOLU) requirements state that activities to convert native 
ecosystems to generate GHG credits are not eligible under the VCS Program. Where conversion within the 
project area occurs within 10 years prior to the proposed project start date, evidence must be provided to 
demonstrate that this clearance was not done with the intent to create GHG credits.  
Annex 9 provides detailed justification for why the clearance of native ecosystems within the project area 
within the previous ten years was not for the purposes of generating GHG credits, but instead a result of 
land-use change trends within the landscape that were out of the control of the project proponent. 
Forest cover in the project area was 99.1% in 2006 according to the Ministry of Environment and Forestry’s 
national  land-use  land  cover  map.  As  per  the  land  cover  classification  results  for  the  project  boundary, 
forest cover at project start was 1.3%. This means an approximate forest loss of 97.8% of the study area 
during the ten-year historical reference period. The comparison of these two datasets is for rapid evaluation 
and is not to be considered a definitive land cover change assessment.  
During  the  historical  reference  period,  the  only  time  the  project  area  was  under  any  form  of  active  
management  was  between  2008  and  2011.  At  that  time,  the  Merang  REDD  Pilot  Project  (MRPP)  was  
financed by a donor organization with the intention of developing a REDD project. As such, its intent was 
to protect the forest cover rather than any intent to deforest the project area for the purposes of developing 
an ARR project. Funding for the project expired in 2011 and pilot activities were closed out in the area.  
Two significant fire (2012 and 2015) occurred when there was no active management of the project area. 
As per the project start date, January 2016 is when PT GAL’s first active management of the project area 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
91 
began, marked by the construction of the main camp and hiring of staff to live at the site. For both events, 
losses  occurred  due  to  both  the  presence  of  fire  vectors  (illegal  loggers,  fishermen,  etc.)  as  well  as  the  
absence of any active management of the area. In 2015 alone, an estimated 100,000 km
2
 of land burned
29
 
across Indonesia, creating extreme impacts for the economy, human health and forest cover. Fires do not 
occur naturally in peat swamp forests as they are naturally inundated. However, drainage and deforestation 
cause peatlands to become drier and more susceptible to fires. This is a trend that has occurred consistently 
across the project landscape since the early 1990s and with devastating consequences in 2015. 
These  large-scale,  uncontrollable  fires  were  not  unique  to  the  project  area  but  a  feature  of  the  wider  
landscape. These losses were incurred despite the presence of local fire teams in neighboring concessions 
which  attempted  to  manage  the  fires.  These  trends  and  specific  project  site  history  all  support  the  
conclusion  that  fires  were  a  landscape  trend,  rather  than  a  tactic  to  clear  the  land  for  the  purposes  of  
subsequently creating GHG credits from an ARR project. 
The eligible area for ARR crediting is all the non-forest areas where the project activities would carry out 
activities to facilitate natural regeneration of vegetation. Based on the spatial analysis, the area eligible for 
crediting  from  ARR  activities  is  20,627.25 ha. Map 21  indicates  the  ARR  eligible  area  within  the  project  
area, and Table 17 below is the summary of the area. 
Map 21. Eligible areas for crediting ARR project activities.

3.1.4 Baseline Scenario  
In this section we identify the baseline scenario of the SMPP and demonstrate our project’s additionality 
using the “Combined tool to identify the baseline scenario and demonstrate additionality, Version 07.0”.
30
 
Using these tools, we will carry out a preliminary screening (as ‘Step 0’).  
Step 1: Identification of alternative land-use scenarios to the proposed project activity. 
Sub-step 1a. We identified credible alternative land-use scenarios to the proposed project activity. 
There  are  a  variety  of  possible  without-project  land-use  scenarios  that  are  outlined  in  Table 21.  We  
compiled these scenarios as the most likely land conversions or future predictions of the SMPP area without 
our  project,  based  on  a  review  of  the  current  land-uses  across  lowland  peatlands  in  Sumatra  and  other  
areas of Indonesia. 
 
 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
96 
Table 21. Description of the major alternative land-use scenarios for the project area (Outcome of Step 1a). 
Land use scenario Description 
Acacia plantation 
Acacia crassicarpa is among the most common industrial land-uses of lowland 
peatlands in Indonesia.
31
 The tree crop is managed on 4 to 6 year rotations with 
the harvested wood used for paper and pulp wood products. A commercial scale 
plantation requires continuous drainage of the peat to below 70cm depth.
32
 The 
industrial Acacia plantation sector grew rapidly in Indonesia in the 2000’s and 
further development was targeted in the Ministry of Forestry development plans: 
from 10 million ha in 2010, to 13 million ha in 2014.
33
  
In the project landscape, Acacia plantations are already widespread and have 
been established in peatland areas to the north, west, south, and east of the 
project site. There is documentation that shows that applications for 
establishment of more plantations were lodged for unallocated lands in the Lalan 
Forest Management Unit where the project is located, in 2007 and 2011 by a 
number of plantation companies
34–36 
which demonstrates a clear threat of 
conversion in the project area.  
The massive expansion of Acacia plantations in Indonesia has already led to high 
levels of drainage and full conversion of extensive areas of peatlands, 
demonstrating that this is a high potential land-use in the project area. 
Industrial Oil Palm 
Plantation 
An industrial oil palm plantation is a very common agricultural land-use of lowland 
peatlands in Indonesia,
37
 despite the fact that peat soils are not ideal for its 
cultivation.
38
 The crop is managed on 25 to 35-year rotations and becomes ready 
for harvest after 4-5 years. Like industrial Acacia plantations, palm oil plantations 
require continuous drainage of the peat to below 70cm depth.
22
  
The oil palm sector in Indonesia has increased dramatically over the past few 
decades
39
 including in South Sumatra.  A recent remote-sensing study 
investigating closed canopy palm oil plantations in the lowlands of Sumatra (3.9 
million ha) revealed that by the early 2000s, 6% (or ≈880,000 ha) of tropical 
peatlands in the region had been converted to palm oil plantations. Further, there 
is a palm oil concession located in fairly close proximity to the west and south of 
the project area, including areas that were previously forested peatlands. 
However industrial oil palm expansion has taken place almost exclusively in 
areas legally outside of the forest estate (designated as Other Land Utilization, 
Area Penggunaan Lain, or APL) or within the forest estate in areas ear-marked 
for conversion (designated Conversion Production Forest, Hutan Produksi 
Konversi, or HPK). The project area has no area within its boundaries designated 
as APL or HPK. 
Forest with 
commercial 
logging 
The forested peatlands of South Sumatra were extensively logged by commercial 
entities from 1970-2000 using selective cutting approach. The project area was 
also logged by commercial entities over a number of years as well dating back to 
the 1970’s.
12
 Currently, none of the production forest on peatland in the project 
area landscape is subject to active commercial logging today as most timber 
companies have shut down operations due to changes in the sector that make 
logging unprofitable. One major change has been a tightening of regulations on 
management practices, taxation, and labor management. Historically, timber 
companies were not required to implement long-term management of the areas 
and instead harvested timber at unsustainable levels which led to the natural 
forest areas having few remaining timbers within allowable diameter sizes. With 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
97 
Land use scenario Description 
little remaining timber, the focus has switched to conversion to oil palm 
plantations.  
Unexploited 
Forest 
There are limited forest areas in Indonesia that are unexploited. However, this is 
not the norm in Sumatra and generally these forest areas remain unexploited for 
short periods of time only as a transitional state between phases of commercial 
exploitation. Unexploited forest areas with no formal management activities, 
quickly become degraded as outside actors seek to claim land for farming or 
extract resources like timber. These areas rapidly lose all commercial value as a 
timber concession and become future targets for conversion to industrial 
agriculture or industrial forest plantations which has been the case in the district 
of Musi Banyuasin where the project is located. 
Protected Forest 
Forest area that is designated as Production Forest can legally be converted into 
a protected area although it is rare. In regions near the project, there were some 
former logging concession areas that were converted to national parks, including 
parts of the Berbak-Sembilang National Parks. Further afield, throughout 
Indonesia, a number of production forest areas in the highlands were listed as 
Watershed Protection forest (Hutan Lindung). 
Smallholder 
agriculture 
Small holder agriculture on peatlands is not a major activity in the project region 
due the unsuitability of peat soils for agriculture without commercial scale 
drainage and the high risk of fire in these areas due to extended dry periods from 
El Nino weather patterns. 
The small farms that exist in the landscape are found closer to the rivers and 
villages where mineral soils allow for a variety of tree and non-tree crops, 
including rubber, cassava, pineapple, and oil palm.  
While the project zone has two smallholder farmers located on peat soils that 
cover an area of 12 hectares, smallholder agriculture is not considered a likely 
land-use for the project area. 
Mining and 
Natural Gas 
Extraction 
In the project landscape, coal mining and natural gas extraction are present. 
However, it is not considered a likely land-use for the project as the region was 
surveyed for gas and coal many years prior to the start of the project and mining 
concessions were already awarded for the area and have been in existence for 
considerable time. 
 
In  addition  to  these  major  credible  land-use  scenarios,  we  also  considered  a  number  of  other  land-uses 
including infrastructure development and industrial aquaculture. However, to be included in the analysis, all 
were considered to either lack sufficient credibility or precedence. 
3.1.5 Additionality 
Sub-step 1b. We ensured consistency of credible alternative land-use scenarios in the context of applicable 
and enforced mandatory laws and regulations. 
The major credible land-use scenarios identified under Sub-step 1a were assessed from a legal perspective 
based on Indonesian laws and regulations. Since the project area is completely designated as ‘Production 
Forest’ or ‘Hutan Produksi’, the analysis focuses on what is legally permissible in this type of land area.  
 
 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION

Land use scenario Description 
Commercial 
Acacia Plantation 
The Forestry Laws No. 41/1999, 19/2004 and later by Ministry of Forestry 
Decree No. 31/2014 legally allow for the development of commercial Acacia 
plantations. 
Commercial Oil 
Palm Plantation 
The legal designation of the project area is production forest or Hutan Produksi 
(HP). Legally, a commercial oil palm plantation cannot be established in an area 
designated as production forest. A commercial palm oil plantation can only be 
established legally by first removing the area from the forest estate as per 
Government Decree PP No. 60/2012. Further, only forest areas formally 
designated as Conversion Production Forest (Hutan Produksi Konversi or HPK) 
can be removed from the forestry estate. The project area does not include any 
forest areas designated as HPK, as a result the scenario of commercial 
conversion to oil palm is not considered a legally viable scenario. 
Forest with 
commercial 
logging 
As per Forestry Laws No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004, and Ministry of Forestry 
Decree No. 31/2014, commercial logging is a legally permissible land-use.  
Unexploited Forest 
The area could remain as unexploited forest if it does not become subject to any 
license application for logging or conversion to industrial agriculture. Secondly, 
the site could be subject to an application for management as an ecosystem 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
99 
Land use scenario Description 
restoration concession, a form of logging concession permissible on production 
forest land as regulated and later by Ministry of Forestry Decree No. 31/2014 or 
a carbon storage and sequestration license. 
Protected Forest 
Land designated as production forest can be legally converted to some form of 
protection or conservation forest. However, this re-designation takes 
considerable time, political support at many levels, and is a long process. 
Smallholder 
agriculture 
As per Forestry Laws No. 41/1999 and No. 19/2004, and Ministry of Forestry 
Decree No. 31/2014, smallholder agriculture is not a legally permissible land-use 
on areas designated as production forest.  
However, unexploited forest land which is not actively managed, is often targeted 
by smallholders via a number of quasi-official routes. 
Commercial Coal 
Mining and Natural 
Gas Extraction 
Commercial coal mining is only legally permissible within the project area with an 
appropriate license. The granting of these licenses is governed by laws that 
highly restrict the extent of an area that can be mined and outline the 
compensation levels which must be paid to existing concession holders. 
Currently there are no active or exploration mining concessions overlapping with 
the project area.  
 
As a result of the legal analysis, commercial oil palm plantations are not considered a credible alternative 
land-use  scenario  in  the  project  area  as  it  is  not  legally  permissible.  While  smallholder  agriculture  is  not  
legally  permissible  in  the  project  area,  it  frequently  occurs  across  Indonesia  and  therefore  merits  further  
consideration as part of the barrier analysis.  
Step 2: Barrier Analysis 
Step 2a. Identification of barriers that would prevent the implementation of at least one alternative land-use 
scenarios 
In  this  section,  we  the  assess  the  remaining  barriers  of  the  remaining  six  credible  land-use  scenarios  in  
order to determine the most likely land-use scenarios. The results of this analysis are shown in Table 23. 
Table 23. Identification of barriers that would prevent the implementation of each scenario (Outcome of Step 
2a). 
Land use scenario Description 
Commercial Acacia 
plantation 
There are no barriers to the establishment of a commercial Acacia 
plantation. At the start of the project and until now, the project area has a 
land designation of production forest which allows it to be converted to a 
commercial Acacia plantation. 
Forest with commercial 
logging 
The primary barrier to this land-use is the lack of commercial-sized timber 
due to the majority of the site having been logged between 1970-2000.  
The business model around commercial logging has also changed. When 
commercial logging first started in the 60’s and 70’s, a combination of loose 
regulations on tax collecting, cheap labor, and minimal labor laws allowed 
companies to operate profitably on marginal forest sites. The market was 
also conducive as timber prices were high and export markets were very 
open. This land-use is considerably more difficult as high value export 
markets are now difficult to access without following strict certification 
standards and this would be very difficult to obtain for production coming 
from peatlands. 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
100 
Land use scenario Description 
Unexploited Forest 
There are economic and institutional barriers to this land-use. Since the land 
is legally designated for production activities, if it stayed without a 
commercial license, it would not be in line with documented government 
economic development plans. Politically this would not be popular as the 
area would not generate tax revenues and economic benefits for the state 
and local communities. Historical analysis across Indonesia shows that 
unprotected forest does not remain intact for an extended period of time.  
Protected Forest The creation of a protected forest with the project area would not have 
political support locally as this would require the local government to use 
existing state budgets to cover the costs of forest protection for the area. The 
government would prefer a land-use that would generate tax revenue and 
local jobs. 
Smallholder agriculture 
In general, peat soils are not suitable for growing crops and is therefore a 
major barrier to smallholder agriculture which is further supported by the low 
levels of smallholder agriculture within peat areas in the region and across 
Indonesia.
5,40
 Second, the project area was prone to repeated fires and 
farmers are reluctant to invest in areas where the farms will be destroyed on 
a consistent basis. The expansion of smallholder agriculture in areas 
designated as production forest is rare and occurs primarily when there is no 
active management license for the area. Since there are no barriers for the 
development of commercial forest plantations in the project area and the 
likelihood of the area remaining an unexploited forest for a long period of 
time is low, smallholder agriculture expansion in the project area is not 
considered credible.  
Mining and Natural 
Gas Extraction 
The main barrier to the expansion of commercial coal mining in the project 
area is the lack of suitable coal deposits and the peat overburden. This is 
confirmed by absence of any commercial mining exploitation permits for the 
area (Map 24). 
Natural gas extraction is taking place in close proximity to the project area by 
Conoco Philips. However, the region was previously surveyed and there are 
no gas fields identified by the government that are overlapping with the 
project area.

Map 24. Regional mining concessions. 
 
Step 2b. Elimination of land-use scenarios that are prevented by the identified barriers 
In conclusion, there are significant barriers that prevent the realization of all the previously listed scenarios 
leaving a single credible land-use scenario: industrial Acacia plantation. Thus, this alternative was identified 
as the baseline scenario. 
Sub-step 2c. Determination of baseline scenario. 
Step 3: Investment analysis 
Because a single credible land-use scenario was identified through the analytical steps above, a detailed 
investment  analysis  is  not  required  by  the  A/R  CDM  additionality  tool.
30
  However,  a  GGGI  study  on  
peatlands supported the identification of Industrial Acacia plantation as being the most profitable and likely 
land-use on areas legally classified as production forest.  
Step 4: Common practice analysis  
It  is  not  a  common  practice  for  the  Indonesian  government  to  have  intact  forest  on  land  designated  for  
production that is not being commercially exploited and generating tax revenues. However, there are some 
examples of this that tend to be small donor-backed conservation projects or where large corporates in the 
extractive industries are paying restoration costs of sites as reparations for areas damaged as part of their 
operations in other locations. These are not widespread and are typically very small in size. 
Conclusion 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
102 
The project is considered additional, with the most likely and plausible business-as-usual scenario being 
conversion to industrial Acacia plantation.  
3.1.6 Methodology Deviations 
The VM0007 methodology underwent a slight deviation for our project. The modifications were only related 
to  the  selection  of  proxy  areas  for  the  leakage  analysis  and  did  not  relate  to  any  other  parts  of  the  
methodology. The deviation did not negatively impact the conservativeness of the quantification of GHG 
emission reductions or removals.  
Module  BL-ARR  and  Methodology  extension  AR-ACM0003,  do  not  include  specific  requirements  for  the  
justification of the plantation establishment rate. To justify our conservative baseline conversion/plantation 
establishment rate (which is not deforestation) and to calculate leakage with a baseline deforestation rate, 
we  followed  Module  BL-PL.  The  conservative  approaches  of  BL-PL  provided  an  analytical  basis  for  the  
plantation establishment rate. Although this analysis was not needed for our baseline rate of conversion, 
we employed this approach to give our baseline rate more validity. A slight deviation from the requirements 
of BL-PL took place when justifying deforestation rate used for the subsequent LK-ASP. 
For the percentage forest cover used in similarity criteria for the proxy area analysis, we chose to use our 
site condition in 2013 (the year of license granted) as the reference for comparison for two reasons. First, 
by  using  the  project  area  forest  cover  in  2015  (as  the  project  starting  date  is  01/01/2016),  we  could  not  
identify the minimum number of required concessions (n = 6) in Indonesia within the last ten years that met 
the forest cover along with the other similarity criteria. This is because we would have compared the project 
area after a catastrophic fire event in 2015 to concessions prior to the fire event. The 2015 event made the 
project area a unique situation and therefore impossible to find enough sites with similar (low) forest cover. 
Second, by choosing 2013 as the year for comparison we selected a year in which forest cover was higher, 
thus translating to proxy concessions that had higher initial forest cover (i.e. in the year of license granted). 
This was a conservative approach as a higher forest cover meant that areas were more difficult to convert 
and therefore experienced a lower rate of conversion.

3.2.1 Baseline Emissions 
In this section we describe baseline emissions derived from the VCS methodology VM0007 (version 1.6) 
REDD+ MF and its modules BL-PL, BL-ARR, AR-ACM0003, BL-PEAT, and E-BPB. 
3.2.1.1 General assumptions 
We used analyses of the most likely baseline scenario, described in Sub-section 3.1.4, to assess baseline 
emissions as well as the resulting changes on carbon stocks. 
These emissions sources included: 
1.    Changes in aboveground biomass stocks (characterized by conversion to plantations); 
2.    Peat microbial decomposition; 
3.    Peat burning; and  
4.    Dissolved organic carbon from bodies of water. 
All of the assumptions and omissions of emissions that we describe here are conservative efforts that result 
in lower emissions to maintain validity of our calculations and estimates of a baseline scenario.  
We assumed that no other events of rewetting would occur in the baseline scenario. Examples of natural 
events that we excluded in our analysis may include canal collapses, progressive subsidence that could 
lead  to  changes  in  relative  water  table  depths,  or  reduced  CO
2
  emission  rates  with  increasingly  thinner  
aerobic soil layers. 
We  also  assumed  that  regular  canal  maintenance  activities  typically  carried  out  for  drainage  and  
transportation would stay in place.  
As information on the volume and frequency of dredging is unavailable (for example if dredging activities 
caused  the  peat  to  be  exposed  to  aerobic  decomposition),  we  omitted  these  emissions.  This  is  a  
conservative   omission   as   water   body   emissions   are   lower   than   those   resulting   from   microbial   
decomposition in peat, and therefore results in lower emission estimates in the water body stratum than 
strata at the same location in the project scenario.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
105 
In the baseline scenario, we calculated the annual emissions for carbon dioxide (CO
2
) and methane (CH
4
) 
as these emissions are impacted directly by changes in land cover classes and drainage status over time. 
However, we conservatively omitted nitrous oxide (N
2
O) and GHG emissions derived from biomass burning.  
3.2.1.2 Proxy area analysis 
Module  BL-ARR  and  Methodology  extension  AR-ACM0003,  do  not  include  specific  requirements  for  the 
justification of the plantation establishment rate. To justify our conservative baseline conversion/plantation 
establishment rate (which is not deforestation) and to calculate leakage with a baseline deforestation rate, 
we   followed   Module   BL-PL.   We   do   not   have   a   verifiable   plan   at   the   project   area   for   the   
conversion/deforestation rate. Therefore, per BL-PL requirements, we selected a minimum of six areas to 
determine the baseline rate of conversion/deforestation. These were further used to demonstrate the risk 
of abandonment. 
In this section we describe the analytical processes to determine the conservative baseline rate of plantation 
establishment as per Module BL-PL which was used subsequently in LK-ASP.  
3.2.1.2.1 Data collection and compilation 
We  selected  suitable  proxy  areas  as  reference  regions  through  an  iterative  process  of  data  collection,  
compilation, review and processing of global or national-level datasets. Our description of the globally and 
nationally available datasets used for performing iterative analyses to select appropriate proxy areas, as 
well as detailed methodology and justification is available in Annex 9.  
3.2.1.2.2 Similarity analysis to select proxy areas 
According to the methodology, all proxy areas must meet the following criteria: 
1.    Land conversion practices shall be the same as those used by the baseline agent or class of agent; 
2.    The  post-deforestation  land-use  shall  be  the  same  in  the  reference  regions  as  expected  in  the  
project area under business as usual; 
3.    The reference regions shall have the same management and land-use rights type as the proposed 
project area under business as usual; 
4.    If suitable sites exist, they shall be in the immediate area of the project; if an insufficient number of 
sites  exists  in  the  immediate  area  of  the  project,  sites  shall  be  identified  elsewhere  in  the  same  
country as the project; if an insufficient number of sites exists in the country, sites shall be identified 
in neighboring countries; 
5.    Agents  of  deforestation  in  reference  regions  must  have  deforested  their  land  under  the  same  
criteria that the project lands must follow (legally permissible and suitable for conversion); 
6.    Deforestation in the reference region shall have occurred within the 10 years prior to the baseline 
period; and 
The three following conditions shall be met: 
1.    The forest types surrounding the reference region or in the reference region prior to deforestation 
shall be in the same proportion as in the project area (± 20%); 
2.    Soil types that are suitable for the land-use practice used by the agent of deforestation in the project 
area must be present in the reference region in the same proportion as the project area (± 20%). 
The ratio of slope classes “gentle” (slope < 15%) to “steep” (slope ≥ 15%) in the reference regions 
shall be (± 20%) the same of the ratio in the project area; and 
3.    Elevation classes (500-m classes) in the reference region shall be in the same proportion as in the 
project area (± 20%). 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
106 
We have described and justified a slight deviation from Module BL-PL in Section 3.1.6. Requirements for 
the reference regions had to meet the following criteria in Table 25 below. 
  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3

Table 25. Reference region selection criteria.  
Project area at project starting date Reference region requirement
