The selection took place in a step-wise elimination approach by examining one item on the criteria list at a 
time. After the final step of elimination from the land-use, management practice, elevation, slope, and soil 
(peat) criteria, 28 shortlisted reference regions remained for the forest cover similarity analysis. 
Because the MoE&F dataset was not consistently available on an annual basis (available for 2006, 2009, 
2011, 2012, 2013 and 2015) during the ten years prior to our project starting date, not all the potential proxy 
area license years were covered by the years of MoE&F data. Ten of the 28 shortlisted concessions had 
their license start in 2007. Since the MoE&F data did not cover this year, we chose to use the MoE&F forest 
cover  in  2006  as  a  proxy  of  2007  with  two  assumptions  met.  First,  we  assumed  that  large-scale,  major  
planned conversion/deforestation would only begin after the licenses were issued. Second, by only using a 
single  and  credible  data  source,  we  avoided  uncertainty  resulting  from  mixing  datasets  derived  using  
different methods.  
We  verified  the  first  assumption  by  using  two  independent  satellite-derived  datasets  produced  by  the  
Indonesian government and by the project technical team, namely the forest cover (Indonesian National 
Carbon Accounting System or INCAS) and pixel-based change detection analysis of Landsat-8 NDVI for 
2006-2007. INCAS and MoE&F datasets had a mean 78% agreement between forest/non-forest areas.
42
 
Because  the  INCAS  dataset  used  the  same  satellite  platform  (Landsat)  as  the  MoE&F  and  the  data  
development  involved  standards,  inputs  and  staff  from  the  MoE&F,
43
 using  INCAS  data  as  a  verification  
reference was viable. We first compared the INCAS data of 2006-2007 with the MoE&F 2006 percentage 
forest  cover  and  eliminated  one  site  that  showed  very  high  difference  between  the  two  data  sources  
(MoE&F: 5.8%; INCAS: 78.8% in 2006 and 81.6% in 2007) because this discrepancy indicated significant 
difference in definition of forest or analysis bias/errors. We also confirmed by using the INCAS dataset to 
examine if there was any major forest cover difference between 2006 and 2007. We eliminated another site 
that had moderate difference between the two years (INCAS: 90.4% in 2006 and 81.5% in 2007) because 
this indicated potential industrial deforestation/conversion between 2006-2007. 
Next,  for  the  remaining  concessions  that  had  >  1%  of  forest  cover  difference  in  INCAS  2006-2007,  we  
conducted  change  detection  analysis  by  comparing  the  difference  in  vegetation  reflectance  (i.e.  NDVI  
value) between 2006 vs. 2007 using the ENVI software version 5.3. Differencing NDVI (dNDVI) has been 
a well-known pre-classification change detection method to identify discernible shift in vegetation or land-
use  types.
44
  We  produced  a  dNDVI  map  in  the  cloud-free  areas  of  each  2006-2007  pair,  smoothed  the  
output  map  with  a  majority  filter  algorithm,  and  categorized  the  standard  deviation  of  dNDVI.  The  
categorization  identified  multiple  spatial  clusters  that  represented  several  different  types  of  vegetation  
change: 1) industrial deforestation/conversion; 2) crop/plantation to harvest; 3) plantation rotation cycle; 4) 
smallholder  activities;  and  5)  no  change  (i.e.  no  loss  or  gain  in  dNDVI).  We  cross-checked  between  the  
dNDVI category map and Landsat-8 surface reflectance bands (in composite band combination of 6-5-4) 
to assign the spatial clusters into a dNDVI category by visually inspecting the imagery texture, color, pattern, 
patch size, patch shape, land features and adjacent patch types. 
When  the  visual  interpretation  showed  possibly  different  conclusion  of  forest  cover  or  land-use  change  
among dNDVI, Landsat-8 surface band composite, INCAS and MoE&F datasets, we compared them with 
two  more  global  satellite-derived  datasets  in  2007:  1)  vegetation  loss  (Terra-i);  or  2)  tree  cover  loss  
(University of Maryland). We eliminated one more site that had almost 100% forest cover in the INCAS and 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
108 
MoE&F  datasets  but  showed  moderate  level  of  tree  cover  loss  in  the  University  of  Maryland  data  and  
industrial-level deforestation/conversion in the dNDVI analysis. 
The summary comparison of sites analyzed in this process can be found in Annex 9. From this iterative 
process we confirmed that the remaining seven sites did not show major planned deforestation/ conversion 
activities from 2006-2007. We concluded that their forest cover shown in the MoE&F 2006 could serve as 
a good proxy to represent their 2007 cover. We then proceeded forest cover similarity analysis using these 
seven sites along with other shortlisted sites licensed in different years. 
3.2.1.2.3 Conversion/deforestation rate calculation 
After  the  final  elimination  step  using  the  MoE&F  forest  cover  data,  seven  proxy  areas  remained.  One  
concession  only  had  1.31%  average  annual  forest  loss  from  2009  (licensed  year)  to  2015  (prior  to  our  
project start date on 01/01/2016), very different from the other six sites. According to our visual inspection 
by  examining  Landsat  medium-resolution  imagery  in  the  licensed  year  and  2015,  this  site  did  not  show  
prominent expansion of large-scale conversion/deforestation and development activities compared to the 
other sites. Irregular, patchy and small-size deforestation/conversion indicated smallholder activities and/or 
natural  hazards/disasters.  It  is  likely  that  1.31%  mean  annual  forest  loss  might  not  result  from  industrial  
concession operations, but could be a result of smallholder practices, forest succession from hazards, or 
natural disasters. The land conversion practice and post-deforestation land-use of this site would not meet 
the similarity criteria when compared to the project area baseline scenario. 
The   visual   inspection   also   confirmed   that   the   other   six   sites   showed   signs   of   continuing   
conversion/deforestation for plantations, thereby not subject to risk of abandonment. Therefore, considering 
their similarity to the project area’s baseline scenario, we selected these six remaining sites as the proxy 
area for calculating conversion/deforestation rate. 
The summary comparison of sites analyzed in this visual inspection process can be found in Annex 9. We 
summarize the six final reference regions below in Table 26 and Map 25.

We estimated the average annual conversion/deforestation rate for these six proxy areas to be 7.85%. Our

on  document  research  and  concession  manager  interviews  indicated  a  typical  conversion/deforestation  
rate of 12-13%. Since we took conservative approaches analytically during the similarity analysis to set an 
estimated rate lower than known practices, the baseline rate of conversion/deforestation uncertainty was 
set to zero. 
Likelihood of deforestation and risk of abandonment 
Because  HTI  concessions  are  designated  in  zoning  for  deforestation  and  are  not  under  any  particular  
government control while licensed, we assumed the likelihood of deforestation (L-D
i
) to be 100%. 
We assessed the risk of abandonment using the six proxy areas selected above for calculating the average 
annual conversion/deforestation rate. After confirming the criteria in the proxy area analysis, we accessed 
and visually interpreted Landsat satellite images to determine if any of them experienced abandonment for 
forest regrowth. All proxy areas showed clear signs of continued conversion/deforestation and plantation 
activities for 2006-2015, therefore the BL-PL Module is applicable.

3.2.1.3 Projection of conversion under baseline scenario 
We  allocated  the  project  area  spatially  to  produce  a  map  based  on  the  calculation  of  total  annual  area  
converted in the baseline (퐴퐴퐴퐴
푝푝푝푝푝푝 푖푖푖푖 푝푝푝푝.푖푖,푝푝
). We stratified the project area into five strata, determined using an 
iterative,  spatially  explicit  delineation  process  with  four  land-use  types,  two  drainage  statuses,  and  one  
water  body  class.  A  baseline  scenario  map  is  shown  in  Map 26.  The  spatial  allocation  consisted  of  the  
following steps:  
1.    We  defined  the  maximum  area  allowed  for  development.  According  to  MoE&F,
45,46
  10%  of  the  
project area must be set aside for conservation, while the remaining 90% could be developed. 
2.    Of 90% of the project area available for development, maximum 5% could be designated as ground 
facilities  and  infrastructure  (camps,  guard  posts,  access  roads,  canals,  and  log  collection  ports),  
based on common practices obtained through interviews. 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
112 
3.    Delineation of water bodies: 
a.    Delineation of water bodies present at the project start date (i.e. natural river such as Buring 
river). The natural water body stratum does not change for the entire project period. 
b.    Delineation of secondary (≤ 7 m) and tertiary (≤ 2 m) canals that existed prior to the project 
start  date.  These  canals  are  then  converted  into  1)  baseline  primary  canals,  2)  partly  
baseline secondary canals, 3) partly baseline tertiary canals based on their locations in the 
zoning design and the conversion year, or 4) Acacia planting zone. The widths of canals 
were based on observations from the field, and Google Earth high-resolution imagery of 
the project area. 
c.    Delineation  of  baseline  primary  canals  (12  m)  converted  in  the  first  year  of  baseline  
scenario, widened from the existing secondary canals and aimed to serve for water surface 
control, log, seedling, and workers transportation.   
d.    Delineation  of  7-m  wide  baseline  secondary  canals  that  enclosed  planting  blocks.  We  
divided  the  secondary  canal  development  into  the  first  four  years  of  conversion,  
corresponding to review of common practices.  
e.    Delineation  of  2-m  wide  baseline  tertiary  canals  that  enclosed  one  planting  block.  The  
tertiary  canals  were  assumed  to  be  developed  in  the  same  year  as  the  corresponding  
planting zone’s conversion. 
4.    Delineation of drained/undrained areas: 
a.    Studies   showed   that   close   proximity   to   canals   influenced   water   table   level,   peat   
subsidence,  burned  area  depth  and  probability  of  high  frequency  fire  events.  Observed  
impact  of  canal  proximity  varied  across  different  regions,  from  300  m  (size  class  of  
secondary canals),
47
 600 m (size class of primary canals),
48
 up to 1,300 m.
48–50
 Based on 
the literature, we employed a conservative approach to assume that areas within a 300-m 
distance  from  secondary  canals  and  a  600-m  distance  from  primary  canals  would  be  
drained. Lacking literature that studied the distance impact of canals in the size class of 
tertiary canals, we took a conservative approach to prorate an assumed distance (150 m) 
within which drainage would occur.    
b.    We  created  a  600-m,  300-m,  and  150-m  buffer  around  both  sides  of  the  primary,  
secondary, and tertiary canals, respectively, to account for peatland drained at the project 
start date and in the subsequent years of baseline scenario. 
c.    The distinction between drained and undrained areas was taken into account for emission 
characteristics and calculations. 
5.    Delineation of conservation area: 
a.    Delineation of intact dense forest and nearby closed or open canopy tree/shrub mix areas 
and  the  surroundings  at  the  project  start  date.  Summary  and  details  of  the  land  cover  
classification are available respectively in Section 3.1.3.1.1 and Annex 3. 
b.    A 25-m buffer zone on both sides of the natural water bodies was added to the conservation 
area according to MoE&F.
51
 
6.    Delineation of ground facilities: 
a.    We created a 100-m buffer around camps and guard posts. 
7.    Delineation of planting zones: 
a.    Conversions to canals, ground facilities and planting zones followed an annual conversion 
rate of 7.85% (from the proxy area analysis in Section 3.2.1.2). The remaining project area 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
113 
after  deducting  conservation  area,  water  bodies  and  ground  facilities  was  available  for  
planting  zones.  We  delineated  a  total  of  13  planting  zones,  including  a  few  zones  to  
accommodate conversions to ground facilities and secondary canal development. 
b.    Each  planting  zone  consisted  of  multiple  150  x  500  m  (75  ha)  planting  blocks  based  on  
common practices observed in the neighboring concession planting blocks using Google 
Earth high-resolution imagery.  
8.    Annual land-use change: 
a.    Ground facilities (camps, posts, log ponds) were constructed on year 1, based on known 
common practices. 
b.    Baseline  primary  canals  were  developed  on  year  1,  expanded  from  existing  secondary  
canals prior to the project start. 
c.    Baseline secondary canals were developed in four stages (year 1 to 4). 
d.    Baseline tertiary canals were developed in the year of the planting zone development at 
which the tertiary canals were located. 
e.    Existing secondary and tertiary canals that overlapped with a planting zone were converted 
to planting  zone  at  the  year  of  zone  development.  Canals  that  overlapped  with  baseline  
secondary  canals  were  converted  to  secondary  canals  at  the  year  of  secondary  canal  
development.  Canals  that  overlapped  with  baseline  tertiary  canals  were  converted  to  
tertiary  canals  at  the  year  of  corresponding  zone  development.  Canals  inside  the  
conservation area remained unchanged for the project period. 
f. One planting zone was converted and planted immediately per year. 
g.    Each planting zone was harvested and immediately replanted every four years, following 
the Acacia planting cycles known from common practices. For example, year 1 conversion 
was also set as the same year to plant and in year 5 the harvest was also set for replanting 
immediately. 
The baseline strata of emission characteristics are shown in the baseline scenario designation map (Map 
26) by incorporating (1) spatial coverage of land-use and drainage status; and (2) year of land-use and 
drainage status change. 
 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
114 
Map 26. Baseline scenario map of the project area.

3.2.1.5 Baseline emissions from microbial decomposition of peat, peat burning, and water bodies 
3.2.1.5.1 Spatial and temporal variability 
We quantified GHG emissions from microbial decomposition of peat, peat burning, and water bodies across 
spatial and temporal scales. Each baseline stratum included a small unit of land parcels with a combination 
of variables (described in Table 29).  
We conducted calculations yearly to determine annual strata changes, variability of GHG emission factors, 
and any restrictions to GHG emissions (Table 29). We accounted for spatial and temporal scales with our 
GHG emission factors.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
117 
Table 29. Variables used for quantifying baseline GHG emissions from microbial decomposition of peat, peat 
burnings and dissolved organic carbon from water bodies. 
Variables Description 
(A) Spatial Variables     
(A1) Soil Type  Distinction between peat or non-peat. This was used to 
exclude all non-peat parcels from GHG calculation 
(99.99% of the project area includes peat with depth ≥ 
50cm). 
(A2) Initial peat thickness available for 
microbial decomposition and burnings  
Derived from DTM, DEL and Peat Thickness maps as 
described in Sub-subsection 3.1.3.1.4. These maps 
were used to determine the initial condition for 
subsequent calculations of the remaining peat layer 
available for microbial decomposition and burning.  
(A3) Initial stratum  
Stratum of the corresponding parcel at the project start 
date (as derived in Annex 10) before conversion into the 
baseline stratum takes effect. This was used to 
determine the correct emission factor for the 
corresponding parcel for the duration before B1 and B2 
(see below) take effect.  
(A4) Peat burning tag  
This was used to identify whether the corresponding 
parcel was marked as a possible area for peat burning 
and the frequency of total years of historical and 
simulated burns. 
(B) Temporal Variables     
(B1) Year of drainage  
Determines the onset of conversion from initial stratum 
to a drained stratum and set all the drainage related 
parameters/variables accordingly, such as initial 
consolidations, bulk density changes, etc. This does not 
take effect if the initial stratum of the parcel was already 
a drained stratum. Together with B2 this was used to 
determine the correct emission factor for the 
corresponding parcel.  
(B2) Year of conversion/planting of the 
baseline land cover  
Determines the onset of conversion of initial stratum to 
converted/planted stratum. Together with B1 this was 
used to determine the correct emission factor for the 
corresponding parcel.  
(B3) PDT  The PDT defines the period of time that it takes to 
deplete the remaining peat layer by microbial 
decomposition and burning. Once the PDT is reached in 
a given stratum all GHG emissions in that stratum are 
set to zero.  
(B4) Year tag for burning  
Determines whether the corresponding parcel was 
marked to catch peat burning for the corresponding year 
and counting the number of burn scars (and any 
repetitions) of the parcel since year 1. This was used to 
set the correct burn scar depth and other related

CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
118 
Variables Description 
burning parameters for the corresponding parcel 
accordingly.  
(B5) Burning restriction  
If the corresponding parcel was marked for burning in 
the corresponding year (as being checked in B4), this 
restriction further checked whether GHG emissions from 
burning would still be possible based on variables: B1 
(Year of drainage), B2 (Year of conversion/planting) and 
B3 (Remaining peat thickness available for microbial 
decomposition and burning).

3.2.1.5.3 Subsidence related to initial compression, microbial decomposition, and peat burning 
We assume that the initial peat thickness in the baseline scenario is equal to the initial peat thickness as 
mapped at the project starting date (Sub-subsection 3.1.3.1.4). 
We calculated peat loss due to initial compression and microbial decomposition by using the stratification 
of the project area on initial drainage, the year of conversion, and specific subsidence rates that took into 
account the compaction after initial drainage (See Annex 11 on Emission Factors). For the peat loss due 
to burning, we first assessed the number of times the individual pixels of the fire simulation burnt in the 10-
year period before project start date and subsequently applied the associated burn scar depth (Annex 11 
on Emission Factors). We quantified the annual peat loss rates in the baseline scenario as the sum of the 
burn scar depth (in area where peat burning was simulated to take place) and the subsidence rate, using 
the equation (14): 
푅푅푅푅푡푡퐷퐷

=퐷퐷
푝푝푝푝푝푝푝푝푝푝푝푝   푙푙푖푖−퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵퐵,푖푖,푝푝

3.2.1.5.5 Emissions from peat burning 
According to Module E-BPB, GHG emissions from biomass burning can result from: 
1.    Conversion of forest land to non-forest land using fire; 
2.    Periodic burning of grassland or agricultural land after deforestation; 
3.    Controlled burning in forest land remaining forest land; 
4.    Uncontrolled fire in drained peat swamp forest; and  
5.    Uncontrolled peat burning in (abandoned) drained peat sites. 
We  assumed  that  there  was  no  controlled  peat  burning  during  plantation  activities,  as  burning  to  clear  
forests on Acacia plantations is illegal. Therefore, we only accounted for emissions from unintentional or 
uncontrolled burnings in the baseline scenario. In addition, we omitted AGB lost by combustion. 
To  quantify  GHG  emissions  from  uncontrolled  peat  burnings,  we  followed  procedures  from  the  VCS  
methodology VM0007 Module E-BPB with the following equation (19):

Emissions  from  peat  burning  in  the  baseline  are  derived  from  the  mass  of  peat  lost  by  combustion  and  
emission factors from scientific literature (see Annex 11 for the default values used for the calculations of 
baseline carbon losses and emissions from burning). 
For fire emissions, we assumed that the likelihood of random, uncontrolled or repeated peatland burnings 
would  be  higher  in  areas  with  greater  probability  of  fire  risks.  To  identify  areas  of  greater  fire  risk  in  the  
baseline  of  the  project  area,  we  performed  spatially  explicit  habitat  suitability  modeling  to  examine  the  
distribution of fire probability. 
Previous fire probability models showed that in general, higher fire likelihood is related to predictor variables 
of  human  development  activities,  climate,  topography,  vegetation  and  ignition.  In  particular,  human  
population,  distance  to  roads,  bioclimate,  annual  precipitation,  elevation,  slope,  and  land  cover  types  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
127 
associated with agricultural activities had the greatest model contribution and are the strongest predictors 
of  fire  occurrence  probability.  Many  models  of  fire  risk  used  historical  fire  detection  data  derived  from  
satellite  imagery  products  to  calibrate.  For  our  study  purpose  of  aligning  period  of  fire  risk  probability  
modeling and burn scar area calculation (for estimating average burn area per year), we decided to use 
remote sensing data of 10 years prior to project start (2006-2015). 
The initial input data included point locations and 31 environmental variables, including NASA MODIS and 
VIIRS 2006-2015 hotspots, WorldClim bioclimatic variables, NASA SRTM elevation and derived slope and 
aspect, LandScan population count, distance to road, distance to river, and land cover change produced 
by the Ministry of Environment and Forestry for 2006-2015. Land cover type, precipitation and seasonality, 
population, and distance to roads were the most important contributing variables to the final model. 
We  document  detailed  fire  risk  probability  data  compilation,  model  inputs  preparation,  iterative  modeling  
process, model evaluation and final model result in Annex 12. 
Map 28. Fire occurrence suitability that predicted risk probability.  
 
We  then  calculated  annual  percentage  of  burn  rate  in  the  study  area  for  2006-2015,  by  using  historical  
MODIS  and  VIIRS  hotspot  data.  We  assumed  each  hotspot  occurrence  with  a  conservative  burn  area  
estimate or estimate coefficient based on literature values. The calculation produced the average 6.12% of 
burnt area (1,402.85 ha) per year. This value was used as a parameter in estimating the annual area burnt 
threshold in the baseline scenario. 
We assumed that uncontrolled burnings in peatlands repeat randomly on places that are ‘high risk’ areas. 
To determine where the ‘high risk areas’ are in the baseline of the project area, we used the fire occurrence 
risk  probability  model  as  well  as  the  average  annual  burnt  area  estimate  above,  to  simulate  the  spatial  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
128 
position of burning for 100 years. This simulation did not show the “actual area burnt” in the baseline 
scenario, rather showing possible locations where peat burning can be expected to occur randomly. 
The  location  of  possible  burnt  area  in  each  year  of  the  baseline  scenario  was  simulated  as  using  the  
probable areas of fire risk from the model as weight, by randomly selecting parcels annually over 100 years 
in  such  a  way  that  the  annual  average  area  of  the  selected  parcels  approximately  equaled  (but  did  not  
exceed) the area of average annual burnt. Parcels that had higher fire risk probability had greater chance 
to be randomly selected in the simulation model. 
We examined the hotspot history based on MODIS and VIIRS data from year 2006-2015 to document the 
number of times when a selected parcel was burnt. We then examined the 100-year fire simulation of burn 
frequency  and  combined  with  the  2006-2015  data  to  record  the  overall  history/projection  of  burning.  If  a  
parcel had no hotspot record in 2006-2015 and was selected randomly in the first time of simulation, the 
parcel was marked as “catching the 1st burning”. If it had a hotspot record and was randomly selected once 
over the 100 years, it was marked as “catching the 2nd burning”, and so forth. 
The  detailed  methodologies,  justifications  and  calculations  of  annual  burnt  area  and  100-year  fire  
simulations can be found in Annex 13. 
The peat burning baseline will be re-assessed every 10 years based on observations of burning frequency 
and extent in reference region and/or based on the latest scientific findings of ‘repeated burnings’ pattern.  
The total GHG emission from uncontrolled peat burning in the baseline scenario per stratum are shown in 
Table 33 below, and annual emissions and total emission per stratum are shown in Table 34.

3.2.1.5.6 Emissions from water bodies  
The only type of open water bodies present in the baseline scenario are river and drainage canals, as we 
assumed that the baseline agent would not create ponds and/or lakes. The area of river and canals in the 
baseline  scenario  was  determined  based  on  common  practice  observed  in  the  adjacent  Acacia  
concessions, as well as direct measurements from high-resolution satellite imagery. The water bodies were 
temporally and spatially stratified, as set out in Section 3.2.1.4. In short, primary canals were set as existing 
canals before new conversion, whereas secondary and tertiary canals were to be constructed when new 
canals appeared starting in Year 1 and when converting an area into a planting zone, respectively. IPCC 
does not provide default emission factors for CO
2
 and CH
4
 emissions from water bodies, thus we used the 
default values for dissolved organic carbon (∆ DOC) (for detail see Annex 11). 
The only natural river in the project area is the Buring river, hence in the baseline this is the only WB area, 
on which the default value for natural water bodies is applied (0.57 t C ha
-1
 yr
-1
). For all other water bodies, 
i.e. canals, the default values for drained peatland (0.82 t C ha
-1
 yr
-1
) was applied.  
We used the approach as set out in the VCS methodology VM0007 Module BL-PEAT (equation 21), and

3.2.1.6 Baseline emissions from ARR activities 
Under the baseline scenario, the area designated for Acacia plantation, would sequester carbon in trees 
through growth (emission removals) and release it again after harvesting. We accounted for the growth of 
Acacia as baseline ARR activities. As detailed in Sub-subsection 3.2.1.3 the conversion and thus planting 
of Acacia would be carried out sequentially during the first 13 years of the project, resulting in a total planted 
area of 19,915.65 ha. We assumed a rotation cycle of 4 years. The annual area changes under the baseline 
scenario are detailed in Annex 10, and summarized in Table 36 below.

3.2.1.7 Significant sources of baseline emissions 
We  have  determined  that  all  sources  of  carbon  not  included  in  the  baseline  or  project  scenarios  can  be  
conservatively excluded. Significance tests were not necessary as all of the required pools of carbon and 
other GHG emissions within the SMPP have already been included in our calculations.  
3.2.2 Project Emissions 
To  determine  project  scenario  emissions  and  changes  in  carbon  stocks  within  the  SMPP  from  a  
combination of field-based data, proxy analyses, and default emissions factors from the IPCC.
53
 
We accounted emissions in the project scenario from: 
1.    Aboveground biomass (AGB) stock changes due to ARR activities (from 2019 as this is the first 
year the project will conduct an AGB inventory); 
2.    Peat microbial decomposition; and 
3.    Water table level changes due to WRC activities. 
Our project activities specifically related to climate are described in Section 2.1.11 and include rewetting 
drained peatland areas, conservation of existing natural peat areas, natural regeneration of vegetation, and 
fire control measures with a target of zero burning in the SMPP. 
We have operated consistent fire patrols since the start of the SMPP, and we also have an online satellite-
based  early  warning  system  (EWS)  in  place  to  detect  possible  fires  as  quickly  as  possible.  Our  project  
activities will also consist of fire prevention trainings and as a result of the project there will be a reduction 
of human activity in the project area. If any uncontrolled burning does occur in the SMPP during the project 
period, we have extensive fire monitoring plans in place, and we will adjust our calculations to account for 
these emissions.   
We also plan to construct compacted peat dams over all the existing canals throughout the SMPP area, so 
we assumed there will not be any non-human induced rewetting in the project scenario. 
3.2.2.1 Emission characteristics stratification for WRC activities under the project scenario 
For the project scenario, we stratified the project area based on two land cover classes (forest and non-
forest), two drainage statuses (drained and undrained) and one water body class. through a spatially explicit 
delineation process. A project scenario map is provided in  
Map 29. The mapping process involved the following steps:  
1.    Delineation of water bodies: 
a.    Delineation of natural water bodies present at the project start (i.e. natural river such as 
Buring river). The natural water body stratum does not change for the entire project period.  
b.    Delineation of secondary and tertiary canals that existed prior to the project start and do 
not change for the entire project period. The widths of canals were based on observations 
from the field and Google Earth high-resolution imagery of the project area.  
2.    Delineation of drained/undrained areas: 
a.    Following  the  literature  studies  of  canal  proximity  impact  on  peatland  (as  discussed  in  
Section 3.2.1.3), we employed a conservative approach to assume that areas within a 300-
m  distance  from  secondary  canals  and  150-m  distance  from  tertiary  canals  would  be  
drained.  
b.    We  created  a  300-m  and  150-m  buffer  around  both  sides  of  the  secondary  and  tertiary  
canals,  respectively,  to  account  for  peatland  drained  at  the  project  start  date.  The 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
137 
distinction  between  drained  and  undrained  areas  at  project  start  date  was  taken  into  
account for emission characteristics and calculation. 
3.    Delineation of forested area: 
a.    Delineation  of  dense  forest  at  the  project  start  date  was  based  on  the  land  cover  
classification summarized in Section 3.1.3.1.1 and detailed in Annex 3. 
4.    Annual status change: 
a.    As described in Section 2.1.11 and detailed in Annex 8, the project plans to construct and 
implement  dense  compacted  peat  dams  over  canals  across  the  project  area  in  2019.  
Based  on  the  planned  canal  blocking  locations  recommended  by  EMM and  other  
biophysical  parameters,  we  constructed  hydrological  models  of  water  table  level  at  the  
project start date, and annually until the predicted water table level reached saturation (see 
details in Annex 7). 
For areas delineated as “drained” or “undrained” at project start date (see above), we defined annual 
rewetting  efficacy  by  calculating  the  ratio  of  each  year’s  water  table  level  increase  from  the  initial  water  
table level over the maximum of total water table level increase as a proxy. This ratio served as basis to 
adjust emission factors. The hydrological models estimated that maximum water table level was reached 
in in the fifth year since rewetting. Therefore, we predicted all “drained” areas would become “undrained” 
after  this.  Table 38  and Map 29  below  show  stratification  of  the  project  area  based  on  emission  
characteristics. Table 39 lists dynamics in strata. 
 
Table 38. Stratification of project area based on emission characteristics from peat and water bodies at

Map 30. Strata changes in the project scenario.  
 
We will collect site-specific hydrological monitoring data to predict spatially and temporally explicit estimates 
of water table level and flow under the project scenario. Sufficient direct measurements from the field will 
serve as a proxy to calculate GHG emissions. Rewetting efficacy may change for the strata with the most 
dynamic water table levels (rewetted strata drained at project start date). The hydrological monitoring plan 
is detailed in Annex 7.  
Together, land cover stratification based on updated land cover classification and change detection from 
remote  sensing  imagery  and  site-specific  emission  proxies  will  be  used  to  re-stratify  non-forest,  if  any  
changes occur. Strata with less dynamic water table levels (undrained forest stratum at project start date) 
will  not  be  re-stratified  (unless  significant  changes  in  emission  characteristics  are  observed)  and  GHG  
quantification method remains unchanged. 
3.2.2.2 Emission characteristics stratification for ARR activities under the project scenario 
Although  the  project  activities  will  include  reforestation  (nursery  and  replanting,  see  Section  2.1.11),  for  
delineating  ARR  emission  characteristics  we  only  accounted  for  natural  regeneration  of  vegetation.  The  
eligible  area  for  ARR  crediting  is  all  the  non-forest  areas  where  the  project  activities  would  carry  out  
activities to facilitate natural regeneration of vegetation, including rewetting, fire avoidance, etc. Based on 
the spatial analysis, the area eligible for crediting from ARR activities is 20,601.37 ha, as shown in Section 
3.1.3.1.7. 
3.2.2.3 Emissions from peat and water bodies (WRC) 
The 2016 land cover classification (detailed in Annex 3) and water body data collected by PT GAL served 
as a basis for developing strata for WRC activities (see Table 38).  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
140 
The strata for the project scenario analysis include:  
1.    Drained forested peatland;  
2.    Undrained forested peatland;  
3.    Drained non-forested peatland;  
4.    Undrained non-forested peatland; and  
5.    Water bodies. 
We then quantified the GHG emissions from microbial decomposition of peat and DOC loss through water 
bodies in peatlands on spatial and temporal scales.  
3.2.2.3.1 Spatial and temporal variability 
Each stratum in the project scenario are outlined in Table 38. Temporal variability in project emissions was 
based  on  annual  time  steps.  Table 40  outlines  the  variables  that  determined  strata  changes,  temporal  
variability  of  GHG  emission  factors,  and  restrictions  to GHG  emissions.  We  accounted  for  spatial  and  
temporal scales with our GHG emission factors.

decomposition of peat and dissolved organic carbon from water bodies in peatlands in the project scenario.  
Variables   Description 
 (A) Spatial Variables    
(A1) Soil Type  
Distinction between peat or non-peat. This was used to 
exclude all non-peat parcels from GHG calculation. 99.99% 
of the project area has predicted peat depth ≥ 50 cm. 
(A2) Initial peat thickness available for 
microbial decomposition and burnings

(as derived in Annex 10) before change into rewetted 
stratum takes effect. This was used to determine the 
correct emission factor for the corresponding parcel for the 
duration before B1 and B2 (in this table, below) take effect. 
 (B) Temporal Variables    
(B1) Year of rewetting  
Determines the onset of conversion from initial stratum to 
rewetted stratum and sets the drainage related parameter, 
i.e. emission factor for the corresponding parcel.  
(B2) PDT  
The PDT is the period of time that it takes to deplete the 
remaining peat layer by microbial decomposition. Once the 
PDT is reached in a given stratum all GHG emissions in 
that stratum are set to zero.

3.2.2.3.3 Subsidence related to microbial decomposition of peat

assumed no burning in the project period, and therefore there would not be any peat loss from peat fires.

3.2.2.4.1 Current projections for project emissions at the start of the project  
With  sufficient  long-term,  field-based  measurements  of  peat-related  emissions  not  yet  available  for  
estimating the overall emissions, GHG emission factors provided in Annex 11 were used as a conservative 
approach. The effect of rewetting was taken into account by multiplying the emission factor for each stratum 
by an efficacy factor derived from the annual water table changes predicted by the hydrological models (for 
details  see  Annex 7).  As  stated  earlier,  the  model  predicted  that  complete  rewetting  will  be  achieved  in  
2024, five years after the completed dam construction. We further assumed that once the carbon stock in 
the vegetation is equal to the carbon stock of secondary peat swap forest,
54

3.2.2.4.2 Future approaches for calculating project emissions 
To determine carbon and GHG fluxes from peat microbial decomposition in the project scenario, we will 
take  approaches  with  varying  levels  of  resolution  (IPCC  TIER  1  –  TIER  3).
53
  Throughout  project 
implementation, we will conduct measurements in the field (IPCC TIER 2 and TIER 3) and collect data to 
reduce emission uncertainties and account for spatial and temporal variation in the water tables.
53
 
We  will  develop  site-specific  TIER  3  emission  factors  (in  2020)  and apply  these  to  estimate  project  
emissions. These methods work together to thoroughly reduce uncertainty (see Annex 14).  
We began initial, preliminary water table level monitoring in the project area in 2018 and will expand to the 
whole  project  area  in  2019  following  canal  blocking  implementation  (see  Annex 8).  Soil  subsidence  
measurements  will  start  in  2019.  Monitoring  of  all  variables  will  be  continued  throughout  47-year  project  
duration.

3.2.2.6 Emissions from uncontrolled burning 
We are implementing a variety of project activities to address burning, such as peatland rewetting and best-
practice  fire  management,  with  goals  of  zero  burning,  fire  control,  and  fire  prevention  (Section  2.1.11). 
Therefore, we assumed that uncontrolled burning is absent in the project scenario.  
We have operated consistent fire patrols since the start of the SMPP, and we also have an online satellite-
based early warning system (EWS) in place to detect possible fires as quickly as possible. If uncontrolled 
burning does occur in the SMPP during the project period, we will map the area of the fire scar and the burn 
scar  depth  soon  after  the  burning  event.  We  have  compiled  historical  tracking  of  hotspots  and  direct  
observation data in the project area to determine the repetition of burning in the SMPP. We will quantify 
equivalent GHG emissions from uncontrolled burning and deduct these from our emission reductions.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
150 
In addition, we calculate GHG emissions that result from peat burning using dry mass loss based on burn 
scars, depth, bulk density of peat, combustion factors, and GHG potential of GHG species. We determine 
GHG  emissions  from  biomass  loss  from  burning  using  land  cover  type,  combustion  factors,  and  GHG  
potential of GHG species. Bulk density of peat is assumed constant throughout the project period and was 
found to be relatively homogeneous throughout horizontal and vertical peat soil profiles (Annex 5 on peat 
inventory).  
In  cases  where  there  were  repeated  burns  within  the  SMPP,  scar  depths  of  the  1
st
,  2
nd
,  3
rd
,  4
th
,  and  
subsequent burnings are assumed as 0.17 cm, 0.1 cm, 0.06 cm, and 0.02 cm, respectively. The detailed 
parameters we used to quantify GHG emissions from uncontrolled burning are given in Annex 11.

3.2.3 Leakage 
We determined leakage according to the requirements in Module LK-ASP in the VCS methodology VM0007 
REDD+  MF.  As  expressed  in  Section  3.2.1,  baseline  scenario  activity  in  the  SMPP  includes  planned  
conversion to industrial Acacia plantations and resulting peatland drainage. Our project activities combine 
Avoiding  Planned  Deforestation  (APD),  Reforestation  (ARR),  in  combination  with  Conservation  of  
Undrained and Partially-drained Peatland (CUPP) and Rewetting of Drained Peatland (RDP). 
Accordingly,  we  may  see  potential  sources  of  leakage  emissions  from  displacement  of  planned  
deforestation  activities  and  pre-project  activities  on  non-forest  land.  These  potential  sources  and  the  
corresponding applicable modules for quantifying total leakage emissions are listed in Section 3.1.2 and 
Table 46 below. 
Table 46. Applicability of leakage modules 
Module   Applicability  
Estimation of emissions from activity shifting for 
avoiding planned deforestation and planned 
degradation (LK-ASP)  
Applicable. The project may cause activity 
shifting of avoided planned deforestation.  
Estimation of emissions from activity shifting for 
avoiding unplanned deforestation (LK-ASU) 
Not applicable. The project is not categorized 
as avoiding unplanned deforestation. 
Estimation of emissions from displacement of 
fuelwood extraction (LK-DFW)  
Not applicable. The project is not categorized 
as avoiding unsustainable fuelwood extraction.  
Estimation of emissions from displacement of 
pre-project agricultural activities (LK-ARR)  
Applicable. The project is categorized as 
afforestation, reforestation, and revegetation 
and may cause displacement of pre-project 
agricultural activities.  
Estimation of emissions from market-effects (LK-
ME)  
Not applicable. The project does not reduce 
the production of timber, fuelwood, or 
charcoal.  
Estimation of emissions from ecological leakage 
(LK-ECO)  
Applicable. The project is categorized as WRC 
and may cause ecological leakage 
 
3.2.3.1 Estimation  of  emissions  from  activity  shifting  for  avoiding  planned  deforestation  and  planned  
degradation (LK-ASP) 
As discussed in Section 3.1.4, the most likely agent of deforestation in the baseline scenario is conversion 
to industrial Acacia plantations. Section 5.2 of the LK-ASP Module describes three options for estimating 
emissions associated with changes in land-use activity. We chose to use the most applicable option here, 
Approach  1.  The  steps  below  follow  Section  5.1  of  the  LK-ASP  Module.  For  considering  the  leakage  
potential in peatland areas, we followed the steps in Section 5.3 of the LK-ASP Module. 
3.2.3.1.1 Steps to estimate activity shifting leakage for avoiding planned deforestation 
STEP 1: Determination of the baseline rate of forest clearance by the class of deforestation agents 
Of  the  three  options  in  Module  LK-ASP  for  estimating  the  baseline  rate  of  forest  clearance  by  the  
deforestation agent, we used Option 1.1. We determined the national historic trend for areas licensed for 
HTI  to  provide  insight  regarding  plantation  establishment,  so  we  could  determine  baseline  scenario  
deforestation (see Table 47). Without official government data indicating clearance in these concessions 
and  according  to  Module  LK-ASP,  we  defined  the  rate  of  clearance  to  the  baseline  rate  of  deforestation  
(D%) as 7.85% as derived from proxy area and analysis further outlined in Section 3.2.1.

We  conducted  a  regression  analysis  to  test  the  significance  of  the  historic  trend  in  the  cumulative  area  
licensed for conversion to HTI plantations between 2006 and 2015 (Figure 4).The regression had a p-value 
of < 0.000001 and an adjusted R
2
 of 0.95, which met requirements in the LK-ASP Module (p ≤ 0.05 and 
adjusted R
2
 of ≥ 0.75). We then multiplied the projected annual area licensed by the estimated deforestation 
rate of 7.85% from the baseline proxy analysis to derive the estimated annual area converted to plantations 
between 2016 and 2062.

annually for HTI plantations between 2016 and 2062. Applicable laws and common practices suggest that 
10%  of  the  total  licensed  concession  area  would  be  reserved  for  conservation  while  the  remaining  90%

STEP 3: Monitoring of all areas deforested by baseline class of agents of deforestation through the years 
in which planned deforestation was forecasted to occur 
For each monitoring event, we will estimate all areas deforested by the class of agents in the country by 
monitoring  the  total  area  licensed  for  conversion  to  HTI  concessions  and  the  actual  deforestation  (when  
applicable data become available). We will document areas of deforestation and leakage in our monitoring 
report by using the equation (34):

As a result of project activities described in Section 2.1.11, we assumed that no leakage will occur. We will 
monitor and report the actual leakage throughout the duration of the project. 
STEP 4: Monitoring of GHG emissions outside the project boundary by baseline agent of deforestation 
This can only be monitored and quantified when a specific deforestation agent is identified and therefore, 
is not considered by the project. 
STEP 5: Estimation of peat carbon in all of the class of agents’ concessions 
We determined the emission factors for activity shifting leakage to peatlands based on carbon lost at PDT 
(C
peatloss
,
tPDT
) in the undrained peatland of alternative areas. We estimated the PDTs using the principles in 
the  equations  (1)  to  (12)  set  out  in  Module  X-STR  by  applying  drainability  limit  restrictions  similar  to  
principles applied to the project area. 
Using the land-use designation of Production Forest (Hutan Produksi or HP) in Indonesia right before the 
project start date, we considered areas that could potentially generate peat emissions as a result of shifting 
leakage.
56
  Some  HP  areas  are  unlicensed,  while  others  have  become  licensed  HTI  concessions  (Table 
50). We examined these unlicensed HP and licensed HTI areas as a conservative measure because other 
types of industrial forestry-based plantations could potentially occupy unlicensed HP areas in the future. 
We  assumed  that  on  peatlands,  anytime  there  is  deforestation  there  is  simultaneous  drainage  We  
conservatively assumed that drained peatland is equal to deforested peatland area because industrial-level 
conversion and deforestation on large areas of peatlands results in canal development that drains the peat. 
Furthermore, the 2015 nation-wide catastrophic fires resulted in deforestation in areas that might not have 
been   drained   when   the   fires   occurred.   By   assuming   these   deforested   areas   were   drained,   we   
conservatively included a possible scenario that licensed HTI or unlicensed HP areas would eventually be 
drained.  In  order  to  determine  the  proportion  of  undrained  peatland  in  unlicensed  HP  areas,  we  
conservatively compared the total, forested HTI plantations in HP and unlicensed HP areas, from actual 
spatial data.
56
 From the analysis, approximately 7,309,070.22 ha (61.3%) of the licensed HTI in HP area 
was  estimated  to  have  been  deforested  at  the  project  start  date.  We  assumed  conservatively  that  all  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
160 
peatlands in the unlicensed area are forested and undrained. This assumption considered a higher AGB 
carbon stock in peatlands despite the actual vegetation composition, hence a more conservative approach 
for accounting growth rate and emission factors.

STEP 6: Estimation of CO
2
 emission factor for leakage to peatland per ha 
We used the proportion of deforested area (54.7%) in the HP area licensed for HTI concessions, to estimate 
the proportion of deforested or drained peatland before the project start date. We assumed the projected 
undrained peatland that would be drained in the baseline in HP areas licensed for HTI to be equal to the 
forested peatland without the area designated for conservation (10% of the peatland). We estimated the 
projected undrained peatland that would be drained in the unlicensed HP areas as equal to 90% of peatland 
area.

We  assume  that  no  leakage  will  occur.  There  are  extensive  leakage  mitigation  activities  in  place  for  the  
SMPP,  and  we  will  continue  to  monitor  and  report  any  leakage  that  occurs  during  the  project  crediting 
period.  
3.2.3.1.3 Estimation of emissions from displacement of pre-project agricultural activities (LK-ARR) 
The  VM0007  Module  LK-ARR  requires  the  use  of  the  CDM  tool  “Estimation  of  the  increase  in  GHG  
emissions attributable to displacement of pre-project agricultural activities in A/R CDM project activity” area. 
Step 1 of the CDM tool requires that the area subject to pre-project agricultural activities that is expected 
to  be  afforested/reforested  (therefore  the  activities  having  to  be  displaced)  be  identified.  In  the  areas  
identified for ARR activities, the SMPP project area did not have any pre-project agricultural activities taking 
place  within  the  project  area.  As  a  result,  no  pre-project  agricultural  activities  will  be  displaced  by  ARR  
project activities, and hence Change_C_LK-ARR = 0. 
3.2.3.2 Estimation of emissions from ecological leakage (LK-ECO) 
Applicability  conditions  of  the  VM0007  Module  LK-ECO  require  that  ecological  leakage  affecting  the  soil  
(peat) carbon pool does not occur. With a robust design and implementation plan of constructing compacted 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
164 
peat dams to block existing canals, we expect that no ecological leakage will occur in the project scenario. 
We will monitor the effectiveness of rewetting activities to confirm that no ecological leakage is occurring at 
each monitoring event. Monitoring of ecological leakage will take place by installing monitoring dip wells 
within the vicinity of canal block positions inside and bordering the project area (see details in Annex 8).

We calculated  CO
2
  and  CH
4
 emission  uncertainties  from  microbial  decomposition  of  peat  and  dissolved  
organic carbon (DOC) from water bodies in the baseline and project scenarios, using the standard error 
data  from  the  peat  emission  factors  in  the  IPCC  Wetlands  Supplement  2013  (see  Annex 11).
53
  We 
established that CH
4
 emission uncertainty was zero, as it was conservatively excluded from our emission 
calculations. We also established that uncertainty from GHG emissions from peat burning was also zero in 
the  project  scenario,  as  we  assumed  full  fire  prevention  in  the  project  area.  However,  we  calculated  
uncertainty from GHG emissions from peat burning in the baseline using the dry mass burnt per stratum 
per  year  and  their  standard  deviations  converted  to  95%  confidence  intervals.  We  combined  emissions  
data from CO
2
 and CH
4
 burning to produce an overall uncertainty in CO
2
 equivalent, because Module X-
UNC does not distinguish between these emissions.  
We calculated the uncertainty for WRC as 34.79% and 22.29% for the years 2016 and 2017, respectively 
in the baseline scenario. In the project scenario the uncertainty was calculated as 1.99% and 1.41%, for 
2016 and 2017, respectively. This resulted in a total error in this project activity at 26.12% and 17.03, for 
2016 and 2017, respectively. Assuming a threshold of 15% uncertainty, we deducted the VCUs for 2016 
and 2017 by 11.12% and 2.03%, respectively. Further details on all calculations are provided in Annex 14.

3.2.4.4 Calculation of the VCS non-permanence risk buffer withholding

VM0007, the annual buffer withholding for all activities was determined as a percentage of the total carbon 
stock benefits which excludes emissions due to leakage (see Table 57). As the project does not account 
for emissions from fossil fuel combustion, direct N
2

also omitted from calculations.

3.3.3 Monitoring Plan 
3.3.3.1 Approaches for climate impact monitoring 
The  adaptive  management  plan  for  climate  impact  monitoring  incorporates  lessons  learned  from  past  
monitoring to revise goals and objectives, includes the most recent scientific findings and new technologies 
as  they  become  available  in  order  to  systematically  increase  effectiveness  of  existing  practices.  The  
adaptive management plan creates a tool to unify past and present experience into strategic planning for 
project management  as  part  of  a shared  learning  process.  The  following  tasks  will  be  performed  on  an  
annual basis by the project team as part of the monitoring report compilation: 
• Disseminate and evaluate field data collection, and improve workflows were needed. 
• Review recent literature on the scientific advancements of the specific sections of the climate 
impact monitoring system (i.e. remote sensing, forest inventories, in-situ GHG emission 
monitoring from peat decomposition, water bodies, peat and biomass burning, and peat 
characteristics and peat subsidence). 
• Review the latest technological advancements in the different fields and incorporate these were 
feasible and needed. 
 
Climate impact monitoring includes monitoring changes in land cover, peat thickness and water table level, 
as per the VCS VM0007 methodological requirements. A summary of the main monitoring methods is given 
below. 
3.3.3.1.1 Remote sensing 
We will obtain and analyze satellite imagery to detect land cover change, such as natural regeneration of 
vegetation, or (if any) vegetation loss caused by fire or encroachment. In cases where forest changes take 
place, we will follow the procedures outlined in VCS methodology VM0007 Module M-MON to quantify the 
relevant parameters.  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
180 
M-MON outlined  three  main  steps  for  monitoring  of  greenhouse  gas  emissions  and  removals by 
implemented by applying the following: 
STEP 1. Selection and analyses of sources of land-use and land-cover (LU/LC) change data 
STEP 2. Interpretation and analyses 
STEP 3. Documentation 
We will update the land cover map at each monitoring period and carry out the update annually. In Step 1 
(with  more  details  of  the  process  documented  in  the  Remote  Sensing  Standard  Operating  Procedure  
document in Annex X), our base image will be medium-resolution satellite image covering the Project Area 
(e.g. 30 x 30 m resolution of Landsat 8 or finer resolution).   We plan to use the same source of remotely 
sensed data and data analysis techniques within the period for which the baseline is fixed.  
We plan to use Landsat 8 Level-2 image containing surface reflectance and spectral index bands, as the 
Level-2 image is the same source data used for producing the forest benchmark map and has gone through 
geometric, topographic and atmospheric corrections. If for any reason we need to replace the source data 
with different types of image, we will perform minimum pre-processing that involves geometric correction 
and georeferencing and cloud and shadow detection and removal. 
In Annex  X  we  document  detailed  post-processing  to  locate area  change  detected  in  the  imagery  and 
calculate the area of each land cover type of change within the Project Area at the end of each monitoring 
period,  in  order  to  update  the  remaining  area  of  forest  in  RRL  (ARRL,forest,t)  At  the  time  of  baseline  
revision, we will calculate the area of each land cover type in the Project Area and then update the forest 
cover benchmark map. 
We will quantify the area of recorded deforestation by subtracting change areas of forest cover for Step 2 
to produce an estimate of the emissions resulting from any deforestation that occurs within the Project Area 
(ΔCP,Def,i,t).  The  quantification  of  newly  deforested area  is  based  on  common  practices  of  change  
detection  techniques  detailed  in  Annex  X.  We  will  then  calculate  the  emissions  from  deforestation  by  
multiplying areas of deforestation by the average forest carbon stock per hectare. The area of remaining 
forest will be based on subtracting non-forest area and we will record it in a forest benchmark map, which 
will be updated for each monitoring period. 
Map 31 below shows the initial benchmark map of forest/non-
forest  area  distribution.  The  net  carbon  stock  change  as  a  result  of  deforestation  is  equal  to  the  area  
deforested multiplied  by  the  emission  per  hectare.  The  emission  per  hectare  is  equal  to  the  difference  
between the stocks before and after deforestation minus any wood products created from timber extraction 
in the process of deforestation. 
 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
181 
Map 31. Forest cover benchmark map 
 
In addition to the above, we will use MODIS and VIIRS hotspot data to provide nearly real-time updates on 
potential fire activity in the project area and alert ground fire controlling/prevention team to examine the field 
conditions. 
In Step 3 to document the methodological procedures used in Steps 1-2 above, in Annex X we list details 
of data and documentation preparation. In particular, we will document data sources and pre-processing 
steps undertaken for the remote sensing data, definition of the land cover classes, classification approach 
and classification algorithms, description of the ground truth or ground reference data collected for training 
purposes, ancillary data used in the classification, software used to perform the classification, and additional 
spatial data and analysis used for post-classification analysis. For the classification accuracy assessment, 
we  document  accuracy  assessment  technique  used,  description  of  the  ground  truth or  reference  data 
collected for classification accuracy assessment, and final classification accuracy assessment result. 
3.3.3.1.2 Field measurement 
Monitoring C stock 
In  2020  we  will  begin  monitoring  change  in  carbon  stocks  of  tree  biomass  through  field  measurements  
using a point sampling method with an allometric equation on tree diameter (diameter at breast height or 
DBH).
58
 Per CDM A/R Methodological Tool “Calculation of the number of sample plots for measurements 
within A/R CDM project activities,”
59
 the  project  will  establish  monitoring  plots  for  areas  representing  the  
ARR activities in 2020 and continue to monitor thereafter every five years. Allometric equations based on 
DBH will be the main parameter. 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
182 
Monitoring GHG emissions from microbial decomposition of peat 
In  2020  we  will  begin  monitoring  GHG  emissions  from  microbial  decomposition  of  peat  by  directly  
monitoring  proxy  variables  for  GHG  flux  that  are  used  when  calculating  GHG  emissions.  For  strata  with  
less dynamic water table levels (e.g. undrained forested peatland) as well as strata where water table levels 
are more dynamic or without forest cover (e.g. initially drained deforested peatland), we will monitor forest 
and non-forest cover as well as water table levels to update annual strata cover conditions and drainage 
status. Remote sensing methods in Section 3.3.3.1.1 will inform changes in vegetation cover. To monitor 
drainage status, we will implement point-based monitoring using dip wells installed and distributed along 
transects  comprising  each  water  table  level  stratum.  We  will  then  develop  a  hydrological  model  to  
extrapolate dip well measurements into a continuous water table level map. We will examine changes in 
vegetation  cover  and  water  table  levels  to  determine  status  change  strata  (if  any),  and  efficacy  of  
compacted peat dams for canal blocking. Annual GHG emissions from microbial decomposition of peat will 
be summarized by using IPCC default emission factor and following procedures in the VSC methodology 
VM0007 Module M-PEAT. A detailed canal blocking plan, as well as water table level monitoring sampling 
design and SOP are available in Annex 8. 
Monitoring GHG emissions from water bodies 
We  will  determine  the  amount  of  time  necessary  to  develop  and  monitor  site-specific  dissolved  organic  
carbon (DOC) value(s). The value(s) will be used to calculate emissions from water bodies by considering 
changes  in  area  (i.e.  channel  widths  and  lengths  derived  from  visual  assessment  with  medium-high 
resolution remote sensing images). 
Monitoring GHG emissions from peat and biomass burning  
In  addition  to  the  hotspot  remote  sensing  data  listed  in  Section  3.3.3.1.1,  we  will  also  monitor  GHG  
emissions  from  peat  and  biomass  burning  with  regular  patrol.  At  every  burning  incident  (if  any),  we  will  
delineate burning area by a combination of ground checking and visual assessment from remote sensing 
imagery. We will summarize GHG emissions from burning annually following Module E-BPB. 
Peat characteristics and subsidence 
The baseline scenario requires updating every ten years. Upon the year to update, we will conduct peat 
inventory at sampling locations stratified by a DTM. The inventory data collection will include peat depth, 
peat  type,  bulk  density  and  carbon  content.  The  field  data  will  inform  the  most  suitable  topographic  and  
hydrological variables for deriving an interpolated peat thickness map. We will then use this model output 
to characterize subsidence and other baseline emission characteristics. The details of monitoring design 
can be found in Annex 8.    
3.3.4 Dissemination of Monitoring Plan and Results (CL4.2) 
All data generated by the project area flows through a multi-stage data collection, QA/QC, compilation and 
analysis management process (

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
183 
Fi  gure 5). Both soft (scanned or digital) and hard copies of data are stored in the project’s central office in 
Jakarta,  synchronized  in  a  network  attached  storage  and  backed  up  in  a  cloud  system.  This  allows  
simultaneous multi-user inputs and data versioning history, as well as lost data recovery.
 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
184 
Figure 5. Data collection, QA/QC, and processing workflow. Steps in oval = conducted internally by the 
project staff; Steps in rectangle = conducted by either internal project staff or external project consultant. 
 
We  standardize  data  through  standard  operation  procedures  (SOPs)  and  perform  multiple  quality  
assurance and quality control (QA/QC) assessments. All data collected, recorded, stored, and reported are 
subject to review and approval by data analysts, data managers, the technical project lead, and the project 
team  leader  with  reference  to  each  level  of  data  management.  In  order  to  ensure  the  security  and  
traceability of data entry and QA/QC, all data in raw, intermediate, and final stages have access levels pre-
defined for different types of users.  
Brief, regular monitoring reports will be generated to assess the quality of data being received from the field 
and investigations into any potential outliers. Any possible monitoring equipment failures will be carried out 
to ensure the data passes QA/QC. 
We will have the climate monitoring plan available for public review at the project office. The full results of 
the  initial  climate  monitoring  included  in  this  project  document  are  being  made  publicly  available  in  the  
project zone. Additionally, a project document summary is written and provided to communities throughout 
the project zone in English and Bahasa Indonesia. 
3.4 Optional Criterion: Climate Change Adaptation Benefits  
3.4.1 Regional Climate Change Scenarios (GL1.1) 
Precipitation  in  Indonesia  is  strongly  influenced  by  El  Niño/  Southern  Oscillation  (ENSO)  events,  which  
might  become  more  frequent  and  intense  as  a  result  of  climate  change.  Because  Indonesia  typically  
experiences drought during El Niño events (the warm phase of ENSO) and excessive rain during La Niña 
events (cool phase of ENSO), the shifting global pattern of ENSO will have substantial regional impacts.
60
 
Various  reports  show  that  the  geographic  region  of  the  project  zone  is  likely  to  exhibit  various  effects  of  
climate  change  with  greater  weather  anomalies  by  2050,  2080,  and  2100.  Temperatures  will  increase  
consistently with increased duration of heat waves and dry spells.
61,62
 There will be a considerable shift in 
precipitation  patterns,  with  increase  in  rainfall  especially  during  the  wettest  months  of  rainy  season  
(December to February).
60,63
 Pattern of seasonality is likely to change, with the wet season begins several 
weeks later than the average compared to historical periods.
64
 This delay would result in increase in rainfall 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
185 
later in the crop year and decrease in rainfall later in the dry season.
62
 There will be increased frequency 
and intensity of heavy rainfall events.
61
 
The region is vulnerable to other weather-related disasters and climate hazards such as forest and land 
fires,  storms,  and  drought  that  damage  infrastructure  and  degrade  ecosystems,  leading  to  loss  of  life,  
property, ecosystem services, and livelihoods.
61
 Accordingly, longer dry season and a more intense rainy 
season will result in prolonged drought and more intense flooding.
64
 
3.4.2 Climate Change Impacts (GL1.2) 
Changes  in  land-use,  temperature,  and  precipitation  have  already  caused  widespread  wildfires  in  the  
project  zone,  as  evidenced  by  the  catastrophic  fires  in  2015.  Altered  biophysical  features,  as  well  as  
vegetation  composition  and  structure  impact  biodiversity  and  ecosystem  services  functioning.  Climate  
change would exacerbate this problem. 
Climate  change  has  food  security  and  economic  growth  implications.  Changing  precipitation  patterns,  
evaporation, run-off, and soil moisture would all impact agricultural production levels. In addition, reduced 
availability of freshwater will have implications for health and economic production processes.
62
  
Specific to agriculture, heat stress will reduce production, heat and humidity increase will increase pests 
and diseases, heavy rainfall will increase flood damage to cultivated land, and increased inundation and 
drought will cause production loss or failure. For water resources, elongated dry seasons will reduce water 
supplies  for  domestic  consumption  and  agriculture  and  floods  will  decrease  water  quality.  For  natural  
resources,  increased  frequency  and  extent  of  wildfires  will  cause  decline  in  biodiversity  (or  extinction  of  
endangered  species)  and  ecosystem  services  and  affect  viability  of  forest-dependent  livelihoods.  For  
human  health,  increased  floods  and  humidity  will  introduce  incidence  of  waterborne  and  vector-borne 
diseases, whereas increased heat will bring risk of food security.
61
 
In  the  province  of  South  Sumatra  where  the  project  zone  is,  climate  change  will  impose  declines  in  the  
value of agricultural output, increased exposures and costs due to impacts of dengue fever and malaria, 
and increased value of agricultural losses.
63
 
3.4.3 Measures Needed and Designed for Adaptation (GL1.3) 
The project area and project zone are vulnerable to the potential impacts of climate change, which imposes 
impact on ecosystem services functioning, livelihoods and health. In the project zone, we aim to strengthen 
community  (human)  and  environmental  (non-human)  resilience  by  implementing  adaptation  options  
through a variety of project activities (see details in Section 2.1.11). We summarize activities relevant to 
climate change adaptation and their respective benefits in Table 59 below.
