4 COMMUNITY 
4.1 Without-Project Community Scenario  
4.1.1.1 Descriptions of Communities at Project Start (CM1.1) 
The  SMPP  is  in  an  isolated  area  with  comparatively  little  interaction  with  local  people.  The  closest  
surrounding villages are removed from the project site at a significant distance by boat, car, or motorbike 
(Map 8). For example, the Kepayang village is located about 15 km from the SMPP’s main camp but given 
road conditions it is about a 45-minute to 1.5-hour motorbike or car ride. On a small motorized canoe (often 
used by local fishermen), it can take roughly two hours.  
There are still some important groups that interact with the project landscape and activities. In the Climate, 
Community & Biodiversity Standards, Third Edition, a “community” refers to any group of people that derive 
any  income,  livelihood,  cultural  values,  or  other  contributions  to  well-being  from  within  the  project  area.  
There are no individuals living within the SMPP area and few that rely on it for their livelihood or ascribe 
cultural value to the zone. The SMPP interacts with a total of two community groups, according to the CCB: 
a group of 21 fishermen, distributed across the Kepayang river, the Buring river, and the Tembesu Daro 
canal, and two small-scale palm oil farmers and their families.  
 
Following the CCB methodology, we have defined the residents of nearby settlements, including from the 
formally recognized Muara Muang and Kepayang villages, as well as other unofficial settlements along the 
rivers, as “other stakeholders” as they do not derive their livelihoods from the project area. However, these 
communities  are  an  important  aspect  of  the  social  development  component  of  the  project  and  thus  the  
community profiles and our program objectives for them are covered in detail in section 4.3, which covers 
other stakeholder groups.  
 
Fishing  activities  are  spread  across  three  main  areas,  including  those  used  by  four  fishermen  along  the  
Kepayang river, four fishermen along the Buring river, and the remaining along the Tembesu Daru canal. 
Proximity  of  large  industrial  activities  among  other  factors,  has  resulted  in  the  Lalan  river  becoming  too  
polluted to fish safely. Fishing communities therefore rely on the cleaner waters in and around the SMPP 
for safe and productive fishing, despite the considerable distance away from their homes. The smallholder 
farmers cultivate palm oil on the eastern border of the project boundary and the project team has an on-
going and open dialogue with them about the impact and potential benefits of the project.  
4.1.1.1.1 Local ethnicities 
All  of  the  people  living  in  this  region,  the  fishermen  and  farmer  communities,  as  well  as  the  other  
stakeholders,  have  diverse  ethnic  backgrounds.  There  are  currently  no  longer  any  self-described 
indigenous people living in the area. The current inhabitants of the area have mostly migrated from other 
parts  of  Indonesia  and  have  lived  in  this  region  for  a  long  time,  dating  back  to  the  founding  of  these  
settlements  and  villages.  Many  people  have  come  from  other  areas  in  Sumatra,  further  north  from  the  
SMPP  area,  including  from  Jambi  province  and  further  away  from  Medan  in  North  Sumatra,  and  ethnic  
groups also include people of Javanese and Melayu origin. 
4.1.1.1.2 Land-uses and economic well-being 
The community of fishermen interacting with the SMPP are comprised primarily of retired older men, many 
of  whom  may  not  be  able  to  complete  other  jobs  or  do  not  have  other  stable  income  sources.  Of  these  
fishermen, nine individuals rely solely on this fishing activity as their livelihood and harvest fish full time. 
The other twelve individuals conduct their fishing activities on a seasonal basis and rely on other jobs, using 
fishing to supplement their income and diversify their food sources. Both categories of fishermen consider 
fishing within the SMPP and neighboring areas a crucial aspect of their livelihoods to support their families. 
The farmers that cultivate land along the southeastern border of the SMPP are of a lower economic bracket 
compared to others within the nearby villages, and as there are many opportunities for other more formal 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
189 
jobs in the wider area, farming is much less common. Specifically, these farming activities focus primarily 
on small-scale palm oil production. 
Our  21  community  fishermen  range  in  age  from  39  to  69,  and  live  in  village  and  settlement  areas  of  
Kepayang, Tebing Harapan, Buring, Bakung, and Pal 8. We have demographic information about each of 
the community fishermen and farmers, however this information will be kept confidential. Through dialogue 
with these with local fishermen, we have estimated the average economic value of fishing livelihoods in the 
SMPP results in a monthly net income of $47 USD per fisherman. The average monthly salary in the Muara 
Merang and Kepayang villages ranges from $139-$209 USD per household, which is also well above the 
international poverty line of $1.90 USD per day according to the World Bank.
65
 
Adjacent to the project area, there are two farmers that occupy 8 and 7 ha of farmland, respectively, with 
5.9 ha and 5.6 ha located within the SMPP license area. These two farmed patches of land are identified 
in    Map 33. All of these community members live among the other stakeholders in the closest villages and 
settlements (however it is important to note, as described in Table 3 and in Section 2.1.6 , that these villages 
and settlements are still a significant distance away from the SMPP).
 
 
4.1.1.1.3 Public health 
The  quality  of  the  main  river  tributaries  that  connect  with  that  project  site  river  water  are  poor  and  often  
unsafe for use, however the water becomes cleaner closer into the project site. Smaller canals are used for 
washing,  bathing,  and  toilet  use  if  proper  toilets  are  not  available.  For  drinking  and  cooking,  transient  
fisherman or farmers working during the day use collected rainwater or bring in wholesale bottled water, 
sourced  from  treated  rainwater  or  containers  brought  from  Palembang.  The  transient  community  groups  
that interact with the project area have no other infrastructure to rely on other than makeshift latrines in the 
farming areas.  
4.1.1.1.4 Education 
Community  fishermen  that  frequent  the  SMPP  are  comprised  primarily  of  retired  older  men  and  the  two  
farmers are also older males with education levels ranging from elementary to high school. The areas near 
the SMPP have a variety of opportunities for formal education and the project has a strategy to invest in 
these  opportunities  for  project  community  members  and  their  families  and  those  defined  as  other  
stakeholders.  
4.1.2 Interactions between Communities and Community Groups (CM1.1) 
The communities identified by SMPP are outlined in Section 4.1.1.1 above. According to the standard CCB 
definition,  specific  groups  within  communities  that  can  be  distinguished  are  referred  to  as  “community  
groups”, but due to the size of our defined communities we do not have any community groups in our project 
zone.  As  we  do  not  have  community  groups  in  the  SMPP,  we  do  not  have  any  interactions  between  
communities and community groups.  
4.1.3 High Conservation Values (CM1.2) 
The SMPP project aims to protect and improve the quality of high conservation values (HCVs) that support 
community well-being within our project area. There are two HCVs identified within the SMPP area related 
to community well-being (outlined below in Table 60). The HCV areas are further described for HCV 4 in 
Section 5.1.3.5 in Map 41 and for HCV 5 regarding fishing in, Map 34, Map 35, Map 36, and farming in Map 
33.

4.1.4 Without-Project Scenario: Community (CM1.3) 
The SMPP area is largely surrounded by industrial plantation forest and therefore—in the absence of this 
project—this endangered peatland ecosystem would be at extremely high risk for conversion to an industrial 
forest plantation, namely fast-growing Acacia plantations grown for pulp and paper. This activity would have 
significant impacts on the physical conditions of the SMPP area, and in turn would impact the farming and 
fishing communities that operate within the SMPP, in addition to the other stakeholders that would be hit 
by  downstream  effects  from  fire  and  water  quality  degradation.  First,  an  Acacia  plantation  would  require  
further  draining  of  the  already  degraded  peat  leading  to  a  further  homogenization  of  important  aquatic  
habitats.  This  drainage  would  further  reduce  the  water  quality  in  the  SMPP  and  negatively  affect  fishing  
grounds, and massive canalization would disrupt soils. Secondly, the short average rotation of Acacia tree 
crops would mean that plantation forests cannot provide in situ, permanent long-term biodiversity benefits 
that  affect  overall  ecosystem  services  for  stakeholders  near  and  far.  As  such,  this  land-use  conversion  
would  directly  impact  the  fishermen  and  farmers  that  rely  on  the  SMPP  area  for  their  livelihoods.  
Additionally, there is no guarantee that in the absence of the project the fishermen, and farmers in particular 
would  maintain  access  to  the  land  they  need  to  support  their  livelihoods.  Therefore,  the  without-project 
scenario would lead to net negative impacts on communities, negating any limited positive impacts derived 
from the establishment of Acacia plantations such as opportunities for employment and limited corporate 
social responsibility programs. 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3

4.2 Net Positive Community Impacts  
4.2.1 Expected Community Impacts (CM2.1) 
As described in Section 4.1.1.1 above, the SMPP has a limited number of communities within the project 
area. The small group of fishermen and farmers that derive their livelihoods from within the SMPP area all 
reside  in  the  neighboring  areas  outside  of  the  project  boundary  (see  Section  2.1.6 for  a  definition  of  
communities and other stakeholders). However, the project will still provide a variety of positive impacts to 
these stakeholders, as outlined in Table 61 below. Due to the lack of individuals living near or within the 
SMPP, we will focus much of our community development programming on those defined by the CCB as 
other stakeholders, which live primarily in the Muara Merang and Kepayang villages. These activities are 
outlined in detail throughout Section 4.3. Given that the direct community members also live in these same 
surrounding  areas,  our  work  to  provide  benefits  to  other  stakeholders  in  neighboring  settlements  and  
villages will also benefit community members beyond what has been immediately identified below.

4.2.2 Negative Community Impact Mitigation (CM2.2) 
The SMPP communities of fishermen and farmers do not reside within the SMPP but only enter and exit 
for  activities  related  to  their  livelihoods.  It  is  possible  that  as  biodiversity  returns  to  the  SMPP  area,  
fishermen or farmer communities may be impacted by large wildlife such as sun bears or tigers. To deal 
with  this  potential  negative  impact,  we  have  a  wildlife  human  conflict  reduction  strategy  and  will  train  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
197 
community  members  on  how  to  reduce  this  risk.  We  will  also  continue  to  maintain  direct  lines  of  
communication with the project communities to address issues through our adaptive management plan to 
mitigate issues as they arise.   
Increased  water  levels  from  canal  blocking  and  other  peat  rewetting  strategies  could  potentially  impact  
farmer  productivity,  but  through  in  person  interviews  our  farmer  community  members  have  expressed  a  
sentiment  that  the  decreased  risk  from  fire  from  mitigation  activities  by  the  project  outweighs  any  
inconvenience  caused  by  higher  water  levels.  The  biggest  risk  to  smallholder  farming  assets  is  from  
wildfires, which could completely wipe out their investment, as evidenced by the 2015 fire which degraded 
a large portion of the area.  
4.2.3 Net Positive Community Well-Being (CM2.3, GL1.4) 
With the SMPP, we aim to generate significant net positive impacts for the fishing and farming communities 
within  the  SMPP.  The  project  will  ensure  that  fishermen  will  be  able  to  maintain  access  to  their  fishing  
grounds  and  in  addition  the  project  will  buy  fish  to  support  these  individual’s  livelihoods,  which  will  also  
provide sustenance for project staff. The project will also offer employment opportunities for this community 
if they are interested.  
Better hydrology management is expected to improve the water quality in the project area which will create 
more abundant and protected fishing grounds. The project will also help reduce fires throughout the wider 
project  landscape,  by  deploying  a  fully  equipped  fire-fighting  team  and  early  warning  system  (EWS)  for  
fires.  These  efforts  to  protect  the  concession  also  protects  vital  community  assets,  reducing  the  risk  of  
fishing grounds or farms being destroyed. The identified farmer community will have long-term livelihoods 
security through an agreement with the project that guarantees land tenure for their existing plots of land 
that overlap into the project boundaries. These stakeholders are also engaged and have been trained in 
fire monitoring and other forest patrol activities.  
Additionally, the well-being of nearby stakeholders will improve as we enact our community development 
plan and bring community-focused activities into the SMPP and the surrounding villages and settlements. 
Positive  impacts  from  the  SMPP  community  development  program  targeting  other  stakeholders  will  also  
affect the fishermen and farmers that rely on the project area, as these communities live in the villages and 
settlements  surrounding  the  SMPP  area.  Benefits  from  this  community  development  include  improved  
access  to  clean  water,  effective  waste  management,  health  care  and  education  (see  Section  4.3.1).  In  
general,  ecosystem  services  in  the  project  area  will  be  significantly  enhanced.  When  compared  to  the  
without-project scenario, the communities and other stakeholders in the region will benefit from an overall 
increased quality of life and improved livelihoods as a result of project activities.  
4.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (CM2.4) 
Our  project  will  ensure  that  no  HCVs  related  to  community  well-being  will  be  negatively  affected  by  the  
SMPP. The identified communities that interact with SMPP rely heavily on the protection of the HCV 5 areas 
for fishing and small-scale farming. The other stakeholders in nearby villages benefit from the protection of 
the peat swamp forest and other areas within the SMPP for overall improved water quality. Our project’s 
main objective is to protect and enhance these areas, and therefore there are no predicted negative impacts 
on  these  HCVs.  The  SMPP  will  utilize  our  community  development  team  on  the  ground  to  continuously  
monitor for any potential negative impacts on these HCVs in the project area.  
4.3 Other Stakeholder Impacts  
The project defines “Other stakeholders” as any groups other than communities  that  will  be  affected  by  
project  activities,  namely  proactive  social  development  efforts  and  livelihoods  programming.  The  SMPP  
aims to work with residents of nearby settlements and villages, including those primarily from the formally 

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
198 
recognized Muara Muang and Kepayang villages, as well as other unofficial settlements along the rivers, 
all of which are defined as other stakeholders (see Table 3 in Section 2.1.9 for further detail).  
Approximately 3,316 people comprising 851 households reside in Muara Muang, and 2,436 people in 789 
households  reside  in  Kepayang.
12
  It  is  important  to  emphasize  that  these  stakeholders  are  still  fairly  
removed from the project site, at a significant distance from the SMPP. However, SMPP is committed to 
robust  development  activities  in  the  villages  and  settlements  that  surround  the  project  site,  despite  their  
distance. Not only are these efforts a key part of the benefit sharing strategy for the project, but the creation 
of strong and supportive relationships with local stakeholders will also prevent risks associated with future 
encroachment, in the case where the project’s restored ecological functions become an attractive target for 
illegal activities such as logging or poaching.  
Other stakeholders – Economic Profile 
Other  stakeholders  find  livelihoods  through  more  formal  employment  in  areas  throughout  the  Musi  
Banyuasin regency. There are a wide variety of economic opportunities available the villages immediately 
surrounding  the  SMPP,  and  these  options  ensure  that  the  majority  of  people  can  find  consistent,  stable  
incomes. Potential occupations include working for nearby companies with mining, oil and gas, industrial 
agriculture, paper pulp, and palm oil production operations, as well as other farming including cultivation of 
rubber, cassava, palm oil and pineapple crops.  
These diverse opportunities have resulted in above-average income and employment levels and levels of 
community infrastructure that are also above the national average. The relatively stable and varied access 
to livelihood opportunities results in communities that have above-average access to resources, a steadily 
declining poverty line and adequate community infrastructure. Most people live in well-built sturdy homes 
with  sanitation  facilities,  and  many  residents  with  higher  income  levels  own  cars  and  other  forms  of  
motorized transportation.  
In  2018,  9.8%  of  the  population  of  Indonesia  lived  below  the  national  poverty  line.
10
  In  Musi  Banyuasin  
regency, the percentage of the population that is  impoverished has been declining over the years with a 
drop  from  18.99%  in  2011  to  16.75%  in  2017.
11
  In  the  province  of  South  Sumatra,  the  poverty  level  is  
defined  from  a  monthly  household  expenditures  metric  that  is  set  at  less  than  or  equal  to  IDR  400,000.  
Additionally,  in  2017  the  Human  Development  Index  (HDI)  of  the  Musi  Banyuasin  regency  was  66.96.  
Based on a social appraisal of the nearby villages, the average monthly income per household in the Muara 
Merang  and  Kepayang  villages  is  2-3  million  IDR,  or  24-36  million  IDR  per  year  (estimated  through  
discussions in these villages). This value equates to around $4.61- $6.91 USD per day, which is also well 
above the international poverty line of $1.90 USD per day according to the World Bank. 
Other stakeholders – Public Health 
The  public  health  conditions  of  other  stakeholders  are  described  in  Section  2.1.6.4.  As  part  of  SMPP  
community  development  program,  we  will  work  with  SMPP  stakeholders  to  increase  the  level  of  public  
health services available.  
Other stakeholders – Education 
The  educational  facilities  and  educations  levels  of  other  stakeholders  residing  in  Muara  Merang  and  
Kepayang are described in detail in Section 2.1.6.5. As part of SMPP community development program, 
we will work with SMPP stakeholders to implement a variety of education initiatives and support.  
4.3.1 Impacts on Other Stakeholders (CM3.1) 
The  majority  of  SMPP  community  development  activities  will  be  directly  focused  on  the  project’s  other  
stakeholders, who we have defined as residents of nearby settlements and villages surrounding the SMPP. 
We do not anticipate any negative impacts to this group of other stakeholders as a result of project activities, 
especially as these stakeholders do not derive their livelihoods from project area and reside a considerable 
distance  away  from  the  SMPP  (see  Section  2.1.6).  The  SMPP’s  community  development  activities  will  

         CCB + VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
199 
generate  positive  impacts  for  other  stakeholders  as  the  activities  are  designed  to  strengthen  family  
livelihoods by creating local employment opportunities, making new investments into small businesses and 
increasing  the  quality  of  education  in  nearby  villages.  The  following  expected  impacts  on  these  other  
stakeholders include:  
• Education support through continued education opportunities for local teachers through Open 
University (Universitas Terbuka), after school programs for elementary school children, adult 
education for employees who do not hold a high school diploma, improvement of elementary and 
middle school sanitation facilities, and enrichment and maintenance of school libraries. 
• Improved livelihoods and quality of life through local food sourcing for project employees and 
training for craftsmen/women, as well as investments in women’s groups in both Muara Merang 
and Kepayang villages and support to community health programs.  
• Youth and local employment to increase the number of SMPP field staff recruited locally in 
addition to the creation of educational development and capacity building opportunities for 
employees.  
• Community garbage and plastic waste cleanup initiatives including plastic recycling and trash 
collection, which creates value from plastic waste, supported by community cleanup events on a 
monthly basis. 
• Co-financing for the construction of household rainwater catchment systems to address the need 
of clean and pollution-free water. 
Community investments are designed to be an integral part of the SMPP’s operating expenses. The project 
aims   to   deliver   tangible   benefits   to   surrounding   communities   through   community-based   business   
development  programs  and  education  initiatives.  To  support  these  efforts,  the  project  will  continue  to  
allocate a significant portion of its operating budget for community development throughout its long-term 
operations.  
4.3.2 Mitigation of Negative Impacts on Other Stakeholders (CM3.2) 
Identified other stakeholders, principally the residents of the settlements and villages closest to the SMPP 
rarely  engage  in  activities  in  the  project  area  and  reside  a  great  distance  away  from  the  SMPP,  which  
reduces the risk of negative impacts from project activities (see Section 2.1.6).    
However, it is possible that as biodiversity returns to the SMPP area, local stakeholders may be impacted 
by large wildlife such as sun bears or tigers, but we have assessed this risk as low. To deal with this potential 
negative  impact,  SMPP  will  be  developing  a  wildlife  human  conflict  reduction  strategy  and  will  train  
stakeholders on how to reduce this potential negative risk in due course. We will also continue to maintain 
direct lines of communication with local stakeholders and should any other negative impacts emerge, we 
will be prepared as part of our adaptive management plan to adjust operations so that potential negative 
impacts can be mitigated effectively.  
4.3.3 Net Impacts on Other Stakeholders (CM3.3) 
As  described  in  Section  4.3.2 above,  the  SMPP  has  no  net  negative  impacts  to  other  stakeholders.  All  
positive impacts to other stakeholders are discussed in Section 4.3.1.

4.4 Community Impact Monitoring  
4.4.1 Community Monitoring Plan (CM4.1, CM4.2, GL1.4, GL2.2, GL2.3, GL2.5) 
The SMPP has created a monitoring plan to track community and other stakeholder impacts delivered by 
the  project.  This  monitoring  plan  addresses  the  following  core  issues:  improved  livelihoods,  education  
support,  and  health  (Table 62).  This  plan  considers  the  components  necessary  to  conduct  successful  
monitoring  of  all  stakeholders  and  the  impacts  of  the  SMPP  throughout  the  duration  of  the  project.  Our  
community monitoring plan considers the overall requirements of the project combined with the financial, 
logistical,  and  staff  capacity  constraints,  starting  with  the  specific  fishing  and  farming  communities  and  
widening to the equally-important group of other stakeholders who live in nearby settlements and villages. 
Further information about how SMPP will address social impacts related to the HCVs 4 and 5, please refer 
to  Section  4.1.3.  All  further  details  and  descriptions  of  the  activities  that  will  take  place  as  part  of  our  
community monitoring are outlined in the SMPP Monitoring Report.

4.4.2 Monitoring Plan Dissemination (CM4.3) 
Our community monitoring plan will be disseminated to project communities, other stakeholders and the

4.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Community Benefits  
The project is not seeking to be validated to the Gold Level for the Community component as the majority 
of the residents in local villages near the project area are not below the national poverty line.
11
 Additionally, 
the project is not owned or co-managed by local communities, and as a result the project does not meet 
the CCBA criteria to obtain this distinction. Despite this, we will seek to deliver high levels of net positive 
impacts  for  the  identified  project  communities  and  other  stakeholders  who  live  in  nearby  villages  and  
settlements through the implementation of the project’s community development plan.

5 BIODIVERSITY 
5.1 Without-Project Biodiversity Scenario  
5.1.1 Existing Conditions (B1.1)  
Tropical biodiversity is in rapid decline globally, driven largely by habitat destruction and over exploitation 
as  well  as  a  range  of  interacting  drivers  including  climate  change,  invasive  species,  and  habitat  
degradation.
66
 The terrestrial biomes of Southeast Asia have been identified as some of the most at risk 
biomes on the planet,
66
 and Indonesia has suffered significant forest and biodiversity loss over the last 40 
years.
67
   
Sumatra is the sixth largest island in the world and the second largest island in Indonesia after Borneo. The 
remaining  forests  of  Sumatra  still  maintain  a  rich  biodiversity  with  high  levels  of  endemism.  However,  
Sumatra globally has one of the highest deforestation rates in the world, driven largely by transmigration 
and  expansion  of  commercial  agricultural  production  in  the  form  of  pulp/paper  and  palm  oil  production.  
Between 1985 and 2001, Sumatra lost 12 million hectares of natural forest, or 48% in 22 years. In 2007, 
Sumatra  only  had  30%  forest  cover  remaining  or  about  13  million  hectares  of  forest.  The  forests  in  the  
eastern plains of Sumatra, where the project site is located, are especially vulnerable to species extinction 
risk, and natural forest cover in the area has already declined by 70%.
68
 The SMPP aims to reverse this 
trend  by  protecting  a  site  of  acknowledged  international  importance  in  an  area  with  significant  peatland  
ecosystems and high conservation value species. The project aims to increase the variety, integrity, and 
extent of the peat swamp forest and to increase the abundance, diversity and habitat features of critically 
endangered and endemic wildlife.    
5.1.1.1 Biodiversity Baseline  
Ongoing ecological surveys of the SMPP site, including recent field visits in both May and June of 2018 
and in January 2019, have confirmed these areas still contain many globally significant species, despite 
the degraded nature of the forest throughout most of the concession. Species of particular interest include: 
• Sun bears (Helarctos malayanus), which are surprisingly abundant judging by the number of 
tracks, scrapes, and other signs observed.  
• Agile gibbons (Hylobates agilis), which are present in sufficient numbers to suggest the potential 
for the site to ultimately hold a significant population, provided habitat regeneration occurs as 
planned.  
• Hornbill species (Bucerotidae) and pheasants such as the Crestless Fireback (Lophura 
erythrophthalma). 
• The Sumatran tiger (Panthera tigris ssp. Sumatrae) has also been documented by project staff 
through visual sightings, vegetation markings, and observed footprints.  
There are several tiger prey species—such as pigs and deer—of enough population density in the project 
area  which  indicates  tigers  may  be  using  the  site  regularly  as  a  hunting  ground.  Similarly,  they  may  be  
using the project area to move between adjacent habitat blocks with the projects site acting as a significant 
biological  corridor.  With  adequate  long-term  protection  of  the  prey  base,  particularly  the  native  deer  and  
wild pig populations, it is very likely that the project site will support resident tigers. SMPP biodiversity staff 
continue to investigate how to strengthen the project site as a habitat for this critically endangered species, 
including boosting prey species.  
Several external threats have been identified within the project site, including Illegal logging, forest and land 
fires, hunting, lack of understanding and awareness of rare and threatened ecosystems by communities 
and peat drainage. In a January 2019 field visit, no sign of illegal logging, hunting or poaching was found. 
However,  forest  patrol  teams  and  project  management  staff  consider  it  essential  to  ensure  threat  levels  

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
206 
maintain  at  their  current  low  level,  especially  as  wildlife  populations  recover  over  time  in  response  to  
successful project implementation.  
Despite  the  degradation  in  the  forest  landscape,  the  SMPP  site  has  maintained  a  significant  level  of  
biodiversity  with  a  number  of  RTE  and  endemic  species  present  (Table 63),  with  monitoring  conducted  
throughout the project area since 2017 (Map 37).

5.1.2 Threats to Biodiversity 
Potential threats to biodiversity, including fire, conversion to industrial timber plantations, small-scale illegal 
timber  extraction,  and  smallholder  agricultural  expansion,  are  described  in  Table 6,  in  Section  2.1.18.  In  
Table 64 below,  the  internal  threats  to  biodiversity  and  the  associated  mitigation  measures  are  detailed.  
This data is a result of HCV biodiversity assessments at the project site that was undertaken to support the

5.1.3 High Conservation Values (B1.2)  
The SMPP project aims to protect and improve the quality of high conservation values (HCVs) within the 
project area. Through planned biodiversity monitoring and other project activities, the project is identifying 
and  understanding the  characteristics,  distribution,  sensitivity  and  vulnerability  of  these  HCVs.  Project  
efforts to restore and rehabilitate degraded peat swamp forest are a relatively low intensity activity, as these 
efforts do not include activities relating to forest conversion to monoculture plantation, grazing or browsing 
by domestic animals, or abstraction of water.  
However, there are some activities that could be considered higher risk, including:  
• The potential for areas to emerge with more homogenous vegetation due to reforestation programs. 
• The modification of hydrologic connectivity from canal blocking, and potential hunting or trapping 
in the management area by local communities or outside groups.  
Based  on  the  scale  and  intensity  of  activities  of  the  project  and  the  vulnerability  level  of  the  HCVs,  the  
overall  risks  to  HCVs  have  been  are  low.  Specific  information  about  the  presence  of  HCVs  related  to  
biodiversity are described further in Section 5.1.3.5. Despite the low risk level, protective measures have 
been developed to avoid negative impacts, and these mitigation efforts are further elaborated in Section 
5.2.2. 
5.1.3.1 Protected areas 
Although  there  are  no  officially  allocated  protected  areas  within  the  SMPP,  this  area  is  a  key  biological  
corridor for species that transit the Berbak-Sembilang National Park to the northeast, and other adjacent 
forest blocs surrounding the project site. Many endangered and endemic species have been cataloged in 
areas allocated for conservation in nearby concessions in the north and south of the SMPP, which present 
additional  biodiversity  opportunities  if  these  areas  can  also  be  put  under  long-term  restoration  and  
protection programs.  
5.1.3.2 Threatened species  
Overall, of all the species identified ~4% are critically endangered, ~20% are endangered and ~30% are 
vulnerable. This indicates that the SMPP site will have a significant positive impact on biodiversity under 
the project scenario.  
The biodiversity assessments carried out in 2018 identified 46 species of mammal, bird, reptile and fish, 
many of which are classified as RTE species in the IUCN Red List or are Convention on International 
Trade in Endangered Species of Wild Fauna and Flora (CITES) species of concern (Table 65). Of the 15 
present bird species, 14 are listed as CITES Appendix II species, and one was listed as vulnerable under 
on the IUCN Red List (Wallace's hawk-eagle). As expected, a significantly larger proportion of the 
mammals present in the areas are classified as threatened species. Of the ten identified mammal 

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
213 
species, one is classified as critical (Sumatran tiger). Three species are currently listed as endangered 
(Sumatran Surili, agile gibbon, Malay tapir) and three are listed as vulnerable (Smooth-coated otter, sun 
bear, Sambar deer). Similarly of the mammals identified, three are CITES Appendix I species, and four 
species are under Appendix II.  
Given the recent rapid loss of peat swamp forest and the ongoing threats, it is no surprise that the majority 
of the plants and trees identified in the area have been afforded some form of protection by international 
classification bodies. Three of the eleven species are classified as endangered and seven as vulnerable, 
with five species also being listed in CITES Appendix II. Of the seven identified reptile species, both the 
False Gharial and the Spiny turtle are listed as endangered with two more species of turtle and one species 
of snake listed as vulnerable. Six of the seven reptile species are also listed in CITES Appendix II. Only two 
species of fish were identified in the rapid assessment. However, one of them, the Asian arowana, is listed 
as endangered and is a CITES Appendix II species.  
5.1.3.3 Endemic species  
Biodiversity assessments to date have identified three important endemic species in the project area 
namely: the Sumatran tiger, the Sumatran Surili and the Sambar deer, all of which have various levels of 
threat associated with them (Table 65).  
5.1.3.4 Threatened or rare ecosystems 
The SMPP aims to protect and restore 22,922 hectares of a threatened peatland ecosystem. The SMPP 
sits within the Merang-Kepayang peat dome, the largest peat swamp dome in South Sumatra, which covers 
more than 150,000 ha.
36,69
 A number of critical climate and biodiversity assets are linked to the wider peat 
swamp forest zone that surrounds the SMPP: the Sembilang-Berbak National Park to the northeast and 
the Muaro Jambi peat swamp to the north. These areas are especially important for sheltering high levels 
of biodiversity and a multitude of critically endangered and vulnerable species, for example the sun bear 
(Helarctos  malayanus),  the  Malay  tapir  (Tapirus  indicus),  and  the  Sumatran  tiger  (Panthera  tigris  ssp. 
Sumatrae). The remaining ~400 ha of intact closed canopy peat swap forest in the SMPP project area is 
identified as an HCV 3 area and its protection and eventual expansion and enhancement as a corridor to 
surrounding concessions is a key aspect of the project scenario.  
5.1.3.5 HCV areas in SMPP  
Based on the preliminary data collection and knowledge of current conditions, the project has designed an 
appropriate   biodiversity   management   and   monitoring   approach   for   the   long-term   management   of   
biodiversity risks and benefits, including those related to HCV values. 
The project assessed HCV areas for biodiversity based on the High Conservation Value Resource Network 
or HCVRN’s 2014 Global Guideline for Management and Coordination of the Global Secretariat as well as 
the 3
rd
 edition of CCB Standards (Climate, Community, and Biodiversity). The assessment of HCV areas 
for community was based on the HCVRN's Common Guidance for the Identification of HCVs (2013). This 
was  in  combination  with  the  identification  of  HCV’s  sub-categories  according to  the  HCV  Identification  
Guidelines for Indonesia (2009) which provide context specifically for Indonesia. 
The  assessment  team  also  used  the  same  resources  listed  above  as  a  reference  to  develop  the  HCV  
management  and  monitoring  recommendations  and  the  CCB  Standards  (The  Climate,  Community  and  
Biodiversity Alliance) June 21
st
, 2017 edition was also used. 
Comprehensive biodiversity assessments were conducted in the SMPP area by multiple teams throughout 
2018. In April 2018, preliminary surveys were conducted which were followed by a comprehensive review 
in  January  2019.  Additional  monitoring  with  the  use  of  camera-trapping  techniques  began  with  the  
installation of a 10-point camera-trap system, which was installed in May and June of 2018. The data from 
this  study  was  compiled  in  September  2018  when  the  cameras  were  collected.  A  month-long  camera-
trapping  study  in  the  HCV  3  area  was  conducted  in  January  2019  to  better  understand  the  animal  
populations  in  the  SMPP.  There  are  three  HCVs  identified  within  the  SMPP  area  related  to  biodiversity  
(outlined below in Table 65). These HCV areas are further described in Map 39, Map 40, and Map 41.

5.1.3.6 Identifying areas for management to maintain or enhance identified HCVs 
The remaining intact peat swamp forest will require little management to maintain its HCVs. However, in 
order to enhance the existing HCVs across the entire concession and the surrounding degraded land, the 
project  will  ensure  active  management  of  fires  and  rehabilitation  of  the  surrounding  peatland  hydrology.  
Active planting for restoration is also planned around this particular area, to extend a wildlife corridor into 
neighboring forest and throughout the parts of the SMPP.     
5.1.4 Without-project Scenario: Biodiversity (B1.3) 
The SMPP area is largely surrounded by production and industrial plantation forest. In the absence of an 
active restoration project, the area would be at particularly high risk for clearance, drainage and conversion 
to  an  industrial  forest  plantation  to  produce  pulp  and  paper.  The  full  conversion  of  the  area  to  industrial  
Acacia  pulp  and  paper  plantation  under  the  business  as  usual  scenario  would  endanger  biodiversity  
outcomes significantly when compared to the project scenario. 
It  should  be  noted  that  in  some  cases  Acacia  plantations  have  been  shown  to  improve  biodiversity  on  
degraded  lands,
70
  assisting  conservation  by  providing  a  complementary  but  short-term  forest  habitat,  
creating  buffer  effects,  and  increasing  connectivity  where  the  natural  land  cover  has  previously  been  
forest.
71
 However, any potential positive impacts to biodiversity would be limited and short term.  
Net negative impacts would be more likely when compared to the project scenario for several key reasons. 
An  Acacia  plantation  would  require  further  draining  of  the  already  degraded  peat  leading  to  accelerated  
GHG emissions, further homogenization of important aquatic habitats and increased risk of fire. Secondly, 
the short average rotation of Acacia tree crops would mean that plantation forests cannot provide in-situ, 

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
219 
long-term biodiversity benefits. Thirdly, conversion to Acacia would also lead to indirect loss of the remnant 
intact peat swamp forest, which support populations of globally important species. Further degradation of 
the  hydrological  system  would  isolate  animal  populations  and  ensure  that  the  viability  of  many  rare  and  
threatened species is put at higher risk.  
Without  the  project,  an  increase  in  Acacia  plantations  would  result  in  total  habitat  destruction  over  time,  
especially through diminishing connectivity of habitats, massive canalization disrupting soils and hydrology 
and eventual clearance and replanting of the Acacia forest area. Loss of the remaining intact peat swamp 
forest, which is protected under the project scenario, would lead to net negative impacts on biodiversity, 
negating any limited positive impacts derived from the establishment of Acacia plantations. More detailed 
information  on  critically  endangered,  endangered,  and  vulnerable  species  and  their  without-project 
scenarios is provided below in Table 68 in Section 5.5.2. 
5.2 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts  
5.2.1 Expected Biodiversity Changes (B2.1) 
Under the project scenario, the maintenance and improvement in both the quality and extent of threatened 
habitats will have significant positive impacts on the range of biodiversity in the area. Reduced degradation, 
fragmentation  and  reduction  in  the  fire  disturbance  frequency  will  ensure  the  ongoing  survival  of  the  
documented  species  at  the  site  and  the  expansion  of  important  species  into  newly  restored  areas.  
Restoration efforts are also expected to lead to an eventual long-term increase in the carrying capacity for 
a range of species across the project area. As the landscape recovers and is returned to a regenerated 
state,  colonization  of  species  from  outside  the  project  area  into  newly  established  habitats  will  drive  
increased  diversity,  including  species  that  are  currently  using  the  area  only  as  a  corridor  but  that  may  
eventually  become  residents.  Peatland  rehabilitation  will  lead  to  improved  ecosystem  services  for  
biodiversity, affecting an increase in important pollinating and seed dispersing species which will accelerate 
natural regeneration within the project area. Through implementation of the project scenario, the survival 
and eventual recovery of populations of many species of conservation concern is expected. These species 
would otherwise become severely depleted or would be eliminated entirely from the landscape under the 
baseline scenario.  
These  benefits  will  be  achieved  by  reducing  the  threats  outlined  in  Section  2.1.8.  No  negative  effect  on  
biodiversity in the project area is anticipated from any project activities (see Table 66 for further outline of 
changes to biodiversity). 
The expected net benefits for biodiversity will be:  
• The  persistence  of  the  full  range  of  intact,  ecologically  functional  habitats  which  are  minimally  
degraded or undisturbed by humans. 
• The  restoration  and  regeneration  of  drained,  burned  over  and  degraded  peatland  into  functional  
forest. 
• The protection of drained, burned over and degraded peatland from fire risks. 
• The  persistence  and  recovery  of  populations  of  species  of  conservation  concern.  These  
populations  should  fulfil  their  full  range  of  ecological  functions  and  eventually  grow  to  carrying  
capacity. 
• The  augmentation  of  the  current  extant  biodiversity  though  provisioning  of  habitat  though  
restoration activities.  
• Improvement  of  both  terrestrial  and  aquatic  habitat  quality  and  quantity  that  currently  acts  as  a  
biological corridor. 
There  are  two  fundamental  assumptions  dictating  why  the  above  prescribed  biodiversity  benefits  will  be  
achieved  by  avoiding  the  baseline  scenario  of  conversion  to  an  industrial  Acacia  plantation.  The  

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
220 
assumptions  relate  to  each  of  the  two  key  current  habitat  types  occurring  in  the  project  area.  Firstly,  a  
majority  of  the  land  cover  is  already  degraded,  drained  or  previously  burned  peatland.  The  current  
vegetation  structure  across  much  of  the  project  area  consists  of  open  patches  with  scrub  and  early  
successional species (Figure 2). The last incidence of significant fire in the area was 2015 and the process 
of natural regeneration is clearly visible. The project will significantly reduce the risk of fires to ensure that 
the  degraded  areas  can  regenerate  further.  This  natural  regeneration  will  be augmented  through  active  
restoration and replanting with species of both genetic and ecological significance.
 
The  second  assumption  is  that  these  biodiversity  benefits  will  also  be  achieved  by  reducing  the  threats  
outlined in Section 2.1.8. in the remaining area of closed canopy peat swamp forest (~400 ha). Because 
this area will remain undrained in the project scenario it will continue to provide important habitat for both 
flora and fauna as it will benefit from active threat reduction, improved hydrology, and gradual expansion in 
size. It will also provide additional benefits as a seed and seedling source for reforestation efforts in other 
parts of the project area. Seeds and seedlings that disseminate naturally from the remaining peat forest are 
an appropriate ecotype for local restoration efforts and will help to expedite the restoration process. 
The key threats that will be addressed are habitat loss, increased fire resilience and resistance, hunting in 
all  forms,  and  over-harvest  of  non-timber  forest  products  (NTFPs).  Although  hunting,  fishing  and  over-
harvesting of NFTPs are not currently issues in the project area, as the forest recovers, and the biodiversity 
improves the area may become targeted for such activities. These, and other threats listed in Section 2.1.8, 
will  be  addressed  by  the  activities  set  out  in  Section  2.1.11.  The  conceptual  model  described  in  Section  
2.1.11 (Figure 3) further illustrates each of the assumptions underlying the implementation of each of these 
activities.  
Table 66. Changes to biodiversity.   
Biodiversity Element Increased aquatic species abundance and diversity 
Estimated Change 
Consistent  or  increasing  presence  of  fish,  as  well  as  higher  diversity  of  
species reptiles and amphibians. 
Justification of Change 
One of the key project activities is the rewetting of drained peat across the 
project  area  through  the  installation  of  canal  blocks.  This  will  significantly  
increase  the  volume  and  extent  of  ground  water  across  the  project  zone.  
These activities, in combination with riparian replanting activities and natural 
regeneration  will  lead  to  increased  heterogeneity  of  aquatic  habitats.  More  
diverse habitats will, over time, lead to increased diversity and abundance of 
aquatic  species  in  the  SMPP.  There  are  already  some  extant  threatened  
aquatic species in the project area (see   
Table
 65). Blocking of canals will naturally increase the extent and quality of 
different spawning grounds (fish and amphibians) and nesting sites (gharials 
and turtles) this natural diversification of the canal substrate will be further 
augmented by selecting species for replanting around the canal blocks that 
encourage the formation of root systems to bind the compacted peat material 
used for canal block construction. These root and vegetation mats will also 
provide spawning and nesting grounds for the aquatic community.

Estimated Change Consistent or increasing presence and diversity of mammal species. 
Justification of Change 
Without the presence of our project, it is likely that species diversity would 
be  negatively  affected  by  increasing  development  and  land-use  change 
within the wider SMPP area. However, with the project in place, the ability 
for these areas to support biodiversity will be strengthened and these areas 
will be protected. Increased forest cover and the planting of tree species that 
are important to mammal species for some aspect of their ecology such as 

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION

for  food  sources  or  nesting  will  likely  encourage  population  growth  of  key  
mammal species such as gibbons, sun bears, pigs, and Sambar deer.

Justification of Change 
Without the presence of our project, it is likely that species diversity would 
be  negatively  affected  by  increasing  development  and  land-
use  change  
within  the  SMPP  area.  Currently  the  majority  of  the  land  is  significantly  
degraded, although the land is used by mammals of significance for global 
biodiversity  conservation  such  as  the  Sumatran  tiger,  it  is  unlikely  that  the  
project  area  holds  resident  individuals.  Rather,  it  is  more  likely  that  the  
project area is being utilized as a corridor between other hunting grounds or 
adjacent habitats. Under the project scenario, tiger prey species such as pigs 
and  Sambar  deer  are  likely  to  increase  from  their  current  densities  as  the  
rewetting  and  reforesting  progresses.  In  combination  with  the  predicted  
increased  forest  cover,  this  will  likely  result  in  increased  use  of  the  SMPP  
project  area  as  a  corridor  by  tigers  in  the  area.  The  project  area  is  also  
capable of holding resident individuals and it is hoped that continued project 
activities will result in reestablishment of the SMPP as a habitat.  
 
Biodiversity Element Increased invertebrate diversity and abundance  
Estimated Change Consistent or increasing presence and diversity of invertebrate species. 
Justification of Change 
Without the presence of our project, it is likely that species diversity would 
be  negatively  affected  by  increasing  development  and  land-
use  change  
within  the  SMPP  area.  However,  the  project  in  place,  the  ability  for  these  
areas  to  support  biodiversity  will  be  strengthened  and  these  areas  will  be  
protected.  Increasing  heterogeneity  of  landscape  habitats  will  likely  be  
associated with a marked increase in invertebrate diversity and abundance. 
This in turn will likely accelerate the restoration of the project area through 
improved ecosystem service provisioning in the form of pollination services. 
Honeybees  are  already  present  in  the  remnant  peat  swamp  forest  in  the  
SMPP  and  their  population  would  likely  grow  significantly  with  increased  
protection and reforestation of the degraded areas.   
 
5.2.1.1 Expected Negative Impacts 
Even though the threats to biodiversity in the SMPP area are considered low, the project proponents will 
ensure that all possible threats are observed and monitored to ensure they do not negatively impact the 
project. The SMPP is focused on restoring the degraded natural peatland ecosystems and is designed to 
deliver positive impacts within the project area and the wider landscape in the process.  
5.2.2 Mitigation Measures (B2.3) 
The project seeks to avoid any negative offsite impacts on biodiversity and other social aspects by devoting 
significant  efforts  and  resources  to  detecting  the  presence,  understanding  the  characteristics,  and  
identifying the sensitivity of all project activities. As discussed in Section 5.2.1.1, the SMPP is designed to 
bring positive impacts and does not anticipate any negative impacts to biodiversity. 

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
222 
Even though the project does not expect any negative impacts to biodiversity, the plan is to implement the 
following  mitigation  measures  to  increase  species  abundance  and  diversity  within  the  project  area:  
patrolling, fire suppression, canal blocking, and reforestation activities. As such, few additional biodiversity-
specific  activities  are  expected  at  this  stage.  However,  an  active  and  regular  biodiversity  monitoring  
program  will  be  implemented  to  assess  periodic  increases  for  specific  species  and  inform  management  
activities across the SMPP area. 
Key biodiversity management activities include devoting significant resources to detecting, identifying and 
understanding the characteristics, distribution, sensitivity and vulnerability of HCVs. 
As mentioned in the Section 5.2.1 above, any predicted negative impacts are most likely to occur as the 
prescribed project activities successfully lead to increases in biodiversity. It is possible that new animals 
and plants returning to the area may provide incentive for short-term opportunities for exploitation in the 
project  area.  These  activities  could  be  illegal  logging  of  or  poaching.  However,  a  variety  of  mitigation  
measures have been implemented to avoid any potentially negative impacts to the increased biodiversity 
derived from project activities and to protect the SMPP from illegal activities. The community development 
team also conducts regular consultations to maintain appropriate monitoring of the area. 
The potential offsite impacts that were identified during the early planning process now appear to be unlikely 
to  occur.  In  order  to  ensure  the  absence  of  the  offsite  stakeholder  impacts,  the  project  seeks  regular  
consultations with the project’s communities and other relevant stakeholders to ensure that their concerns 
or challenges are integrated into the project’s further planning. Further details on offsite stakeholder impacts 
are discussed in Section 4.3.  
5.2.3 Net Positive Biodiversity Impacts (B2.2, GL1.4) 
Compared with conditions under the without-project land-use scenario, and because the project does not 
anticipate  any  negative  impacts  to  biodiversity,  the  net  biodiversity  outcomes  can  only  be  positive  (as  
mentioned in Section 5.2.2 above). In combination with the predicted increased forest cover, this will likely 
result in increased use of the SMPP project area as a corridor by tigers in the area. The project area is also 
potentially capable of holding resident individuals. 
5.2.4 High Conservation Values Protected (B2.4) 
Three forms of biodiversity-related HCVs were identified above (Section 5.1.3) for the SMPP. They are: 
• Concentrations  of  biological  diversity  including  endemic  species  and  rare,  endangered  or  
threatened species that are significant at the global, regional and national levels. 
• Rare, Threatened, or Endangered ecosystems, habitats or refugia. 
• Basic ecosystem services in critical situations, including protection of water catchment and control 
of fires. 
By protecting part of the Merang-Kepayang peat dome landscape, the SMPP will be directly protecting vital 
wildlife  habitats  and  safeguarding  critical  water  resources  that  are  essential  for  the  maintenance  and  
functioning the peat dome landscape. Reducing fire risk and maintaining connectivity by keeping the area 
intact will increase the site’s value as an important biological corridor and improve forest cover. To this end, 
all  the  high  conservation  values  (at  the  species,  ecosystem  or  functional  levels)  will  be  much  improved  
under the project scenario compared to the baseline for the reasons noted above (Section 5.2.3). 
5.2.5 Species Used (B2.5) 
The project’s restoration activities that include planting or reforestation within the SMPP area shall utilize 
native or naturalized tree/plant species. No non-native species will be used in the project accounting area. 

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
223 
5.2.6 Invasive Species (B2.5) 
Our project will not introduce any invasive species to the SMPP area. All of the species used in this project 
are already found naturally growing in this area. 
5.2.7 Impacts of Non-native Species (B2.6) 
The SMPP will not use any non-native species for any project activities. 
5.2.8 GMO Exclusion (B2.7) 
Our project will not introduce any genetically modified organisms (GMOs) to the SMPP area. 
5.2.9 Inputs Justification (B2.8) 
No inputs such as any fertilizers, chemical pesticides, or biological control agents will be used for the project.  
5.2.10    Waste Products (B2.9) 
The project has infrastructure in place for waste management at the project site. Systems for separation of 
organic and non-organic waste are in place. Organic or food waste is disposed of by using it to feed the 
animals living at the main camp (e.g. dogs, cats, and chickens). Currently the non-organic waste generated 
at the SMPP site is being stored in a self-contained landfill. Toilet facilities for staff use basic latrine-based 
systems. 
Additionally,  as  part  of  our  community  development  initiatives,  the  project  aims  to  incorporate  plastic  
recycling  and  trash  collection  systems  in  the  Muara  Merang  and  Kepayang  villages  as  the  project  
progresses.  
5.3 Offsite Biodiversity Impacts  
5.3.1 Negative Offsite Biodiversity Impacts (B3.1) and Mitigation Measures (B3.2) 
At  this  stage  in  the  project  development,  no  offsite  biodiversity  impacts  are  predicted.  However,  as  the  
project  continues,  and  biodiversity  increasingly  returns  to  the  SMPP  area,  it  is  possible  that  exploitation  
may  occur,  for  example  with  hunting  or  illegal  logging  activities.  The  project  will  continue  to  pay  close  
attention  to  the  activities  occurring  at  the  project  site  and  will  address  potential  threats  to  biodiversity  
through the adaptive management system to prevent negative impacts from occurring. Further details on 
offsite biodiversity impacts are discussed in Section 5.3.  
5.3.2 Net Offsite Biodiversity Benefits (B3.3) 
The  project  area  does  not  have  villages  and  settlements  with  significant  populations  of  people  in  close  
proximity. The project anticipates that there will be significant spillover of species outside of the project area 
with biodiversity benefits extending beyond the boundaries and into the surrounding landscape. Because 
of the low numbers of people living in the area, it can be expected that the project will maintain a net gain 
in biodiversity in the surrounding areas as there are no anticipated interventions required in these areas at 
this time.  
Project teams will systematically monitor hunting and fishing grounds in and around the project area and 
mitigation plans will be developed if at any time there is an indication that the biodiversity outside of the 

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
224 
project area is being over harvested or net positive impacts are not being achieved. See risk table in Section 
2.1.18 for more justification that the risks are very low. 
5.4 Biodiversity Impact Monitoring  
5.4.1 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan (B4.1, B4.2, GL1.4, GL3.4)  
The  SMPP  has  created  a  comprehensive  monitoring  plan  to  address  biodiversity  within  the  project.  The  
monitoring plan is built around standardized and well recognized surveying and monitoring techniques and 
methodologies.  It  contains  all  the  required  components  necessary  to  conduct  successful  monitoring  of  
biodiversity  and  the  impacts  of  SMPP  project  activities  throughout  the  duration  of  the  project.  The  
biodiversity monitoring plan considers the overall requirements of the project, combined with the financial, 
logistical, and staff capacity constraints, as well as the target species and habitats.  
There will be two key stages required before effective monitoring can be put in place within the SMPP area. 
First, all permanent monitoring sites (camera-trap sites and listening posts) and transects (bird surveys and 
patrolling  areas)  must  be  investigated  and  established, including where necessary tracks need to be cut 
and/or  flagged  for  regular  use.  To  minimize  any  negative  impacts  on  the  landscape,  multiple  monitoring  
methods will be used at each site and transects established in between monitoring points. For example, 
travelling between camera-trap sites on either side of the peat swamp forest will utilize the existing trails 
linking gibbon listening posts within the remaining closed canopy peat swamp forest. Similarly, these same 
trails/tracks will have bird monitoring stations established incrementally along their course. Although trails 
need  to  be  clearly  marked  and  will  often  follow  naturally  occurring  contours  or  game  trails  some  
maintenance and initial cutting may be required. However, this will be kept to a minimum not only to limit 
damage to the vegetation, but also to discourage easy access for users from outside the project area. The 
second key component is the training and capacity development of the project staff. All monitoring—barring 
the  annual  bird  surveys—will  be  conducted  by  field  staff  from  the  SMPP.  The  focus  for  biodiversity  
monitoring includes: 
• Gibbon and hornbill monitoring protocols using listening posts; 
• Use of Spatial Monitoring and Reporting Tool (SMART) conservation software as part of patrolling 
protocols; 
• Set up of camera-traps and recording methods; 
• Vegetation surveys at camera trap-sites; 
• Annual site side bird surveys; and 
• Management of all data collected across all methods. 
The existing gibbon population in the SMPP area is located within the remaining intact peat swamp forest. 
To monitor this group there will be one sampling point and three listening posts (Map 37 in Section 5.1.1.1). 
Based  on  assumptions  for  how  far  gibbon  calls  can  travel,  the  sampling  procedures  outlined  here  will  
capture the full extent of the present gibbon population. This gibbon monitoring will be conducted annually. 
All trainings will be conducted by the biodiversity manager on site with full participation and support from 
all project staff. After the establishment of the permanent listening posts, a full survey will be conducted by 
a well-trained monitoring team. 
The  SMART  system  will  be  used  to  monitor  project  activities  at  multiple  levels.  Firstly,  it  will  be  used  to  
monitor inputs and track the performance of forest patrol teams in the field which is vital from a management 
perspective.  Secondly,  SMART  data  will  provide  a  means  of  monitoring  threat  levels  (i.e.  levels  of  
hunting/fishing  and  logging)  through  the  generation  of  catch  per  unit  effort  (CPUE)  indices.  Using  
standardized threat indicators allows for comparisons over space and time and this will help to evaluate the 
effectiveness of enforcement and the impact of other conservation interventions. Thirdly, and of particular 
importance for SMPP in the earlier years of project activities—when threats of illegal activities are expected 
to remain low—the SMART system will be used to collect wildlife observations, and thus contribute to the 

         CCB & VCS PROJECT DESCRIPTION 
                                                                                                CCB Version 3, VCS Version 3  
 
CCB v3.0, VCS v3.3 
225 
wider program of biodiversity monitoring. The use of a system like SMART and the resulting standardized 
reporting of such global indicators will improve management effectiveness. The steps in the use of SMART 
as a tool for adaptive management are as follows: 
• Forest Patrols: Patrol teams collect and record data on where they go and what they see while on 
patrol, such as threats (e.g. poaching signs), patrol results, and wildlife observations. 
• Debriefing: Patrols report their patrol activities and patrol data and routes are checked. 
• Data Entry: Patrol data are stored in a SMART patrol database. 
• Data Analysis and Reports: Data are processed into visually appealing (easy to understand) tables, 
charts,  and  maps  showing  patrol  effort,  coverage,  and  results,  thus  forming  the  basis  for  patrol  
analysis and evaluation. 
• Feedback  and  Patrol  Planning:  Regular  meetings  with  rangers  are  held  to  discuss  patrol  effort  
results and set new patrol targets. 
The project’s biodiversity monitoring and survey design for SMPP utilizes well-recognized and scientifically 
sound  methodologies.  The  methodology  does  not  target  specific  species  (barring  gibbons/hornbills), 
instead,  it  aims  to  capture  the  full  range  of  biodiversity  changes  in  the  SMPP  area  across  a  range  of  
important species. To this end it is a monitoring and survey plan that is both feasible and repeatable.  
Camera-trapping  is  particularly  able  to  provide  a  balanced  view  of  the  mammal  community,  as  camera-
traps take photographs of all animals larger than 500 grams that move in front of the sensor. A standardized 
camera-trap set-up provides information about the distribution or abundance of certain taxa and information 
about wildlife communities, such as species richness. Furthermore, repeated surveys will allow the project 
to  evaluate  population  trends  over  time,  providing  an  important  indicator  on  the  effectiveness  of  
conservation interventions and managements decisions (as outlined in in Section 5.1.1.1). 
For bird monitoring, the project will measure relative abundance, using the point count method. In the SMPP 
area, seven transects will be established for monitoring birds in Section 5.1.1.1). Each transect will consist 
of  ten  permanent  bird  counting  stations  spaced  at  200  m  intervals.  These  seven  transects  have  been  
designed to capture data across the various types of land cover classifications within the project. Each bird 
species will be tracked by recording visual and audio data, along with the environmental variables at each 
station, including time, sunshine, wind and ambient noise. 
Further information about how the project will address biodiversity impacts related to the HCVs 1, 3, and 4, 
can be found in Section 5.1.3.5. All further details and descriptions of the activities that will take place as 
part of the biodiversity monitoring will be outlined in the Biodiversity Monitoring Report.  
5.4.2 Biodiversity Monitoring Plan Dissemination (B4.3) 
The  biodiversity  monitoring  plan  will  be  disseminated  to  project  communities  and  other  relevant  
stakeholders and the public. Summaries of this work will be available in the same manner as other project 
documents, and this dissemination is discussed in Section 2.3.2.   
5.5 Optional Criterion: Exceptional Biodiversity Benefits  
The SMPP aims to achieve exceptional biodiversity benefits and be validated to the Gold Level based on 
the presence of ecologically important IUCN Red Listed species in our project area.

5.5.2 Trigger Species Population Trends (GL3.2, GL3.3) 
Trigger species in the SMPP are defined, in accordance with the CCB standards, as species that cause the 
site to meet Key Biodiversity Area qualifying conditions, which are determined based on IUCN vulnerability 
criteria.
71
 We have identified 29 trigger species present in the SMPP including one apex predator, seven 
other  mammal  species,  four  bird  species,  five  reptile  species,  one  aquatic  species,  and  eleven  plant  
species. Further information about these trigger species is outlined in Table 68 below. Overall, the trigger 
species in the SMPP will be positively affected by the with-project scenario in all cases. At this time, we do 
not have estimations of population numbers of these species within the SMPP.

diversity would be negatively affected by increasing development and

Population Trend at Start of 
Project 
The spiny turtle (Heosemys spinosa), false gharial (Tomistoma 
schlegelii), and Asian arowana (Scleropages formosus) are all listed 
as endangered species. The Asiatic softshell turtle (Amyda 
cartilaginea), Amboina box turtle (Cuora amboinensis), and king cobra 
(Ophiophagus hannah) are listed as vulnerable. All of these reptiles 
and fish are distributed throughout Asia. Populations of false gharial, 
Asian arowana, and king cobra are decreasing, while the population 
trends of the other species listed here are unspecified by the IUCN. 
Without-project Scenario 
Without the presence of our project, it is likely that species diversity 
would be negatively affected by increasing development and land-use 
change within the SMPP area. These species are threatened by 
habitat loss and disturbance, exacerbated by the changes to natural 
aquatic or water-logged areas that Acacia plantation activities 
generate in the baseline scenario. 
With-project Scenario 
One of the key project activities is the rewetting of drained peat 
across the project area through the installation of canal blocks. These 
activities will significantly increase the volume and extent of ground 
water across the project. This, combination with riparian replanting 
activities and natural regeneration will lead to increased heterogeneity 
of aquatic habitats. More diverse habitats will lead to increased 
diversity and abundance of aquatic species over time. Blocking of 
canals will naturally increase the extent and quality of different 
spawning grounds (fish and amphibians) and nesting sites (gharials 
and turtles) this natural diversification of the canal substrate will be 
further augmented by selecting species for replanting around the 
canal blocks that encourage the formation of root mats to bind the 
compacted peat material used for canal construction. These root and 
vegetation mats can also provide spawning and nesting grounds for 
the aquatic community.

costulata, Dyera polyphylla, and Shorea uliginosa 
Population Trend at Start of 
Project

marginata, Shorea dasyphylla, and Shorea teysmanniana are listed 
as endangered and the remaining species above are listed as 
vulnerable. There is insufficient data in the IUCN Red List concerning 
the population trends of these species.

degradation.  
With-project Scenario 
With the project, a diverse selection of plant species would have the 
opportunity to return to a thriving forest, without the continued 
pressure from Acacia plantations that threaten floral biodiversity. 
Disturbance in these forests would be reduced and these vulnerable 
species would be protected, especially in areas of intact forest that 
would be quickly destroyed in the project baseline scenario.
